The Red Page
Verses by K.M.H.S.
INARTICULATE.
There is no greater human agony
Than when a moving mouth is filled, with
words
It cannot voice into reality.
It is as though a group of fluttered birds
Were prisoned in a chasm of the wind
mi >e unseen agencies from flight
1 hrough the unbarriered air, or when men
find
day-desires in fleeting dreams at night.
™ W rne some quick, chaotic thing
Which seeks creative fingers and a form:
But I am all unmanned and weak to bring
A calm hand to the spiritual storm,
Who might have been a beaten rock that
wrought
A sea of passion into foam of thought.
THE QUEST.
/ heard a moaning low and sweet,
I thought it was the winter wind,
I saw the trace of golden feet
That sought a way they could not find:
I walked into a frozen grove
Of lilies torn and broken grass,
And still I saw the steps that strove
-/ o path where silent ages pass.
Where frost was thick before a grot
I found my god with parted lips;
I put my mouth against his throat,
And leaned my hands against his hips.
on f S^ us we staved und neither stirred,
And I forgot my own weak greed
Of solace. Yet I found no word
1 o cheer the weary god in need.
And generations in the mist
Were reaching madly with their hands
In supplication, for they wist
Of whispered fears and shadowy bands.
But he sat listening to their prayers
With tired eyes, unweepingly,
In envy of their silver tears
And days' end in mortality.
BAGATELLE.
I plucked a rush beside a lake,
I waved it till it bent and brake—
The feeble hand must still unmake.
I plucked a dew-fed lily rare,
I gave, it to my love to wear:
It stained the fragrance of her hair.
I chose a maid among the throng,
But youth cannot endure for long,
And age is slow, and love —a song.
I sank beneath a weight of grief
And looked in vain for some relief —
Death holds a burden not so brief.
From “Verses,” by K.M.H.S. (Holywell
Press, Oxford).
J*
When a poet remarks: —
My God, listen to me.
I do not fear you, like the multitude of the
sons of men;
How can I fear you, I who have grasped you
by the great beard,
Hissed, you with a kiss of fire on the lips,
Folded you, the ample, within my arms?
he is sufficiently young not to mind making
himself ridiculous. In drawing this picture
of the Lord as a portly, whiskered old gent,
K.M.H.S., the author of Verses (Holywell
Press, Oxford) is merely Whitmanesquely
youthful. These antics do not hurt. And
when he tells us in confidence that: —
A large inexplicable sorrow laps about my
entrails like a tideless lagoon
this is merely the pain in his metrical Little
Mary that comes of having eaten of the
unripe apples of the Hesperides. K.M.H.S.
m sufficiently young to do these things, and
sufficiently in earnest to make us overlook
them. Poets will be poets; and in the first
poem above quoted the author shows that
he can be a poet if he really wants to be one.
The Woman Sings,” too long to quote, is an
achievement that lacks only form. The
vigor is already there, untrammelled. He
J® too, quite youthfully audacious, as in
Dilemma,” where Swinbourne echoes, not
too shrilly. He is intoxicated with the
alliteratidn of words, and can string them
together without meaning like this:—
Unwilling I have made you fast
To that from which you fain would flee,
And in the portico of night
From, pillar to pillar there flashes a light,
A light that flashes fitfully
And crosses the gloom of the portalled past.
All defects inherent in youth, but K.H.M.S.
evidences merits that more than compensate.
There is an ecstasy in his verse; he can
evoke an image and make fine music. This
Song of Solomon may be regarded as
promise, not performance—but the promise
is assuredly there.

Fifty Caricatures by Max Beerbohm
(Heinemann, 65.), as will be seen from the
reproductions herewith, are sheer delight.
Beerbohm’s only rival in England is Will
Dyson.
It is now possible to obtain the complete
works of Tonnyson, with all the notes by the
author and the memoir by Hallam Tennyson
(Macmillan), in one volume for 10s. 6d.
Nevertheless, the “complete works” of any
poet work out but a few ounces of inspira-
tion to the ton.
Thomas Bird Mosher, of Portland, Maine, is the
only publisher known to us whose catalogue of publi-
cations is itself -literature. His latest announcement
is a charmingly printed pamphlet of books in
belles-lettres issued in choice and limited editions,
interspersed with apt and intriguing quotations in
prose and verse.
Fiction
Reginald Wright Kauffmann, author of
Daughters of Ishmael, is one of the United
States authors who treat sex subjects neither
sensationally nor sentimentally. His work is
always sincere, and his subject matter seri-
ous. In Running Sands (Bell, per Dymock’s
Library) he preaches a sermon on the wrong
done when rich men of 50 marry poor
maidens of 18, no matter how much they
love each other—at first. In this story the
rich husband is a decent fellow, who has
kept his youth by straight living ; but the
wife wants more—she wants youth. As in-
variably happens, at least in books, when a
young wife wants youth, the youth appears.
So far, Kauffmann has kept to the universal;
but here he introduces a needless complica-
tion that narrows the application of his
moral. The decent husband, when he recog-
nises the unconscious wrong he has done his
wife, offers to let her divorce him and marry
the young Austrian officer who loves her.
But that individual is prevented by his re-
ligion from acknowledging the validity of
divorce. Then the husband determines to
Duties and Diversions of this Sweeter,
Simpler Reign. King George inspecting an
Infant School.
commit suicide to leave the way open for the
lovers to marry. It sounds melodramatic.
It isn’t. Nor is the sequel. For the hus-
band is afraid to throw himself over the
convenient cliff; he has too great a physical
fear of death. So we are left with the hus-
band and wife compelled to remain together
—a piece of special pleading, since it is cer-
tain that soon another youth will come along
with no scruples about divorce. The novel
is interesting and un-Americanly frank.
It is doubtful whether Frank Hands,
freat critic as he is, is a novelist. Great
>ays (Lane) is a rattling fine story, one
would say for boys, were it not for its
realism ; but it somehow does not convince
as a piece of life. The scene is set in the
time of Napoleon, and his young hero is a
successful smuggler. There is a splendid
open-airness about this story: the hero has
great adventures, and the savor of the sea is
on every page. . Napoleon and Fox appear,
presentable portraits. And there are pic-
tures of the French Revolution that are
brilliantly etched. Yet Harris never seems
to get much below the surface of the hero’s
personality; and the book is a loose narra-
tive rather than a piece of art. It is the
critic writing, not the artist. . His books
on Shakespeare remain Frank Harris’s finest
achievement.
J*
. Upton Sinclair again—this time his topic
is syphilis. Sylvia (Winston, U.S.A., per
Sydney Book Club) is the story of a charm-
ing American girl of the South, carefully'
educated by her aristocratic family in all
the strategy of capturing and managing men;
but kept ignorant of the more important
facts of sex. She is a “pure” girl, and of
course cannot know, and certainly cannot
discuss, these facts. The story of her tri-
umphs in flirtation is delightfully told,
though even here the fictional worship of
the American girl disgusts the Australian
reader. _ This fetich of the divinity of the
maiden is the most characteristic article of
belief in the modern American popular novel-
ist. One wonders if the American ppvelist
has ever met the American girl. Sylvia
makes a triumphant descent upon Harvard,
and there meets all the snobbery and pluto-
cracy of the modern American university.
Sinclair’s description of the luxury of the
life led by the millionaire students is strange
reading for Australians.
She is loved by a poor youth, whose father
had done time”—it is hinted to shield a
weaker brother. But when she learns that
her fiance had got into a row in a brothel,
she, being a “pure” girl, has no choice but to
break with him. She does not even allow
him to give her the innocent explanation of
his presence in the brothel. There is but
one thing “a proud woman” can do, and that
is to send back his letter unopened. Then
she becomes engaged to “Royalty”—this
brand of person being in America a multi-
millionaire who is a leader of the New
York snobbocracy. “You will perhaps find
yourself impatient with the girl at this stage
of her story,” Sinclair remarks. We agree.
“She could not conceive that it was possible
for a pure-minded girl to talk about such a
subject with any human being, man or
woman.” Man, perhaps, but woman? So
she marries her multi-millionaire, with the
result, discreetly half-hinted, of becoming in-
fected with syphilis and of leaving behind
her a blind child. The moral is independent
of the story. One does not see just why
her husband, and not the man she loved,
should have syphilis, unless Sinclair holds
that all millionaires are affected and all poor
men free.
M.L. a Decade Ago.
Had Democracy in New Zealand been
translated from the French immediately after
it was published, 10 years ago, the translation
would have been interesting and valuable
Now Andre Siegfried’s work (Bell) is but of
historical value; it is merely an acute criti-
cism of M.L. as it was 10 years ago. To-day
the perspective lias shifted ; and in his intro-
duction W. Downie Stewart has made this
plain. Yet ma,ny of Siegfried’s penetrating
criticisms remain as true to day as they were
a decade ago. It still remains true that the
hall-mark of the Maorilander in his disdain-
ful insularity. His country “has always lived
a life apart, obstinately shunning every form
of alliance. Only England, who does not
offend her dignity, enjoys her confidence and
entire devotion.” Many Maorilanders “are
honestly convinced that the attention of the
whole world is concentrated upon them, wait-
ing with curiosity and even with anxiety fro
see what they will say and do next The
conception of the country as a cake, which
if is to one’s interest to divide among as few
mouths as possible, seems to be almost uui-
versal.”
One chapter is headed, “Snobbishness in
M.L. Society.” “The King is more known
to them than the House of Commons, and
far from regarding the latter as more im-
portant—though they think its position a
safe one—they delight in thinking of the Eng-
lish nobility as a sort of tutelary body of
a higher order, whose disappearance should
never be required. In their eyes the halo of
member of Parliament, which was once so
brilliant, pales before that of the first lord
who comes along The Court of England!
Can we form any idea of the mysterious and
enchanting charm which these words have
for the colonials who, at the other end of the
earth, pronounce them with so much emo-
tion? The Court is in their eyes the highest
and most brilliant thing in the whole world,
and they regard the King as an almost
divine being The Maorilanders are demo-
crats without being republicans; pitiless to
the plutocracy, they have not a single word
to say against the nobility England has.
put its mark on this new country, a stubborn
mark that generations will not wipe 0ut....
England, in the eyes of the colonials, is
endowed with a halo of romance.”
But it is well to remember that all this
was written before the visit of George.
The “School Reader”
“Jay Kay,” who is prepared to furnish
awful examples, if necessary, dissects the
average “School Reader” :
The average State school is still essentially what it
was when the generation now paying (or dodging)
SIGNOE GABRIELE D'ANNUNZIO.
accounts rendered went thither unassumingly to be
harangued and spanked. Its prevalent unit is an
overcrowded room, wherein one average male or
female does battle with the recalcitrant instincts of
from 40 to 70 of the men of to-morrow. Such being
the case, the Book is still its basis. And what book ?
The “School Reader.” This commences with a pre-
face, a verbose beatitude, on the virtues of “instil-
ling” and “inculcating” whatsoever happens to fol-
low. The competent pedagogue, subject to an annual
overhaul, takes blanky good care that the same is
instilled. Good or bad, appropriate or repugnant,
he sees that this book becomes as much a part of
his class’s metal being as the Bible of Bunyan’s, or
Milton of John Bright’s. The best he does is with
extracts, often ill-selected and always ill- or un-
adapted, from tested and proven standard authors.
His worst work is based on materials supplied by
God-knows-whom. These latter anonymities are, as
a rule, leagued heart and soul with Gradgrind and
Podsnap. They cheerfully perpetrate every fault of
style short of downright ungrammaticalitv. They
are dull, prolix, laboriously didactic, circumlocutory,
wooden, all at once, or by turns. They armor their
simplest commonplaces against the child’s mind in
a garb of pretentious Latinisms.
They spread themselves on topics at once trivial
and unattractive to the mind of incipient youth. To
sum-up, they lack both the universally indispensable
gift of imagination and the peculiarly necessary one
of sympathy.
Their cardinal offence, in the eye of accredited
penmen, is this: They spoil his future market. All
this compelling of the child to assimilate unsuitable
matter renders books (which are half life) repugnant
to him when adult. All this drilling in idiom, either
bad or banal, destroys his discernment of genuine
authorship. Result: Released from his awe of the
rod and “detention.” he reverts to his own crude
and inveterate tastes for hectic rubbish. He applies
absolutely no criteria, intuitive or deliberate, to style
and, cheerful rebel, exclaims to blanky with veri-
similitude ! Moreover, now that “the pitchers” are
all the vogue, he engrosses himself in their obvious
and convincing unrealities. This problem of “kid”
literature is really that of a thinking or a vapid

democracy. Also, until it be solved, H. Caine, M.
Corelli and C. Garvice will continue in office as pur-
veyors of tripe to the public in general.
in conclusion, if any reader thinks this illuatured
advocate has exceeded his brief, let him assay ene
sample from the lode thus derided. The new Pacific
Reader, Book V (M.L.), is as bad as the worst, but
no worse than the majority.

“Chicago” hands us the vegetables:—
Coming from the country that you have a
habit of irreverently referring to as The Land
of Fried Nigger or The Land of Wooden
Ham, I have been interested in the recent
transplanting of the Rymbel. The effort
by Carolyn Wells, reprinted by you, Dec. 11,
from The Centuby, looked good to me, and
I doubted if any competitor would equal it;
but, now that 1 have read the 10 Rymbels
published Feb. 12, 1 take off my hat to Aus-
tralia. Carolyn had better head towards the
big timber; for The Bueletin’s poets have
delivered the goods, sure! They have taken
hold of this “rambling, Rymbel rot” (as one
of them neatly puts it) that was without
definite form and void, and in just over a
month they have beaten it info recognisable
shape, and furnished it with both rime and
reason. In short, they have made Carolyn’s
parent effort look like three cents. Is it
possible that writers of such snappy verse
run wild and unappreciated in Australia?
“Beltana’s” effort appealed to me and the
Rymbel headed “Strike!” and signed
“Moana,” tickled me to pieces. The latter
embalms such a cute idea, runs so smoothly,
and is so neat and obviously complete, that
I would have handed it the prize. Why does
a man who can write so well and so
easily in that form stay in Australia, when
in New York I am certain he could make over
$6OOO a year writing topical verse about
Roosevelt? If “Moana” is a girl (and he
might be), he’d probably make nearer $lO,OOO
a year in my country if he cultivated a
decent sob or two and specialised in house-
maid verse— & la Flla Wheeler Wilcox! If
you want to promote a commercial venture
that will rake in the dollars in great style,
start exporting some of your topical poets
to the States. I know it’s difficult to com-
mercialise artists and poets, they’re such a
boozy, irresponsible crew [Not in this con-
tinent sure!] ; but someone might try it. We
can do with them in my country*, and, judg-
ing by those sample Rymbels, there would be
money in it.
Simpson Newland’s “Romance of the
Australian bush,” Paving the Wap, gains
value with the years, since it presents a
faithful picture of early pioneering life and
of the customs of the aboriginals uncontami-
nated by contact with civilisation. Hence
the new fine illustrated edition (Gay and
Hancock; 10s. 6d.), with 50 photographs
and a well-spaced type, is welcome.
AND ONLY JUST THIRTEEN!
The grave misgivings of the Nineteenth
Century, and the wicked amusement of the
Eighteenth, in watching the progress (or
whatever it is) of the Twentieth.

[After Oliver P. Winchester (. 1810-
80). Amer. manufacturer. | A breech-
loading- rifle with a tubular magazine
under the barrel holding five or more cart-
ridges inserted one by one from the rear, the
bolt being worked back and forth by a lever be-
neath. It ** * was introduced about 1866, and is in
world-wide use especially as a sporting arm.— (Webster’s

WEBSTER’S SAYS
■ WINCHESTER
Repeating Rifles Are “In World-Wide Use”
This statement is authentic. It could also have said with
equal truth that the reason why Winchester rifles are “in
world-wide use” is because they have been found by sports-
men everywhere to be superior in construction, absolutely
dependable, accurate and strong in shooting and moderate in
cost. Another reason is that Winchester rifles are made in
all desirable calibers, from .22 to .50, and in various styles and
models, suited to individual tastes, and to all kinds of shooting.
Cartridges are also “in world-wide use. In
Winchester and all other rifles and in revolvers, they
can be depended on to give the best results. Made for all arms.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Song of the Lost Dredgers.
London, Friday.
The missing dredge Posidonia is uninsurable.
— Cable.
It wasn’t a ship and it wasn’t a house —it
was something in between,
But you sent us out at a sailor’s wage to
navigate your machine;
You sent us out and we didn’t come back to
ask for our hard-earned pay;
But we’ll wallow along with our fires all out
to damn you on Judgment Day.
To save the money that wasn’t yours, you
bought what the foreigners built,
And ordered it over God’s outer sea to har-
vest your harbor silt;
You hired us sailors to take the risk, for a
sailorman’s life is cheap ;
And now your foreign-built tub is gone and
we’re all drowned in a heap.
Whose is the guilt for this ocean sin—this
national treachery ?
Whose faith is so weak that others must
drown with a harbor machine at sea?
Is the work of your brains and hands so
bad —is your sight so cruelly short
That you cannot see it is all sheer waste —a
species of madman’s sport?
The money is gone and the dredge is gone,
and you’ve taken our sailor lives ;
And our skill and strength were all that we
had, so what of our kids and wives?
We might have sailed in a proper ship if
you’d built your dredge at home,
Then you would have your money still and
we would be free to roam.
It wasn’t a ship and it wasn’t a house —it
was something in between ;
But we took the risk for your cursed pay
and our grave is deep and green.
Yes, you searched to prove that you cared
for us and our mourners far away—-
But we’ll come with our skeletons hung
with weed to damn you on Judgment
Day !
N.S.W.
E. O’F.
The Tale of Mullah El Keb.
I know not just how it started —only this:
that the fend was red —•
Aye, as red as the silken turban around Ab-
dullah’s head. >
And learned I this: In the old-time, in a
land remotely far,
Abdullah was foully murdered where the
brown vendettas are.
Ilis blood had clotted the verdure —and his
death was no great harm:
But counted his vileness little against his
■kinsmen’s sharm.
And learned I this as I listened one night to
a camel bell:
That loud was the cry for vengeance where
the lean brown tribesmen dwell.
For theirs was the fierce, hot crimson that
matched the stain on the grass,
And Kandra’s the black, black vengeance
that echoed through range and pass.
They hearkened to Kandra’s curses, so ever
the story ran,
As he made his vows on the hillside of
Takht-i-Suliman.
And down through the Pass of Ivhaiber,
where her and acacia grew,
Mullah el Iveb went sleepless because of the
fear he knew.
Through the hills that Allah had builded,
tramping in sleepless spans,
He travelled and travailed in terror ahead of
the caravans.
For little it counts if spices, or ivory, or
myrrh
Beckon the murderer backward when sliarm's
black hatreds stir.
And out on the plains of Indus no portion of
rest had he;
For vengeance lurks in the shadows, leaving
no murderer be.
Out on the Rim of the Sunset Mullah el Keb
found work,
Steering old Hoogli’s camels back o’ beyond
o’ Bourke.
A lean brown Sphinx of the silence, he
uttered no waking word
Of that which reddened the verdure and
fierce black hatreds stirr’d.
From Bourke to the Border Stations, and out
through the Empty Lands,
Daily he steered the hunchies over and over
the sands;
Smoking at night on the sand-drift, staring
a stony stare
Out at the Silent Spaces—and finding no
comfort there.
But hate is shorn of its terrors as time goes
rolling away,
And the vengeance of Ivandra likened to that
of a far-gone day;
For the sea is not as the Ganges—and who
would challenge the sea
For the sake of a long-dead tribesman? —and
a worthless scoundrel he!
Three years went by. Came a season when
Mullah set out alone
With goods for the Border Stations —a third
of the team his own.
Let tales of the drovers’ telling be accepted
or be spurned,
But I know, with the Sunset People, that
Mullah never returned.
* * * *
And back through the Pass of Ivhaiber, where
her and acacia grow,
Because of Kandra’s story the mode of his
death they know.
In the hills that Allah has builded a strange,
strange tale they tell;
And great is the glory of Ivandra —who had
honored his vengeance well.
NEVER NEVER LAND.
R. J. CASSIDY.

lly scjiiliug direct to Tins LiiJi, listin' Office Twenty-
Six Shillings and Sixpence Sterling you can have
The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.
Chei/ucs, Drafts, etc., which should he crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to The
Manager.
llow to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some
care as to methods of address.
Husiness letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertiscnicnls, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the HdUor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin," is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read, and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “ Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submilled, whether in the form
of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short
Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Australian sub-
jects, and not exceeding two columns in length, or,
say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Tetters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, those
sending MS. to distinctly understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of
MS.

THURSDAY, MARCH 26, 1914.
BIGJOHN’S SCHEME.
A good many years ago Australia went to a
heap of trouble to put in its Constitution a
clause giving itself power to take over the
State debts as they stood at Creation —the
political Creation of this country, that is.
But nothing came of the proviso, and nobody
except George Turner, who loved a vast,
dreary financial problem as if it was a
brother, seriously tried to make anything
come of it. Later on another heap of trouble
was taken to clothe the Australian Govern-
ment with some more authority. It was
permitted to take over, not only the State
debts as they stood on Creation day, but the
State debts as they stood at any time —all
the liabilities, curses and appurtenances—
and to do so on practically any sort of basis
that seemed good in its eyes. Again, nothing
happened. For a long time nothing was
even attempted, and as George Turner
had attempted something the second effort
looked really worse than the first. The
trouble seems to be that the debt question
isn’t a party matter. It is merely a large
and vital Australian question. No consolida-
tion scheme that can be evolved will make
the Government crowd enthusiastic and
unanimous, or make the Opposition wholly
wild and abusive and miserable. It is an
item of business that is even likely to split
parties by making a certain number of
Laborites and Liberals get together on one
side, while a second mixed crowd combines
on the other side. It doesn’t supply an in-
spiring war-whoop for the electors, for
though the benefit to the country would be
enormous if the debts were taken over and
consolidated on wise principles, the benefits
wouldn’t be visible to-day or even to-morrow.
They would accrue to Posterity, and among
the cheap, ordinary, hand-to-mouth politi-
cians there is a rooted objection to doing any-
thing for Posterity. The objection couldn’t
be stronger if Posterity had committed
bigamy.
* * # *
It is to the credit of Bigjoiin Forrest’s
patriotism that he now proposes to attack
the State debt problem. Also it is to the
credit of his astuteness, for the Cook Gov-
ernment, having very little in the way of a
party behind it, is almost forced, in self-
defence, and for the sake of advertisement,
to depart a little from ordinary party jobbery
and try its hand at some national problem.
It may be driven to endeavor a big thing for
want of sufficient voting power to potter at
small things. There are many fascinating
possibilities in the case. If the whole Com-
monwealth was put in the melting pot accord-
ing to a comprehensive programme of Unifica-
tion and Reconstruction, the position would
be simple. The present State Legislatures
would shut Up for ever and a day. There
would be six sacked Ministries and six job-
less State Governors, with their wives, pri-
vate secretaries and aides-de-camp. All the
liabilities and assets and bad debts and bad
specs and frauds and aspersions would
become Federal property, and there would
be no argument as to whether the local com-
munity with the larger debt gained a little
on the deal, or the one with the smaller debt
lost a little. The country would start afresh
as a crowd of small States, each with a
cheap little one-house (not one-horse) Legis-
lature, a cheap local sub-Governor, very
limited borrowing powers or perhaps none at
all, no control of railways, and a humble and
chastened spirit. That would be the best
and simplest way of doing things. But in
the absence of any party which has courage
enough to go in for whole hog reconstruction
there are other possibilities.
* * * *
The scheme which Big John is supposed to
favor is that the Commonwealth shall reach
out a hesitating hand, seize each State loan
as it falls due (and something is falling due
almost every day), attend to the renewal, and
take possession thereafter. Then it will charge
that State with interest and sinking fund on
the debt taken over, and deduct the amount
from its subsidy of 255. per inhabitant per
annum. It is a slow, timid process, and
not nearly so satisfactory as an immediate
assumption of the whole responsibility. The
Commonwealth will be always taking over
State debts and never getting possession of
the whole of them, and the Federal sinking
fund will only apply to a part of the debt —
never to all of it. On the other hand the
scheme —if it really exists —has this present
merit—that no State’s liability in the way
of interest and sinking fund willexceed the
amount of its Federal subsidy for a very long
time to come, therefore the bill can be collected
quite simnly and easily by a process of stop-
page and deduction, and there will be no
immediate need to devise anything in the way
of differential taxation. The device is half-
hearted, but with all drawbacks it will be a
vast deal better than nothing. Forrest,
according to report, proposes to take the line
of least resistance, but then none of his pre-
decessors took anything at all, and none
save Turner tried to take anything.
* * * *
The States will, presumably, go on bor-
rowing, and will pile up new debts while the
Commonwealth is paying off the old ones. This
will be bad, but it might be worse. Every
time a loan falls due and is taken over, the
Commonwealth will present the thriftless
State with another annual bill for staking
fund. It may be an absurdly futile bill —
rumor sneaks of a wretchedly small sinking
fund contribution of ss. per £lOO per annum
—-but it will have a steadying effect. It
will be a constant reminder that the borrow-
ing curse comes home to roost sooner or later.
And there is always the hope that if a begin-
ning is once made in the good work of debt
consolidation and repayment, greater things
may follow. A future Treasurer may gird
up his loins, and take over all the burdens
in a heap, and make the sinking fund £1 per-
cent. all round, and bid a crowd of protest-
ing State Treasurers to flee into Hades.
Anything may happen provided something
begins to happen. And Bigjohn should be
the man to make the beginning. His own
State has a repayment proviso attached to
every debt —in which respect it is unique.
It owns as much accumulated sinking fund
as the other five States combined. There-
fore Bigjoiin can get to work without laying
any extra burden on his own people, or losing
votes in his own country. Also he can take
the opportunity to preach a sermon on the
comparative soundness of Westralian finance,
which used to be his own finance, and on that
topic he can cock-a-doodle at great length.
A MATTER OF CLASS.
Dear Bulletin, —The Red Page (B.
19/3/T4) calls C. E. Jacomb’s recent howl
at all things Australian “the finest exposure
yet vouchsafed us of the soul of the Eng-
lishman.” The phrase is too sweeping. As
a matter of fact, Jacomb’s effort exposes
the soul of one sort of Englishman only—
the middle-class Englishman. I think it is
Hamebton who lays it down That it is im-
possible to make an accurate psychological
study of any Englishman without taking his
class into consideration. The Englishman
of the poorer classes usually approves of
Australia. The Labor Leagues estimate
that about SO per cent, of immigrants be-
come enthusiastic Nationalists and demo-
crats within a year of arriving in the Com-
monwealth. The ones that kick are either
uuemployables, who are unhappy every-
where, or folks whose luck has gone hope-
lessly wrong and got them down. The
Englishman of the aristocratic class —the
typq who in London belongs to the. Marl-
borough or White’s, or one of the big poli-
tical or University clubs, who joins the
local clubs immediately he arrives in Aus-
tralia, and who is accepted everywhere as
a desirable character—finds Australia alto-
gether satisfactory. So alluring does the
Englishman of this class find the Australian
girl (the same girl whom the Jacomb
variety of Englishman considers unspeak-
able) that he marries her. It is
not a question of money. Many Aus-
tralian peeresses—the Countesses of. Lind-
sey, Uarnley and so on —brought no dowries
to their husbands. The simple explanation
is that the class into which they married
views Australia and Australians quite dif-
ferently from the class to which Jacomb
belongs.
’ * * * *
Properly to comprehend the grinding
hatred of Australia (or Canada, or South
Africa, or Maoriland, or even Kerguelen, or
the Falkland Islands, or perhaps St. Helena)
which inspires a Jacomb it is l neces-
sary to hark back to- the very begin-
ning of things. The infuriated young
man is a Harrovian. lie dedicates his
book to Harrovians. lie would not for the
world have you forget the fact that lie was
at Harrow —the reason being that it is the
most important event of his life, from a
social standpoint. To any Australian who
is incapable of appreciating the tremendous
factor that schools like Eton and Harrow
represent in the eyes of the bourgeois Eng-
lish youth, I would recommend I'ekcy
White’s “Colonel Davoron” —a novel, by the
way, which contains a portrait of a very
charming Australian girl. The idea that
the boys at White’s “potty little private
school” have of the tremendous social im-
portance of going to Harrow is
astonishing to one who has no personal
acquaintance with middle-class England.
Harrovians, Etonians and so on divide up
roughly into two sections: (1) those who
are there as a matter of course; who could
not conceive the thought of going to any
other school than Harrow or Eton or Win-
chester, and who consequently never dream
of making a song about. it; and (2) those
who but for the grace of a rich uncle or a
lucky parental spec, would have got a sound
commercial training at some such place as
the Margate Grammar School, or the Bir-
mingham Academy; for Young Gents. It is
this type of youth who makes a nuisance of
himself in after years by getting up Old
Boys’ dinners; forming Old Boys’ clubs;
dedicating bookstand things to “The Old
School”; and forcing his acquaintance on
total strangers who happen to have been at
the Old School. In his opinion—and such
is the staying-power of stupidity that the
delusion remains often till middle-age or
thereabouts —the men of his race consist (a)
of “the right sort of peonle,” meaning those
who have been to a nublic school of stand-
ing; (ft) “outsiders,” meaning persons of
other than royal or noble, birth, who have
been what is called “privately educated.”
*'•'« * *
So long as the bourgeois public-school boy
stays in England his social withers remain
more or less unwrung. There are so many
in a like position to himself. But some-
times he moves to Australia —or to Canada
or S’Africa. And then, if he is inarticulate,
he sits down and wonders dumbly why
God —England’s God, and hence a Deity
who is essentially a Gentleman —allows such
places to pollute the earth ; while, if he has
the fevered pen of a Jacomb, or of the
Hebrew tripper who was responsible for
“The Awful Australian,” he gets rid of
some of his anguish by writing a book.
I can picture Jacomb’s career from the
time his ship left England for Australia as
though I had been at his side. I have
known so many of him. During the voyage
he mentioned, not less than three times
daily on an average, that he had been to
Harrow. Ciiubchill was a contemporary
of his. He referred to Chubciiill tolerantly
as “Winston,” but deplored his radical ten-
dencies. While admitting a certain surface
cleverness on the part of his old school-
fellow, he would note sadly that Winston
was “rather a bounder,” “hardly the sort
of chap one would ask to meet one’s people,”
and so forth. This attitude impressed con-
siderably the flat-faced maiden from Dundee
(N. 8.) who was going to Shanghai to
marry the young fellow in Reutek’s
office there; also the traveller in Manchester
goods who had had all his schooling at the
S. Hampstead Preparatory College. On the
otitter hand it revolted the rest of his table-
coinpanions, more particularly cheery old
squatter Blogges, who had had no school-
ing anywhere, and his friend General Hogge,
who, while regarding Ciiubchill as a
menace to the Empire, considered it well
over the odds that lie should be criticised
by a suburban cad of the Jacomb stamp.
Jacomb’s first introduction to the “Aus-
tralian accent” cast him into a species of
frenzy. This class of Englishman attaches
an inordinate importance to the genteel in
diction. The same remark allies to the
same class of English girl, who, whether
she lives the leisured life of Clapham, or
works for her living in Mabsiiall and
Snellgbove’s or Habbod’s, provides an
equally faithful imitation at all times of
the stage articulation of Miss Gebtie
Millab. That is whv it is becoming fashion-
able to talk carelessly—just as when a wave
of culture smites England it becomes,
presently, the fashion to be illiterate.
Fashion is, to a large extent, a tireless
struggle to be unlike Jacomb. I dined at
the Traveller’s last time I was in Paris.
Amongst others at the table was Jacomb—
or. rather, one of his sort. In a little while
Fbank Otteb came Otteb” of
many diverting Pink ’Un sketches and one
of the best-known men about London and
Paris. It has been his whim from his
schooldays to affect a sort of costermonger
accent. Jacomb was profoundly shocked.
It was plain that he grieved for us who
were on familiar terms with this unspeak-
able member of the proletariat. Not till he
had elicited by careful cross-examination
that Otteb had been at a public school and
Oxford, as a preliminary to entering a cav-
alry regiment, did Jacomb revert to normal.
The ruling accent of Australia differs from
the ruling accent of (say) Jay’s hat shop,
just as the ruling accents of New York
State, Kentucky, Yorkshire, Inverness and
Meath differ from each other. Where the
stupid, or untravelled, or hopelessly genteel
English person errs is in the idea that there
is any significance worth mentioning in such
matters.
* * * *
To revert to the Australian career of
Jacomb. He intimated before arriving in
Victoria that he was a member of the
Junior Constitutional, and wouldn’t mind
joining the Melbourne Club. The Melbourne
Clubites aboard said, privily, that they
would see the d—d remittancer boiled first,
or words to that effect. He told everyone
he met in Melbourne that he was an Old
Harrovian. No one was impressed. Some
of the most unsatisfactory shearers’ cooks
in the Comomnwealth are old Harrovians.
One of the ways in which Australia and
the larger spaces generally differ from
seminaries such as that of which the afore-
said Percy White has written so well, lies
in their blank indifference to where a man
has been educated. Jacomb’s circle of
friends remained severely limited. By the
time he reached Mildura and embarked
in the raisin industry he had almost ceased
to talk about Harrow. It was becoming
apparent to him that Australians Simply
Didn’t Understand. Life in a land where
no one was interested in Harrow, where no
eye took on a reverential gleam when men-
tion was made of the Dear Old Hill, grew
intolerable to the wretched Jacomb. Bereft
of people who were capable of being im-
pressed _by the fact that he was an Old
Harrovian he was merely Jacomb—in a
word, nothing. He sold the raisin-ranch and
published a book. What else could he do
and still remain Jacomb? —Yours, etc.,
“10-2-4.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Absurd Rents of Sydney.
A few day?? ago Premier Holman, of N.S.W.,
stated, with a certain aspect of misery in his
eye, that rents are much higher in Sydney
than in Melbourne or Brisbane, thus malt-
ing life none too good for the toiling classes;
and he enumerated the efforts he had made
to improve things. These consisted of the
Fair llents Bill for the compulsory lowering
of the landlord’s charges; the amalgamation
of the Savings Banks, so as to
create an institution which would lend
money at a low rate to people who
Want to own the houses they live
in ; and the building of Daceyville." The
first two schemes were wrecked in the Upper
House, and the third was so limited and cur-
tailed that -it didn’t do nearly so much as
was intended. To these three failures he
ascribed the high rents of Sydney; the impli-
cation being that Holman had the brains to
find out the cause of the evil, and -the will
and capacity to put things right, only mal-
evolent and uninformed creatures got in the
road. And yet, by his own showing, Mel-
bourne, which has neither Holman nor Hol-
man’s devices, is, by comparison with Syd-
ney, a low-rent city, and the worker there
doesn’t totter under the burden which his
brother carries in the Ma State metropolis.
It is interesting to speculate on the reasons.
Melbourne had, on last census day, a popula-
tion of 588,971, while Sydney had 029,503.
Since then Sydney has greatly increased its
lead, and it is now supposed to have 60,000
or 70,000 more people than the Victorian
capital. All these people are clamoring for
house room. Sydney takes great pride in
its status as the biggest place in Australasia,
and in its extra special congestion, and in
its consequent struggle for somewhere to lay
its head. These dignities, like most others,
have to be paid for. Melbourne puts its
smaller population on 163,480 acres." Syd-
ney crowds its much larger population on a
mere 95,259 acres, and that pressure causes
the intensity of the battle for four rooms and
a kitchen and a backyard. One reason why
Sydney is compressed so badly, and has such
anxiety for a dingy terrace house, is the
lame and blind and imbecile character of its
means of communication. The Sydneyite
can’t live so far out as the Melbourneite or
the Brisbaneite or the Adelaideite. Mel-
bourne has a railway running right through
the city. Sydney hasn’t. Melbourne has
bridges and railways and tramways across
its waterway. Sydney has been talking of
building bridges across the harbor and mak-
ing tunnels under it for half a century, but
it still depends on the Private Enterprise
ferry boat. Melbourne has railway com-
munication with every suburb of importance,
also its trains run down to the piers at Port
Melbourne and Williamstown. A very large
section of Sydney depends entirely on a tram
service, and, by reason of the narrowness and
overcrowding of the streets, the up-to-date
electric cars of Sydney often make worse
time than the antiquated cable contraptions
of Melbourne. (In like manner Adelaide
and Brisbane, though they have a popula-
tion less than one-third that of Sydney, cover
very much more ground. They also have
fairly good city and suburban railway sys-
tems, and so the city workers can live far
out, and scoff a good deal at the grasping
landlord of Paddington or the hungry house-
agent at Darlinghurst.) Sydney tries hard
to be the one monster city of a State of
310,000 square miles, while. Melbourne, bad
as it is, only aims at being the solitary and
bloated centre of a State of 88,000 square
miles. To sum up the case, Sydney’s, draw-
backs are its congestion, its pride of size, its
Millions Club, its lanes, its almost complete
lack of a suburban railway system, the want
of good means of communication over the
harbor, and the complete absence of a rail-
way through the city—a point in which it is
behind even Perth. It doesn’t require a
verbose lawyer politician, who will adver-
tise himself by shaking a disordered axe at
the city landlord, so much as it wants an
engineer politician who will alter the condi-
tions which have made the city landlord boss
of the situation, and a capable financial
politician who will find the necessary mone\
on terms that are not wholly ruinous. Bnt
he must be a real financier —not a boundless
and shoreless public ass or a person whose
chief asset is his untravelled wilderness of
effrontery.


The Imported Tyrant.
Australia seems to have acquired a wholly
undesirable settler in a certain G. 11. Ken-
dall —described as “organiser of the Amal-
gamated Society of Engineers of South
Africa” —for whose extradition the Botha
Government, it is said, proposes to apply.
Mr. Kendall got as far as Melbourne in
his flight, and there, instead of getting to
work as a plain engineer, he started to
address meetings and deafen this country
with ex parte statements about his wrongs
in another country a very long distance
away. Some of his statements —on which
this paper’s opinion of Kendall’s undesira-
bility is based —were as follows :
In comparing Industrial conditions in South
Africa and Australia, although they had it “stuffed
down their necks’’ in South Africa that Australia
was “God's own country” for the worker, he found
the conditions and workmen here very much the same
as they were in any other place, lie had actually
seen men and women running to their work —a thing
he had always been strongly opposed to.
And again :—•
I can see that every possible means must be used
when an industrial strike is on. It is really war,
and you have to treat it as a war. During the strike
a striker, suffering from phthisis, who had only
three or four months to live, and who is dead now,
came to us and said that if we would look after
his wife and children he would engage himself as a
“scab” in Keinfontein, and take in sufficient cyanide
to poison the whole of the “scabs” inside and him-
self as well. (Loud applause.) 1 thought that a
little too much. I am not averse from murdering a
“scab,” but to send a man in cold blood to poison
probably 400 or 500 men is going a LITTLE bit too
far.
I hope the Government of this country will not
lend themselves to the Government of South Africa
by extraditing: me over there. The least this Govern-
ment can do is to allow me to lead a peaceful exist-
ence. I have conformed to the laws since I have
been in Australia. I do not know whether I shall
always conform to them, because, as a rule, I am
a rebel at heart, and in all probability I shall be a
rebel in this country, as I have been on the other
side.
As _ regards people running to their work,
which Kendall is strongly opposed to —
well, if you have cut things too fine you must
run or be unpunctual, and unpunctuality
isn’t a virtue, nor is it big enough to be a
vice. It is a slovenly, untidy, crumpled
habit, like a dirty shirt front, or forgetting
to Clean your teeth, or not washing, or leav-
ing old egg-stains on your whiskers. Un-
punctuality has spoiled many a good busi-
ness, and many a promising revolution, and
many a meeting of Amalgamated Engineers.
In the glorious days of 1715 the Scotch
rebels failed to take Edinburgh Castle be-
cause Charles Forbes, their belated
engineer, walked to his job with the
scaling ladders instead of running. If
the employer is unpunctual with the
wages there is annoyance, and if the doctor
doesn’t run when he hears that Kendall’s
wife is on the edge of death there is a row,
and if. the Lord didn’t make the universe
keep time there would be a most unholy
smash. As for Kendall’s statement that
he expects to be a rebel in Australia, there
are some things that should be impressed
upon him. This country has, at great trouble
and labor, secured absolute manhood and
womanhood suffrage in its Federal Parlia-
ment. Some of the State Legislatures are
not yet quite up to the mark, still they can
be upset any day, and their powers trans-
ferred to the Federal Parliament, by a vote
of the Federal electors, who comprise prac-
tically the whole adult male and female popu-
lation —lunatics and people in gaol are neces-
sarily excepted. In Australia the commu-
nity rules. It may not rule to the satisfac-
tion of an organiser of Amalgamated Engi-
neers who has fled from South Africa, but
it rules. And if a bumptious roarer
with . all the elements of a tyrant
in his disposition—whether he is a
Visconti of Milan, or a Pope or Pompey
of Rome, or a Robespierre of France, or a
Cleon of Athens, or a bolting organiser
from South Africa —proposes to be a rebel
against complete popular Government, it
is best to get in early and sit on that fellow
hard. Either the people must govern, or
the control of things must pass to an
oligarchy like the French Committee of
Public Safety—a. crowd of patriots, or
rebels, or organisers, or strike-leaders or
makers of civil war. Kendall, regarded as
a man who expects to disapprove, in his very
high importance, of the laws which the Aus-
tralian people make for the Australian
people, and to take matters out of the hands
of the public and start to put them right,
has all the ear-marks of a budding oligarch.
He is calculated to please the Tory gang,
which is always glad when it can preach
about the obvious inability of “the masses”
for peaceful self-government, but he is equally
calculated to be disastrous to the com-
munity. He ought to get a job.
♦ —-
The Importance of Providing for Noodle
and Puffy and Edmund Sparkler.
The recent Charles Dickens wrote bit-
terly at times re the foolish politics of aris-
tocratic England. In “Little Dorrit” he
grew satirical over the urgent need to “pro-
vide for” Edmund Sparkler, which futile
character ultimately got a job at the Cir-
cumlocution Office; and in “Bleak House”
he became sarcastic over the way the country
was supposed to be “lost” unless a Coodle
or Doodle Government could dig up a
position for Noodle, or a Buffy Ministry
discover a suitable salaried niche for Puffy.
The present N.S.W. Administration appears
to know more than Coodle or Buffy. Any-
how, it has again got ex-Minister Nielsen
off its mind by sending him forth, with a
salary of £4 per day. to represent N.S.W. at
the Panama Exhibition. Twice before
Nielsen went away to study something hazy
and indefinite, or do something misty and
non-committal, or represent something of
little visible importance, at £4 per day, and
for part of the time he enjoyed his Parlia-
mentary salary in addition. So it begins to
look as if the desire to provide for Nielsen
was even more intense that the necessity to
find paid occupation for Edmund Sparkler,
also honorable employment for Noodle and
Puffy. And between the date when this
frequently-remunerated man came back from
the U.S. for the second time at £4 a day,
and left for the third time to go to the same
place at the same wage, he had other public
employment. It isn’t easy to see why the
State of N.S.W. wants separate representa-
tion in the U.S. on account of the Panama
Exhibition. That republic is an unprofit-
able customer which buys from the Ma
State a comparative trifle worth about
£1,800,000 per annum, while it contrives to
sell to N.S.W. goods worth over £5,000,000.
As a commercial acquaintance it isn’t in
the same street with France or Germany or
Belgium. It really seems that, if any man
was wanted, one man might have served for
the whole of Australia, and done the work,
if there is any work, on less exorbitant
terms. The permanence and frequency of
ex-Minister Nielsen grow wearisome, especi-
ally at a time when the Treasury is in such
desperate straits that it has been considered
necessary to put on the biggest and most
sudden mass of new taxation on record. In
prosperous times it may be allowable to
“provide for” Edmund Sparkler and find
places for Noodle and Puffy. But in des-
perately lean years these gentlemen ought
to go out and get work —at least, some
people are unreasonable enough to say so.
Certain individuals are even so cantankerous
as to complain of another recent appoint-
ment made by the Holman Government —
that of a Sydney Morning Herald man as
“Government Press Commissioner.” A
Government Press Commissioner is a new
idea. The name suggests a sort of advance
agent, and the Cabinet isn’t a dwarf or a
fat girl or a picture show that it should
require a highly-paid advance agent.
The “Telegraph’s” Imported Editor.
The Sydney Telegraph announced officially
the name of its new editor last week, lie
is one D. D. Brauam, of London, lie has
never visited Austialia, and on his record,
journalistic and otherwise, seems likely to
know as little about the community whose
opinions he is expected to form as any liv-
ing writer in a big English-speaking com-
munity. On the other nand, he has had
charge of the Foreign Department of the
London Times, and, according to one of his
references or testimonials which the Tele-
graph had the banal humorlessness to print
in full, as if it was a case of a night-watchman
applying for a job, he is “a very nice fellow
who will get on well with the people he has
to work with.” The Telegraph is pre-
eminently the Australian paper which con-
siders that no good can come out of Naza-
reth, or, rather, Australia. It voices the
opinions of the foreign importer, the re-
mittance man, and the Yankee drummer
who is here to earn or steal a bit and get
away with the plunder as. soon as possible.
In no way could this lamentable tendency
have been demonstrated better than in its
action over the editorship business. With a
self-abasement that is more than Asiatic it
says to N. S. Wales in effect: “We have
been training up journalists for generations.
But at the end of it all we haven’t been
able to produce one that Is as well fitted to
run our paper as a young man who has
spent his life 12,000 miles away, and who
knows no more about the details of the
political and social problems of the country
than a Siberian native does of sugar
planting.” Mr. Braiiam will arrive here
possessed of nothing but the most super-
ficial acquaintance with the constitutional
and industrial difficulties which form the
subject-matter of about half the average big
daily’s editorial comment. His ignorance
will be complete in regard to the
Australian’s attitude towards religion,
sport, the drama, the King, the King’s
representatives—Federal and State —and
a hundred other vital questions. The
attitude of the average local man in these
matters is as widely separated from that of
the average man with whom the new Tele-
graph editor has been accustomed to con-
sort as Brisbane is from Perth. Sometimes
the distinction is obvious —most of Aus-
tralia’s amateur sportsmen, for example,
would be indubitable professionals at Ox-
ford. Sometimes it is a difference which
can’t be stated in words, but which the
native feels in his bones —the local senti-
ment in regard to the Established Church,
the Throne and various other institutions
are cases in point. But the new importation
will have to do more than assimilate local
knowledge and the fine,shades of local public
feeling. He will be compelled, unless he is
the most unusual Times staff man on re-
cord, to make recantation of the doctrines
of a lifetime. Assume that he is a Unionist,
as one fairly may, in view of the facts that
he has been associated with the Times for
16 years, and is acclaimed by one of his
editors as “imbued with strong Imperial
feeling.” Some of his first duties, in getting
to work in King-street, will be to laud the
Parliament Act; heap encomiums on the
Redmondites; denounce the Lords, and
clamor for the abolition of the hereditary
principle. He will probably be an Anti-
Socialist —not the bogus variety which we
see in the ranks of Liberalism, but the real,
consistent, Bruce Smith thing. Also he is
likely to be. if only theoretically, a church-
man. As Telegraph editor he will be
forced to uphold an education system which
is Socialistic to the core: and which is as
much anathema to British Anglicans as it
is to Australian Catholics. He will be
mixed up with Socialistic railways and
tramways. He will run against public
land-ownership. Hp will even meet
with Daceyville. Without laboring the
point it is enough to say that he
will have to spend many humiliating months
swallowing old views, and more months ab-
sorbing fresh ones, if he isn’t to abandon his
job and go back aemin. Meanwhile the men
who might have filled the position will be
doing most of the work and getting neither
credit n r financial recompense for it. It
is a miserable business from start to finish.
What hope has Australia of being regarded
as a grown-up nation in foreign parts, when
its very newspapers see fit to import the
men whose business it is to tell the country
how to conduct itself?
What Do They Get Out of It?
Once more Victoria’s State coal mine at
Wonthaggi has lain idle, and once more the
responsible party was the pestiferous small
boy. Some unduly tired wheelers had recently
to be sacked. The other wheelers in that shift
thereupon “struck.” The management re-
quested some miners to come out and do
wheeling for their mates ; they refused, and
threw the pit idle. Others supported
them, and then the general manager coldly
announced that he was sick of such idiocy
and shut down the whole outfit until such
time as a sound, satisfactory, cast-iron agree-
ment should be arrived at whereby
he might know that men and boys
who contracted with the State to
do certain work for certain wages would
stick to their bargain. After that the rail-
way department despatched its staff of em-
ployees engaged about the mine to Mel-
bourne, having nothing for them to do at
Wonthaggi; with the result that thousands
of pounds in \yeekly wages were being sacri-
ficed ; business in the township was non-
existent ; and things generally were
only middling. The same boys stopped
operations at Wonthaggi only a month
ago. On that occasion the executive
of the local branch of the Miners’
Union considered the matter pnd decided
that the nippers ought to be fined 10s.
apiece for their ill-regulated haste and folly,
but the miners themselves declined to back
up their executive, which promptly resigned.
The newly-elected leaders have evidently
found the blessed wheelers possessed of an
insane notion that they boss the situation —
that they, in fact, are the State mine and
the State as well, and have struck trouble
right at the start. Probably it is necessary
for a mine to have “wheelers”—until some-
one devises a mechanical means of shifting
newly-hewn coal —and as obstreperous lads
can neither be smacked like boys nor argued
with like men it looks as though a deadlock
had arrived. There is no sound reason, how-
ever, why a few of the miners could not
assist their mates to do their work
by taking the wheelers’ places till the
latter's spasm of cantankerousness is over.
A wheeler’s pay is not to be sneezed at, even
by an adult; and by shifting themselves they
could probably earn as much as their coal-
hewing mates. Anyhow, a gang could co-
operate and whack the proceeds. Mean-
time, it would be a soothing circumstance if
some of these 900 miners who were losing
some thousands per week would explain
exactly what they expected to gain by the
whole foolishness. The public, which owns
the mine, is losing heavily through the re-
peated spells of idleness, and a continuance
thereof means the absolute shutting down of
the undertaking. That again means a
term of unemployment for the miners and
probable subsequent employment by Private
Enterprise, with worse wages and no better
treatment. These same miners hacked
up the wheelers in their contumacious con-
duct ; they flouted their own officers in
their honest attempt to control the lads —
now what do they get out of it all?
The Labor* factions in Maoriland continue
to embrace each other with all the fervor of
loaded bulldogs. Thus J. T. Paul, as repre-
senting the Trades Hall crowd, on “Pro-
fessor” Mills, of Milwaukee (George
Washington’s country), organiser for the
Federation of Labor :
It is a strange coincidence that every Labor
organisation in M.L. up to and including the United
Labor Party, which employed Mr. Mills as or-
gnniscr, found its balance on the wrong side of the
ledger when balancing time came:—
Mills, as representing the other side, on
Paul :
For two years Mills was employed by certain
Labor organisations in M.L. with which Mr. Paul was
himself associated. During that time. .. .Mr. Mills
earned in writing or speaking, and entirely apart
from the funds of the party, all he ever received,
and contributed from his own funds over £2OO in
the payment of accounts authorised, approved and
audited by those organisations; and repayment
promised. The payment has not' been made. The
records do not contain any item of personal contri-
bution to these national funds by Mr. Paul. The same
cannot be said of his friend McLaren. McLaren
did promise a shilling a week, and he kept up the
payment regularly and faithfully for three weeks in
succession.
When the mail left McLaren was earn-
estly sharpening his fangs on the kerbstone.
That curious old superstition, about the
necessity, on high moral grounds, to ear-
mark any revenue derived from the totalisa-
tor for charitable purposes, has been heard of
again in N.S.W. And this same unconscious
snuffle persits in other places. There is no
special ear-marking for charity of the money
derived from drink, tobacco or opium, or from
fines levied on drunks, thieves, customs-
defrauders, wife-beaters, child-beaters, sellers
of indecent photographs, assault men, bad
languagers, two-uppers, Rahabs, or em-
ployers who cheat poor people out of their
fair remuneration. The land-tax levied on
land sharks isn’t ear-marked, nor that
charged on the land whereon brothels are
built. Bookmakers’ license fees and the
license fees from gambling clubs aren’t set
aside for hospital purposes. The clothes
which the hanged man doesn’t want any
longer are never sold for the special benefit
of some good cause. Yet whenever the
honest, harmless old tote is mentioned,
shocked persons say the thing is impossible
unless the tainted profits are set aside for
charity. Now which combination of book-
makers finds the money to organise and pay
for all this shock? And what kind of a
beastly thing is charity if it can take the
money which is too unclean to be used for
any other purpose —even for building a gaol?
Dear Bulletin, —Major W. “Karri” Davis,
an Australian by birth, but a South African
as regards experience, recently told the Mel-
bourne Argus that the only way to develop
the Northern Territory was by the importa-
tion of Chinamen. It is rather a pity the
Major didn’t go more deeply into this inter-
esting subject. The Northern Territory
has Chinamen. It is the one Chinese pro-
vince of the Commonwealth. Taking Aus-
tralia and Tasmania as a whole, there are
1175 colored folk in every 100,000 inhabi-
tants, and the proportions range from 412
in Tasmania, 453 in S.A., and 580 in Vic-
toria, to 57,160 in the Territory. The
Territory doesn’t own 100,000 inhabitants,
of course, but these are the proportions. It
is the one place where the Asiatic has been
encouraged, where the Chow has been pam-
pered, and where the blessed colored toiler
forms the majority of the population. It
is Australia’s heroic experiment along what
Major “Karri” Davis and Bruce Smith
and other folk of their kind allege is the
road to salvation. The result so far hasn’t
amounted to much; in fact, the Territory
hasn’t boomed, nor has it salvationed. The
capital city is a filthy agglomeration of tin
huts —the one capital which the Australian
is ashamed to show to a visitor—and that
capital is especially the Chinese centre. The
interior hasn’t been opened up. The rich
mines, after much Chinese gold-digging, are
a failure. Hardly one shaft has more than
scratched the surface. Agriculture is about
as dead as Pharaoh—but then it never was
alive like Pharaoh. The Chow has had
his chance for something like 40 years, and
the only decent things in the Territory are
those which the handful of white men have
created. Now a lot of people fancy that
the hope of the country lies in building a
railway to connect it with the nearest white
cities, and pouring in white settlers, and
thus putting an end to the long and un-
profitable Chow preponderance.—Yours, etc.,
Henry Horsecollab.

Behold the State of New South Wales!
It sitteth upon the Great Sea,
It hath five mighty ports,
Yea, five great cities by the waves.
Every one of them is at the end of a rail-
way,
Every one of them is full of ships,
The merchantmen go to, and fro,
Wheat and wool are brought to the mart,
Yea, beef and mutton and tallow and cheese
are therein;
For the country groweth mightily,
And hath five great upon the sea.
These things have I seen in a vision of the
night,'
But when I am awake Sydney sitteth alone,
She sitteth alone by the sea,
Save for the companionship of Newcastle,
And Newcastle mattereth not a dump,
For she is but as the mouth of a coalscuttle
And as a pond where men may drown cats.
Yet are the five great ports promised,
The Labor party hath sworn that they shall
be,
The leaders have written it upon paper,
They have told it to all them that vote,
They have said it a second time and a third,
Yea, they have said it on 1476 occasions,
And some of the occasions were many years
ago.
What aileth them that they do nothing?
Is not the time ripe?
Yea, the time is very ripe!
Sydney is as one oyster shell filled with 14
oysters,
As one hen sitting upon 217 eggs,
As a curate that hath on 19 collars,
As a fool person that tryeth to bathe in a
quart bottle,
As an acre of ground that crowdeth itself
into a garbage tin,
As 12 aldermen that shelter from the rain
under a clothes line.
Yea, the time is very ripe!
Call ye to them that sit in high places,
That draw screws for that they say
And thereafter quickly forget to do.
Say ye, the railway goeth abroad,
It gathereth spoil of all the State,
It draggeth all things to Sydney;
There are ports to the north empty,
There are ports to the south silent,
The railway goeth past them to Sydney,
The State groweth sick with waiting!
Get a move on!
Act!
Do something!
The Time IS RIPE!
Lo, a voice from the high places,
It speaketh softly in the ear !
“Yea, the time is a little ripe.
It groweth riper at an early date,
It will be quite ripe before long,
The season is backward, yet we are sure
that it will really ripen.”
What talk is this from the high places?
Lo, three years ago they said the time was
ripe,
Six years ago they said it was too ripe,
Nine years ago they said it had ripened
long ago!
Speak unto them that they do something.
Speak unto them that they do it NOW!
Lo, again, the voice from the high places !
“Be patient and wait a little longer;
We have begun to consider the matter;
We have written a letter about it;
We mentioned it only last week ;
We shall go thoroughly into the question ;
In a little time we shall speak about it quite
loudly,
Yea, in a time, or two times, or any old
time
Your ears shall hear
A PRONOUNCEMENT” (I think).
Behold the State of New South Wales!
Sydney sitteth upon the middle of it,
Yea, she sitteth alone by the sea;
There are ports to the north empty,
There are ports to the south silent,
And the Labor party hath sworn a great
oath
That the time is ripe when they should be
opened.
Yet will something be done (I think) !
The matter is not forgotten (I think) !
The subject shall have consideration (I
think) !
If we wait long enough,
And are quite patient.
And don’t make a fuss.
And behave very nicely,
And keep as silent as possible,
Somebody will say something about it (I
think)
Just before next election (I thinkl !

Increase Your Height by from two to three
inches within three months.
No Medicines. Ordinary Habits Retained.
No appliances or drugs of any kind are used,
and it is impossible for my system to cause
overstrain or to injure the health in any
way. On the contrary, my students are in-
variably astonished at the tremendous improve-

duces.
All the time required to be devoted is 10

of life.

Exodus from the Territory.
That is the common heading nowadays, and
the exodus from the hot, damp scrap at the
northern end of the Territory is bad. There
is no news of any exodus from the great,
cool tableland further south, but the table-
land is a different matter. The hot, damp
scrap is the Territory in the opinion of
people who .simply look in at Port Darwin
as the steamer passes, and perspire for a
day. and see the wreckage and misery that
have been made of one of Australia’s grand-
est ports by Australia’s one big experiment
in the way of Chinese settlement, and say
feebly that what is wanted is colored immi-
gration, and consume two awful Chow
drinks, and burn one horrible Mongolian
cigar, and then leave. This mere fragment of
the land, where the great heats and the great
heathen and the passing tourist are, consti-
tutes the bane of the big Federal property.
It has no business to pose as the whole place
—as the entire waggon and the complete
yellow dog underneath —but political con-
siderations keep it in that situation. The
bargain made when S.A. handed the Terri-
tory over to the Commonwealth was that the
line from Adelaide and Port Augusta to the
North should be finished. That would have
opened up the cool, lofty and fairly-well-
watered inland country, and made Middle
Australia a province suitable for white set-
tlement. Also it would have given it the
shortest possible line of communication with
the nearest big city, and made an end of the
ancient lonesomeness. But there was, un-
happily, a lion in the way. Certain noisy
folk in N.S.W. and Queensland desired that
the treaty should be broken, and that the
line should connect first of all with Sydney
and Brisbane instead of Adelaide; and
Andrew Fisher, as a Queenslander, felt
the same way. Incidentally Joseph Cook,
as a New South Waler, feels similarly. On
both sides there is a beastly resemblance.
It was impossible to be honest without losing
votes, in the two big eastern States, or to
be dishonest without losing votes in the
middle State. So the railway, on which the
safety of the northern half of Australia de-
pends, was hung un indefinitely. Nobody was
either honest or dishonest —only somnolent.
But fisher showed his energy by sending a
horde of expensive officials round by the
interminable sea journey, to moulder at the
disconnected Chinese town of Darwin, and
to infest it for no purpose. And Cook
showed his economy by recalling a lot of
these officials. It might have been a mutual
arrangement. The first movement was the
Awakening of the North. The second was
the Exodus from the North. Both were
good advertising stunts. Neither touched
A i .i? U m tloa breaking up the separateness
of the lerritory. Neither so much as looked
at the question of settlement—in fact, the
Darwin Land Board recently reported that
no anangements had yet been made to ren-
der any land available for settlement.
Neither got within 100 miles of the
an area about as large as
K.k.W., and with a much better cli-
mate than much of N.S.W.—where pro-
giess should be started on a large
scale. It was a mere pottering back and
toward of expensive atoms —one politician
sending them to loaf at the other end of
Nowhere, in order to show his desire to push
the public interests, while the other one
recalled them from loafing in the same place,
in order to show his desire to save the
public money. There was no real move-
ment, but there was a great removal of
atoms.
The Financial Peer.
A pleasant slired of hypocrisy was the
debate by the British House of Peers upon
the alleged purchase of titles in Great Brit-
ain. Everybody knows that titles are pur-
chased, and many of the peers who took
part in the debate had doubtless bought
their dignities with cash down, or their
fathers had bought them and passed them
along. There is a regular tariff, and the
sale of honors is the chief support of the
party funds—the funds of both parties. The
game, however, has certain rules, and isn’t
exactly like a nigger insurrection in Hayti.
A rich man whose character looks as if it
would last his life-time sends in his cheque
to help his particular political crowd through
its troubles at next election, and he is care-
fully remembered when the chance arrives.
He must have some semblance of respecta-
bility and permanence, else titles would fall
into disrepute and become unsaleable.
Thus Dooley’s cheque was regretfully re-
turned, it being discovered that Hooley was
a howler who would get into jail some day
—which he did. Barney Barnato’s con-
tribution would probably have been declined
with great sorrow, but that sudden garish
millionaire went melancholy mad amid his
gold and his palaces and his fine raiment
(he was said to own the most wonderful
kimona in Christendom), and committed,
suicide before he had time to be an appli-
cant at the door of the aristocracy. Jabez
Balfour, a most dignified member of the
House of Commons, was just about to be
fixed up with a peerage when the bottom
fell out of his banks and building societies,
whereupon lie fled to Argentina and con-
cealed himself in a brewery. As a general
thing, the King’s Birthday or New Year’s
“honors” are half-and-half. About 50 per
cent. of. the recipients are soldiers, states-
men, scientists and other deserving cases.
The rest are rich, elderly, pious traders and
dealers, who pay to be elevated to a place
among the Uppish Classes. When a
Kitchener goes to the House of Lords he
can easily carry a pair of grocers on his
back, and when a Roberts or a Morley
gets a step up he can drag one or two plain
money-grubbers along with him. The game
has been played so long and so strenuously
by Conservatives and Liberals alike that it
is a very tough and hardy peer who would
deliberately fall over the sleeping dog. To
start an inquiry in this year ’34 of the new-
est century that ever was is almost akin to
asking half the British peerage how its
grandfather escaped penal servitude.
Good Old London.
“Exile” writes:—
Three years ago I was out in London, marooned
for my shortcomings in the most unfriendly and
inhospitable city on earth. Loandon is a nightmare,
a mausoleum, a sepulchre. To an Australian it is
the most detestable town up in Europe. The con-
trasts between the extreme wealth and the awful
poverty which stalk the streets side by side depress
and discourage the sun-loving, heat-worsliipping Aus-
tralian who calls no man overlord, and is a vassal to
none. The railway men had struck for better
wages than 30s. to 255. a week, and all transport was
held up.
Directly the strike was declared I went and lived
in the East End, that diabolical ghetto which so
effectively disgraces London and its rulers and people.
The strike was on at midday on Friday, and finished
on Monday afternoon. By Saturday, at five o’clock,
there was not a scrap or scrag on the meat barrows
in Mile End Road, and not an ounce of bread for
sale. You have no conception of what East End
meat is like. It is not catsmeat even ;it is worse than
that. I verily believe a Cantonese pong would refuse
to eat it, and he loves offal. News had passed round
that there was a strike and no food was being
delivered on the Saturday. From the cellars and
the garrets there was a fierce rush of gaunt, ugly,
unkempt humans, anxious to secure the one meal
a day which most of them put up with. I have
never seen a more dreadful-looking mob. The rush
for the spoil was remarkable and soon over. The
alleged bread and meat vanished, and the starving
had to be content to buy dried herrings, bloaters,
sprats and all the other abominable fish on which
the poorer classes subsist in England. Soon there was
not a bit of salted or smoked fish left in Commercial-
road or the Minories. On Sunday there was nothing
to eat on the barrows in Petticoat-lane. If it
wasn’t for Ldpdon’s 22,000 police that Sunday would
have been the date of a riot.
And some day that mob of sans-culottes will get
an extra meal and find a leader like Lansbury or
Larkin. They’ll march west*, and, when thev reach
the Mansion House, the strekm will split in two—-
one half going up Holborn and the other up Fleet-
street and the Strand. And guillotines will be plenti-
ful in Trafalgar Square.
Here is the summation. Fancy any rich nation
permitting itself to remain in the position of having
only three weeks’ food on hand for the consumption
of 45 million people! No race in this or any pre-
vious civilisation has ever stood in such imminent
danger of entire surrender as the people of the United
Kingdom to-day. They can’t shoot, or ride, or feed
themselves, which causes me to thank God every
morning that my father left Scotland and came here:
He did it for two reasons—the climate was bad and
the tucker worse!
“G.V.W.”: The Brown froth from the
Colonial Sugar Refining Co.’s plantations
in Fiji is finding its way into Maoriland in
an increasing quantity. The law to keep it
off and the administration of the law are
about as effective as a wire fence in keeping
off mosquitoes. In Auckland and Welling-
ton the bulk of the fruit barrows are
attached to a silent discolored alien. Other
blotches are; dribbling over the landscape un-
loading coal along the railways and supply-
ing cheap, humble, deep-thinking farm labor,
and great is the joy of Cocky thereat. In
places they are becoming a fixture like the
Public Debt, and are intermingling with the
Maoris. A weird and eminently worthless
biped will result. Two dark strangers ap-
peared to answer a summons for debt the
other day up Waikato way. The evidence
showed that one had 344 acres of land and
the other owned £9OO worth —area not
stated. If Maoriland is to be kept clean
Premier Massey will have to sit down with
great earnestness and weave a much finer
net than the existing alien obstruction.

Along at the Palace “The Land of Nod”
Is calling the Sydneyite to plod
From his books, or his pen, or his brickman’s
hod,
From the billiard cue, or the fishing rod,
To watch the skin-clad girls that wad-
Dle up on the plank where the tango’s trod
(Some of them gasping like landed cod).
Bilanderson’s asking the church Sy nod
Around in its gaiters one night to tod-
Dle. Each will be handed a hefty clod
To shy at the show if it’s over the odd-
S ; Bill is as calm as a bean in a pod.
“I’ll bet you,” he drawls, “that the said
Sy nod
Won’t cloud the view with a single sod —
Have you seen the legs in ‘The Land of
Nod’ ”?
