Next Wednesday the White City, Syd-
ney’s new pleasure park, will (barring
damp, floods, rain, deluge, drizzle, Scotch
mist, wet feet, rheumatism, cataract, mud,
slush, frogs, tadpoles, cloud-burst, sudden
alluvial deposit, great downpour, whales,
alligators, sharks, tidal wave, eels and sim-
ilar happenings) hold its first carnival.
Special prizes will be given (weather per-
mitting) for the most attractive costumes
(unless ruined by unforeseen meteorological
disturbance) in advertised sections (if
the advertised sections aren’t washed away.)
Wearers of fancy or historical outfits will
be admitted to all side-shows free (after
paying one shilling entrance fee and pro-
vided they are not drowned), and, from
The Bulletin’s experience of the number
and expanse of the said side-shows
competitors are urged to get into
training for the ordeal. A boat will
be kept handy to pick up those who lose their
footing. A good historical character to
assume would be that of Clarence, who had
a vision of the sea-bottom, and was after-
wards capsized into a butt of wine by
parties who are still at large—worse luck !
“ALLAH’S ORCHARD," AT THE
ADELPHI , SYDNEY.
Waring Sahib ( Cyril Mackay) and the good old
blind man (Charlie lay lor) arrange a wedding.
This week’s Sydney picture specialties:—
Crystal Palace: “A Lady of Quality,” concern-
ing a lady who masqueraded as a man. Also proving
conclusively that Wowsers are not a modern inven-
tion.
Lyric : “The Witness of the Will,” another missing
document yarn. In melodrama the property goes to
whoever can steal the will. If there is no will it
goes to the most adjacent villain, who keeps it until
denounced by the comic man. Then the matter is
submitted to the policeman at the corner, who de-
clares that the hero was unjustly convicted, gives
him a free pardon, hands him back the estates, sen-
tences the villain to unbounded imprisonment, and
resumes the even tenor of his job.
Colonial: “A Million in Jewels.” Act one, a
princess with jewels worth one million; a villain
with his eyes on the jewels, and a Yankee young man.
Act two, one princess, a Yank and a corpse.
Empress : “Whimsical Threads of Destiny,” being
further evidence that it is quite time all villains
threw in the towel.
West's Glaciarium and Olympia: “The Curse of
Greed,” 3000 ft. long, with a sensation every six
inches.
Spencer's Lyceum: “The Three Musketeers”—
Athos, Aramis, Porthos and D’Artagnan. And how
Dumas made these four into three is quite unknown.
Incidentally, D’Artagnan was a Gascon. The French
kingdom was saved in the time of Louis “the Spider”
by the dirtiness of the Gascons. Their Burgundian
allies refused to share the same camp with them
when they were jointly besieging Paris because the
Gascons smelled too badly, and this caused a rift in
the lute of the revolt. Spencer’s people ought to
dwell on this point.

Tivoli now, also Adelaide, and there will
soon be Tivolis at Gosford, Birdsville, Thar-
gomindah, Bruthen, Gawler and Mullum-
bimby—provides a turn that has the one
great advantage of being out of the ordinary
run of vaudeville turns. Still The Bulle-
tin isn’t going to encourage anyone else to
risk it for a living. The lady has, up to
now, perfect control over the five beasts, and
though this pleasant state of affairs may
not continue, everyone who likes a combina-
tion of daring and entertainment knows
where to book seats the present. We are
here to-day and eaten to-morrow, but suffi-
cient for the moment is the animal thereof.

playgoers as one of the best Iagos ever seen

“The Sacrifice.” Graus Bros., balancers,

retire shortly and become joint Treasurers
to the N.S.W. Government. It requires a
balancer badly. Tfiese people are also called
equilibrists, and anybody with even a shred
of equilibrium about him is wanted in Syd-
ney politics.

MAKE HIM FEEL YOUNG.
“I had pains in the chest and stomach, which the
doctor said were due to indigestion,” writes Mr. E.
Healey, saddler, Raurimu, N.Z. “As his medicine
did me no good, I was advised to try Chamberlain’s
Tablets, and did so. I got immediate relief, and
am now never without a bottle in my room. I am
a very old man, but Chamberlain’s Tablets make me
feel well and young.”*

On with the dance! The big Imperial
Theatre, at the shank of William-street,
Sydney, used successively as a skating rink
and picture palace, has now been briefed
as a fashionable dancing salon. Large
and expensive alterations are being made for
the luxury of the Most Exclusive and Fas-
tidious. The mere bloque and bloquess have
as much chance of gaining entrance to the
“Salon de Luxe” as to the Jewish Wharf-
lumpers’ Union.
The main props of the National Amphi-
theatre’s claim to a fair share of the swag
set aside in Sydney for entertainment are
Clement May (who supplies representations
of Dickens’s characters so true to line that
the author would find his inanimate char-
acterisations outdone), Josephine Gassman
(and her joyous piccaninnies), Weston’s Liv-
ing Models (good job those girls didn’t live
in George ll.’s 90-mile-a-minute reign) and
the Livermore Animated Doll. There are
other good turns, but those mentioned ought
to attract custom without assistance.
The Star Pictures, run by Coffey and
Powell in the little house that used to be
Adelaide Tivoli, lately put up a commendable
novelty in a story locally filmed, from city to
beach. It is a farcical thing, but may
lead to better; anyway, the praiseworthy
idea is there. Wondergraph Cos. has brought
back “From Manger to Cross” as a Lenten
attraction. The Pav. has “Satan’s Castle,”
a moral drama with some devilish good
effects in it, and West’s are working out
per film the truly Yankee—with a side-
light on Melbourne police—“ Third Degree.”
The Tivoli continues to give successful
Tango-teas, the alleged riskiness of the
underwear-parades being tempered by a lady-
like discretion. Clemons and Dean do their
quaint song-and-dance in the evening bill,
and Talleur Andrews has brought his fine
baritone with him. On the other hand, an
alleged revue staged by Mclntosh on the
Adelaide Oval has dragged along rather list-
lessly, probably because it was not really a
revue at all, but merely a ragtime medley.
The King’s brings in the Carpos brothers to
help a good bill that needed strengthening
on the athletic side. Leonard and Willard
supply some positively “fierce” American
slang, which gives a fresh effect to the ordi-
nary cross-talk of their land. The Dandies,
at the Exhibition Gardens, have exchanged
one or two artists with Branscombe’s other
companies elsewhere.

“Jean Sibi” writes :
Melbourne Hibernians are maddened at Hal Gye's
Bulletin’s caricature of Allen Doone and a few* of
his merry men —or, to put it more accurately, a
few Hibernian supporters of Doone allege that every
Hibernian in Victoria is seething with rage at the
effort of Gye. The astonishing Doone, grasping the
advt. with both hands and all his teeth, as usual,
said that the “cartoon” was “a malicious and unjust
libel, besides being grossly offensive. No one,” con-
tinued Allen, “is better able to appreciate a joke
than an Irishman, but when it comes to holding up
his country and his people to downright ridicule, it
is time he showed his displeasure.” It is in moments
like these that one realises that Doone really is
Irish —a matter in regard to which the writer has
often had doubts in view of his deplorable handling
of so-called Irish characters on the stage. It is true
that an Irishman can appreciate a joke—so long as
it is not up against himself. When it is, he is the
dullest customer on record. A deputation of Irishmen
once visited Norman Lindsay and begged him to cease
drawing people of Hibernian extraction. No English,
Scotch, Welsh, Patagonian, German, Yankee, French
or other national deputation has ever visited Lindsay
for the purpose of uttering cries of reproach. An
Irish crowd went fanti the other day because the
priest in “General John Regan” was put on the
stage. This priest is a harmless—in fact, estimable
—character. There is nothing either evil or absurd
about him. Had he been presented in the form of
an outrageous burlesque—a figure akin, say, to the
Anglican person in “The Private Secretary”-—the
Irish audience might have been justified of its fury.
But it doesn’t need a genuine insult to arouse the
national ire of Hibernia. All excuses for a row are
thankfully received —more especially by slick Yankee
actors, of allegedly Irish extraction, whose scent for
a cheap advt. has been rendered almost unnaturally
acute by years of practice.

To-night (Thursday) is the last of the
panto, at Her Majesty’s (Melb.). Mr. and
Mrs. Ali Baba, their educated moke, also the
Three Rubes and the remarkable lady who
was picked up by Percy in the park, are all
going elsewhere. In place of the panto,
comes the revue, which is entitled “Come
Over Here,” because one name is as good as
another for revue purposes. The new form
of entertainment has been somewhat local-
ised, it seems. A rumor that bald stallites
are likely to be kissed on their nakedness
during the proceedings should help to pre-
vent any public misconception of the matter
about to be considered under revue heading.

is getting a look in between the departure of
“Joseph” and the arrival of “Madame X.”
Two performances of “The Doctor’s
Dilemma” were promised for Tuesday and

“On Our Selection” (which, by the way,
is not effusively complimentary to Australia)
is booming- again at Melbourne King’s,
therefore the staging of the new play from
America is postponed until further notice.
The broad comedy of the “Selection”
accounts for all the rush to the pay-boxes
these times. In the hroad tragedy section of
Bert Bailey’s popular show there are oppor-
tunities for stronger acting than is put into
it by the four persons chiefly concerned.
Lennon, as Cranky Jim, gets no blood-curdling
effects from his assassination of Carey
junior (Bellairs), whose fatal inter-
view with Kate (Miss Oakley) is very
feebly and mechanically conducted on both
sides. And when the corpse comes to be
discovered by Carey, senior (Kensington),
his paternal grief and horror are no more
convincing than the dissembled emotion of
Kate aforesaid. The lurid melodrama scene
in the Rudd family comedy is an item of
no financial importance to the management
apnarentl.y, but it might as well be played for
all it ought to be worth.

AT POVERTY POINT.
London papers mention the matrimonial engage-
ment of Cicely Courtneidge, the Australian-born
daughter of “Bobbie” Courtneidge, to Jack Hulbert,
a Cambridge undergraduate who went straight from
college to a leading part in “The Pearl Girl” at the
Shaftesbury. Their combined ages cannot amount
to more than 43, and their engagement is the natural
result of stage love-making, assisted by duets. Cicely
Courtneidge made her first appearance in mummery
at Melbourne Princess’s, as one of the juvenile fairies
in the “Midsummer Night’s Dream” which her father
produced for George Musgrove when he returned to
Australia on a holiday trip. She has grown into
a quite beautiful girl and a charming comic-opera
artist, with a full equipment of qualifications.
“O Hana San” writes all the way from 32 Coram-
street, Russell Sauare, London, W.C., England
(knock off here for refreshments), to ask The
Bulletin to warn all managers against using or
imitating the Japanese signs, effects, furniture,
crockery or fried fish appearing in her act. Ten
millions of managers are accordingly warned.
P.S.: The Bulletin doesn’t know whether “O Hana
San” has a wooden leg, but, if she has, no imitation
will be permitted. Any manager with a wooden
leg will be prosecuted for infringement of copyright.
Pianist Goll must have felt the emptiness of Mel-
bourne’s musical “culture” when he took the Town
Hall platform the other night to play the piano to a
mere handful' of listeners scattered among the 2000
seats. It is poor comfort for a lonely performer to
be told next day that what the audience lacked in
numbers it made up for in enthusiasm, etc. The fact
is that no Australian city is big enough or sufficiently
lacking in places of entertainment to provide a
crowd of paying public for a resident pianist who
announces a series of recitals. Not many people will
trouble to go and hear a good artist for his art’s
sake, when he has lost his novelty—and they have to
pay for seats. A pianist, to draw a crowd, must
come as a stranger on the boom ticket. Even the
notoriety of a Paderewski would soon lose its finan-
cial value in Melbourne if he lived there and taught
at a local conservatorium. Edward Goll is one of
the very best pianists going, perhaps, but for the
comparatively few people who are interested in his
playing the Town Hall is much too accommodating
these times. A small hall and a general admission
charge of two “bob” is what the musical public
wants.
The. Paths parlor cinematograph is now fairly estab-
lished as a practical means of bringing the sights of
the day into every home. Recently an operator
rushed around Melbourne with the' little camera and
took a series of moving photos which were repro-
duced on the “home” screen at the depot three or
four hours later.
“C. Ockney”: “A Pair of Silk Stockings” was on
February 21 staged at the London Criterion, which,
remembering the dear old “Pink Dominoes” days, is
just the place for them —I mean, it. On the same
night, “The Joy-Ride Lady” was announced for pro-
duction at the New. London is a-going it, as loole
used to say. In the latter piece (a comic opera from
the French, which was originally adapted from the
German), Rutland Barrington, the old Savoyard, has
a part. Rutland, by the way, has been telling the
public in the Sunday Referee that one reason for
the big profits made out of the Gilbert and Sullivan
operas at the start was the modest salary-list. He,
of course, was the original Vicar in “The Sorcerer,”
and the Captain in “Pinafore,” and he received £4 a
week for the former and £6 for the latter part.
Well, lots of rude people will say that if he was
engaged as a singer he was over-paid. One night in
those early daj's Gilbert, who was in front with Sulli-
van, whispered in great agitation to his collabora-
tor. “Good heavens! What’s the matter with Bar-
rington? lie’s singing in tune l”
The Gaud Mayor of Newcastle (Eng.) gave a lun-
cheon lately to all the members of the theatrical
purfesh who were just then appearing in the town.
George Robey was star guest. The Mayor, in propos-
ing the health of mummery, stated that in all his
years on the bench he had never had an actor or
actress brought before him. Whereupon Robey
smiled joyfully and said “Luck!”
“Te Pana”: Tonga has advanced another stage
toward civilisation, the “movies” having found their
way into the dreamy little kingdom. The Tongan
takes to the flickers as he takes to cocoanut-milk. He
gives the delighted hand to the handsome bloque
who rescues the maiden from the Cuss with the
Wicked Eye, sniggers loud and long when the
Parisian peeler falls in the soup, dolefully hands out
the bird to “scenies,” and is great on Scripture pic-
tures. It costs him from a quarter to half a dollar
for the privilege of getting excited and working up a
thirst.
A “wordless humorist” of the Austrian persua-
sion is just now figuring largely at the London
Palace under the misleading name of Joe Jackson.
Austrian Joe appears to be quite un-English in his
modesty. As soon as he arrived in London to play
his engagement. he was asked if he would like to be
interviewed. He said, “Vait a liddle. Ve see vot
happens first ven I open.” Vot a lot of preliminary
mush paragraphs re imported mummers might be
spared to Australia if the press would only vait a
liddle and see vot happens !
Another warning to theatre orcliestrians. At the
Kingsway Theatre (London), where Arnold Bennett's
“The Great Adventure” has been booming, there is
no orchestra at all, and the soulful audiences are not
feeling the want of music between the acts.
Woe to the deadhead ! In the eye of the law
(true, it may turn out to be a dead and glassy eye)
the deadhead is a malefactor, a nuisance and an idle
and disorderly dodger. Somebody has discovered that
on Jan. 17, 1704, Queen Anne (“Brandy-faced Nan”)
issued a proclamation that “no Persons do Presume
to come into a Playhouse without Paying the Prices
established for their respective Places.” This she did
because she was anxious to put down “everything
contrarv to Religion and Good Manners in the The-
atres.” In further pursuance of this praiseworthy
object she proclaimed that “no Person of what
Qualitv soever must Presume to go behind the Scenes
or come upon the Stage either before or during the
Acting of any Play.” Any reprobates “disobeying
these Our Roval Commands will be proceeded against
as Contemners of Our Royal Authority and Disturbers
of the Publick Peace.” What the gentle Anne would
have prescribed for the Lady who brought her few
glad rags from the stage into the publick audience
during the Acting of a I’lay is too dreadful to think
of.
Somebody suggests that the “revue” practice of
bringing show girls from the stage, and landing them
among the Johnnies in the stalls, will have a power-
ful influence for moral good. It will serve to
disillusionise the simple and bring the idolatry of
stage “beauties” into ridicule. Not many of the
beauties are lovely when the glamor of the stage is
off them. And it certainly is off them when they
walk among the audience.
“Illegible”: Why don’t trained nurses
form themselves into a union—a common,
ordinary trades union—to win shorter hours-
and better pay? The nationalisation of the
medical and nursing professions is to come;
but in the meantime the life of a trained
nurse is a good deal worse than the char-
lady’s. Three years of very hard training
in a public hospital, in the course of
which dozens of girls break down;
salary ss. to 12s. a week, and in some
public hospitals, where the probationers
are carefully selected, there is no pay
whatever to start with: 1 nally, examina-
tions to pass before she can go forth to earn
her crust as “a fully qualified trained nurse.”
She may either take up private nursing or
join the staff of a private hospital. At
private nursing, except in midwifery eases,
her wage is £2 7s. 6d. per week, or less.
Very often she is cook and housemaid as
well as nurse; her working hours anything
from 16 to 18 a day; and she is lucky if
she gets six uninterrupted hours of sleep.
Out of that £2 7s. 6d. or less per week she
has to pay £1 per month for “Home fees,”
and the washing bill is heavy. If she joins
a private hospital, as staff nurse, her pay
is £1 per week. For this she generally works
from 6 a.m. till 8 p.m., with two hours off
during the day. She may have as many as
seven private patients to attend to; and, as
these patients are paying heavy fees
to the hospital and the doctor, they
expect “every attention” from the over-
worked nurse. Seven sick people to look
after for 2s. 10id. a day of 12 working
hours ! Would a man go through such train-
ing, acquire such skill, and do so many
hours of often-arduous work for the money?
And if a man wouldn’t, why should a
woman?

“Melbournian”: I know nothing more
beautiful than the sight of a professional
weather prophet watering his garden half
an hour before the rain starts.
There is a very wild man in Sydney. Ilis
case is that lie employs girls for evening
work only at his soft-drinkery in a certain
well-known show place, and that, for the
sake of getting ornamental girls, he pays
225. Gd. a week, whereas the legal minimum
is 16s. Gd. Some jester informed on him
for not giving his employees the legal after-
noon off, and that was how the trouble arose.
Despite his plea that he couldn’t give them
an afternoon off when they hadn’t an after-
noon on he fell in according to the terms of
the law. He didn’t fall in much, but he fell
In ss. and 30s. costs. The full measure
of his degradation only came to his know-
ledge when lie drifted into his club next
night and asked the usual push to have a
drink. The usual push sat tight and wore
a glassy stare. One man even laughed the
metallic laugh. Finally a hollow voice that
sounded like someone throwing dice in Ilell
said these words: “We won’t drink with a
criminal.”

The Sherlock Holmeses of the Victorian
force are hard at it trying to discover the
sender of the bomb that badly damaged
harmless little F. E. Lee, one of Victoria’s
Land Purchase Commissioners, and more or
less injured his fellow Commissioner, Hastie,
and a subordinate. They have reasoned out
that it was intended for Lee, that it was
meant to wound, if not kill; and that it came
from Sydney, as shown by the postmark.
Also, that it was skilfully constructed and
exploded according to specifications. Fur-
thermore, the sender left a clue. The brown
paper wrapper of the parcel had been used
before, and it bore this printed legend:
“Should there be any complaints respecting
the contents of this package, please refer to
our agents, Messrs. , Sydney ; Co.,
London, England.” The culprit apparently
couldn’t resist the jest, and so he or she left
the tag on. All the police have to do now
is to search for a homicidal subject with a
sense of humor.
There was a faint whoop last week when
the Ma State’s Chief Secretary threw out his
chest and remarked proudly, “Governments
do not regard the police-officer of to-day as a
thief-catcher only, hut as a well-trained, effi-
cient all-round officer whose services can he
utilised for the general welfare of the com-
munity.” Then it transpired that all the
trotting and fussing of the 2(500 N.S.W.
policemen in their various sidelines and mis-
cellaneous capacities had been worth just
£40,000 for the year. It isn’t worth it. A
policeman should he a policeman all the time,
just as a thief is a thief 24 hours a day and
seven days a week. No sensible person wants a
house mastiff to knock off being a dog and
come in and mend the stockings or wind up
the gramophone. If a dog of that sort could
he procured, it wouldn’t be as effective as the
plain or straight dog that attended strictly to
business.

Fort Gellibrand, which lurks at the hack
of flat AVilliamstown (Vic.), dropped three
shells under the nose of the speedy bay
steamer Mourilyan one day last week, and
caused that perturbed vessel to shriek loudly
and dive for shelter. The officers in the
fort don’t deny the firing, but they assert
that the dashed projectiles hit the sea a
good GOOyds. away and not within 30 or 40
yards, as the boss bay mariner, Captain
Forbes, says they did. The weight of evi-
dence is with Forbes, his crew and pas-
sengers. People don’t stand on their toes
and howl at a white splash nearly half a mile
off. They are merely interested. Then Gelli-
brand is. badly situated for practice pur-
poses, being just round a busy corner where
ships are always coming and going. A few
years ago it bounced a shot right over the
bows of an outward-bound inter-State boat.
That fact will probably be remembered
against the impatient, sudden-death depot
when the present matter is inquired into.

A matter to be carefully noted and remembered by
cynical students of the Advanced Women s movement
is the murder of the editor of Paris Eigaro by the
wife of a politician whom he had denounced, rightly
or wrongly, as a person unfit to be Minister of
* inance. It is, by the way, a matter of much
personal concern to all newspaper editors, accus-
tomed to the receipt of indignant letters from woman
champions of public men. The Bulletin, for m-
stance can speak of one anonymous correspondent, of
the softer sex, who boils over with indignation re
any unfavorable comment on William Watt, the Vic-
torian Premier. Fortunately she seems disposed to
leave the punishment of the offending editor to be
meted out by God, in the Hereafter; but if she were
a less patient and hopeful lady, the result might
have been a tragedy on earth ere now. At any rate,
she adds to this paper’s editorial interest in the case
of the recent murder of Mr. Calmette, the Paris
Figaro editor, by Mrs. Caillaux. Figaro had for
two or three years been disclosing alleged facts in-
jurious to the character of Mr. Caillaux, and was
threatening to publish some more. Hence the in-
dignation of his wife. On the day before the next
lot of disclosures was due the lady wrote a letter to
her husband wherein she said. “When you receive
this I shall have executed justice.” Then she went
to the Figaro office, saw the editor and fired four
bullets into him, with the result that he died imme-
diately remarking, “I only did my duty.” The
porter who had shown the lady into the room rushed
to disarm her, and she said. Leave .me dvoue, I
am a woman. I shall not run away.” Mrs. Cail-
laux’s insistence upon the dignity of her sex at this
supreme moment was, perhaps, even more humorous
than her assurance that she would not attempt a
mad flight from the building in her tight skirts,
Nevertheless her determination not to do a bolt was
droll enough, seeing that she had already given her
card to the hall porter, and flight would have
availed her nothing unless she took the precaution
to kill the porter and pick up the card before she
fled. However, the main point to be considered is
that Mrs. Caillaux, having fired four bullets into
ing him°a me I
am a woman!” when somebody proposed to treat her
as though She were dangerous. The cry of the egre-
gious feminine will be repeated, in various keys,
all the time she is on her trial for murdering the
editor, “Don’t touch me. I a woman /’’However
the Law may handle her after the trial, Mrs. Call-
laux is pretty certain to be touched more gently
than if she were a man. The very first idea of the
Paris mob was to label Caillaux as. the “assassin,”
and regard his missus as a minor offender. What
the cynical students of Advanced Womanhood would
like to know is how do the W.P.A., the W.N.P.L.
and every other society beginning with “W” for
women, measure the culpability of Mrs. C. in this
matter. Being fully entitled to a vote from the
Suffragette point of view, is she fully entitled to be
guillotined like a man ? And if not, why not ? Ilav-
ing murdered the editor in the interests of her hus-
bands political career (u 3 she supposed), is she less
responsible than hubby for the editor’s sudden
decease ? And if so, why so ? No society beginning
w j th W admits that woman is a weaker vessel or an
irresponsible creature. The question of Mrs. Cail-
laux’s fitness for the last dread penalty of the man-
made Law> is the ques ti o n which all Advanced
Woman societies should promptly dispose of. They
are up against it> The possibility of an indignant
man murderillg a « social editress” looms In the
future and the exact position of M rs. o aillaux should
b e clearly defined.

DIRGE OF A WET SUNDAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rain foil almost continuously throughout yester-
day.—Monday morning paper.
See where the damp, umbrella-d curate passes
Towards his damp, deserted, draughty church ;
With drops of rain upon his staring glasses,
He swims along like some inquiring perch.
The drip, drip, drip of everlasting showers
Has dulled my brain; Imagination leaves
Off like a run-down toy; the grieving hours
Pass in the weeping of the sodden eaves.
The angry hum of some car as it dashes
Through seas of slush diverts me this drear day;
It streams away with many kissing splashes,
Outflinging mud in quite a regal way.
Some thin, cracked music ! (’Tis a cold bell ringing !
The curate plucks it with a thin, cold hand!)
Some organ moaning and some hopeless singing
As Night creeps softly o’er a hopeless land.
N.S.W.

Another outbreak of typhoid at Kew
(Vic.) Hospital for Mental Diseases draws
attention to the fact that the Government
hasn’t yet had that establishment connected
with the sewerage system. The excuse is
that the new hospital at Mont Park was ex-
pected to supersede the hopeless barracks at
Kew and the ramshackle shanties at Yaria
Bend, but it is now found that the new
place will only accommodate the perennial
increase in lunacy cases, and that the ancient
contraptions will have to continue in use
for the Lord knows how long. Yet, as show-
ing the hypocrisy of this excuse, last year
the Government actually did a little to pla-
cate the persistent director, Dr. Jones —it
sewered the laundry and the morgue, though
why the latter place should have been
attended to first is a mystery, inasmuch as
the only persons who use it for more than
a few minutes at a time don’t care a brass
farthing whether it is sewered or not. It is
sienificurtt that the aforesaid Dr. Jones says
that, ever since he has been boss he has been
vainly trying to get decent sanitary systems
applied, not only to Kew and Yarra Bend,
but to similar institutions at Sunbury, Ara-
rat and Beechworth. It should never be for-
gotten that patients in mental hospitals have
no votes, and under the Party Government
system a person without a vote is just as
well dead.
“0.K.”: Rev. Eddy is touring Australia
in quest of funds for the Mission to Lepers.
“Saving the souls” of lepers seems an essen-
tially martyrdomic occupation in an age
when martyrs might be much more usefully
employed. Of all the slow and shocking
diseases that human sufferers must surely
die of (if no fatal accident or other disease
kills the patient in the meantime) leprosy is
most calculated to chasten the doomed ones’
spirit and fit them for life to come. And
seeing that the whole business of priests and
parsons is to lead sinners to repentance by
insisting on the evil of their past and the
dangers of their future —to make them virtu-
ously broody, in short —what can be the
good of talking religion to lepers, whose dis-
ease teaches them resignation, and the
vanity of social worldliness, and leaves them
with absolutely nothing but Heaven to hope
for? The D—l offers the occupants of a
leper Station no inducement to prepare their
souls for ll—l. It is the merest waste of
piety for any healthy person to be a mis-
sionary to lepers. If they get a humane
Government’s consideration for their sick
bodies their souls will take care of them-
selves. Everybody must die as surely as
any leper. The average journalist lives and
dies without having received the least per-
sonal attention from a missionary.

(Torrens) Act 18. 19/2/’I4), a proposal to
use paper instead of parchment, for certifi-
cates of title, caused a howl from most of
Adelaide’s lawyers and land-agents last year;
but the proposal was
carried out, and the howl
has perished. The stiff
paper looks very like
sheepskin, only cleaner,
and of course takes the
ink better. Now that
will-probates, which are
to last for ever, are offici-
ally required to be on
paper—and very flimsy
banknote stuff at that —
the sheep’s outer margin
is going right out of
fashion. And, of course,
under the beneficent R.P.
Act which Torrens orig-
inated in Adelaide (and
which is gradually being
copied all over the world
where lawyers’ vested in-
terests are not too
strong), the Title an
owner clings to so
proudly is only a dupli-
cate. The original is bound
up in a fireproof room at
the L.T.0., and to lose or destroy his certifi-
cate altogether would be a less expensive
matter than losing a £5 note.
The attempt to blow up the Vic. Closer
Settlement Commissioners with a bomb had
at least one humorous result. Melb. Herald
published a photo, of three smudgy men
standing behind a table, each holding some-
thing in his hand and gazing thereat. These
were described as “Detectives examining
fragments of bomb.” The sleuth hounds of
the law had seemingly ’phoned for the
’Erald’s camera fiend to come and “take”
them in their first act of unravelling the
mystery. Their facial expressions were
misty, but their attitudes clearly denoted a
consensus of opinion that the bomb had ex-
ploded from the inside, and that it wasn’t
loaded with soup.
Adelaide Police Court lately found itself
up against an odd incident. The plaintiff
in an assault-and-robbery case was present
when the Court opened, but missing when
the case came on. Later, an agitated tele-
phone message came from him, and still later
he appeared in person. He said he had been

square things, if he would get into the
Glenelg train (which still starts in the

regarded the money as an interim dividend
on the £8 he said he had been robbed of; for
he took it and the train, but got out at the
first station and telephoned to the police.
The Court was feeling good-natured, so it
took no further notice of this adventure, and
just fined defendant £2O, as he wanted to
“get back to Tasmania.” Which seems
rough on Tasmania.
This paper’s “Old Soldier” discourses:
Hamar Greenwood, M.P., in the course of a
vigorous Parliamentary denunciation of the
social influences that sway the British army,
said, according to the cable: —
Australia has wiped out the social status in the
army, and the two best officers in the Australian
Training School are a son of a policeman and a
soil of a shoemaker. Such men would have no chance
at Sandhurst Military College.
As a matter of fact, they would probably
never get to Sandhurst, for the simple rea-
son that their fathers could not afford the
£4OO it would cost to keep them there for
the two years course. Furthermore, it
would be necessary, after they had passed
out, and got their commissions, to give them
at least ±lOO a year for about seven years
till they became captains and could live on
their pay. The cadets’ parents or guardians
have to hand out money heavily for their
board and tuition, supply bed and table
linen and uniform, and also provide pocket-
money on a liberal scale, so that they can
join cricket, football and other clubs. But
these are not the only reasons why the sons
of people of humble extraction are not to
be found at such institutions. In the first
place, their lives either at Sandhurst and
Woolwich would be rendered intolerable by
the other lads. There is no pariah so com-
plete as the “outsider” in a milieu of youth-
ful Britishers. In the second place,
even assuming that the policeman’s offspring
lived through a college life, without being
“ragged” off his head, he would do no good
for himself in his regiment. The feminine
influence, which is all-powerful in many
cases in this environment, is dead against
such a class of warrior. The power of the
women was shown during the Boer war.
Lord Roberts’ staff, for example, was a mass
of dukes and so forth, not .because he
wanted these young men, but for the reason
that his masterful and society-loving wife
dominated the situation. Lady White was
notoriously the better horse in the stable,
which she shared with her husband, the
general. Kitchener is about the only front-
ranker who has been credited with complete
immunity from feminine, which is another
way of saying social, influences. And there
have been doubts even about him.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr Hollis MX.A., thought it would bo unwise
to remove tile remains of those buried in Newtown
,\ T S W 1 cemetery, which is now to be closed in
its Hod's tore capacity. He would not like to see the
ground used for cricket and football or other sports
and he could not see any reason why it should not
be made a pleasant resort and playground for
children. — Daily paper.
Sing “Ring-a-ring-a-rosy” round your grandma’s
grave,
Let your merriment rise high above her clay;
Sing ail the nursery rhymes re wood and wave,
And “Here we go gathering nuts and may.”
Sing “Goosey, Goosey, Gander,” to the vault,
And nonsense verse to Erebus and Dis.
But when you’ve grown a little tis a faulj;
In converted graveyards to behave like this:
“Buck in, Souf Melbourne! Gouge his eye!
Carlton is premiers, though we make the wasters die!
Buck in, Souf Melbourne! Smash their ’ead!
Bash ’em with a thighbone though you wake the
dead !”
How the dear departed love the children’s joy !
When with laughter shrill they break from Sunday-
school !
But the older ones are rot so very coy,
And stay from church to keep the Golden Rule.
Will the dead object to lovers by their tombs,
Though they bar the flannelled oaf and muddied
fool?
Though the babies who sing “Mary quite contrary”
Are quite welcome, will they bar the Golden Rule?
Her head upon his shoulder by the stone,
Where the granite angel points ’yond earthly bars;
Comes no protest from the dead while girlecn droops
her head
And the Joy of Youth encompasses the stars,
“You see it’s this way,” said the courteous ghost,
“I’ve done with agriculture many years.
I died in eighteen fifty, and the most
I claim is neither fear nor praise nor tears.
Full sixty years agone I dust became,
And the worms brought me to surface long ago,
Blowing round the world and caught by wind and
flame,
And it matters not to me the seed you sow.
“The dead are dead —the earth is but for life,
The child, the young man and the ancient fool.
Above me let the new fool take a wife
And play the game that’s called the Golden Rule,
Though “Goosey Goosey’’ wander all the day —
Within my chamber low no child may be,
A 1 or Carlton nor Souf Melbourne —till they pay
The price that makes them care for nought—like
me.”
N.S.W.
VERY BAD PENMAN.
BUFFERS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bishop Mercer, of Tasmania, who is now return-
ing to England after 12 years of ministry among
the apples, has discovered Australia’s chief political
need, and has expressed it in one word: “It seems
to me that you want what we have in England—a
fairly large ‘buffer’ class. That is the class against
which the extremes of Radicalism or of Toryism
dash themselves. If Australia is to become politically
stable there should come into existence a consider-
able ‘buffer’ class that should have sympathies with
both Labor and non-Labor aspirations.”
We’ve democrats and plutocrats
(A sprinkling of the latter),
And Liberals and gibberals
Whose everlasting chatter
Is beerisomcly wearisome,
And cranks a trifle eerisome,
Who really do not matter.
We’ve jingo-gun-and-sabre men,
And lots of ardent Labor men,
Who’d help their weaker neighbor men
To get a little fatter;
We’ve picture, verse and story men
And dull prosaic Tory men —-
Official banquet stuffers.
Our social system suffers
Prom many sorts of duffers.
We’ve wowsy men and frowsy men,
And shoot-their-linen-euffers.
But ’twixt the Rads and Tory men
(Those hard commercial hoary men)
We have not any buffers.
Buffers !
The Bishop sighs for Buffers.
Stodgy men with sturdy beams
To stay the dash of two extremes
And harmonise the rival themes —
The smoother and the roughers.
Murmurs Mercer in his dreams.
“Heaven send ’em Buffers—
Buffers !
Much in need Australia seems
Of Buffers
Buffers !
Buffers!

STRINGER, Maoriland’s
new Arbitration Court Judge.
Extract from the friendly (?) testimonial
supplied by E. W. M. Grigg, editor of the
Round Table, to Braham, the imported
English editor of Sydney Telegraph :
lie has the gift of getting on with those with
whom he is thus associated, and of making the most
of their abilities.
That paragraph wears its sting in its tail.
Whether Grigg intended to put it there is
beside the question. But if Braham had
ever associated with Grigg and made the
most of Grigg’s abilities without Grigg’s
permission, and to his extreme annoyance,
Grigg could not have found a neater way of
calling Braham a literary crow or vulture.
If Grigg is really bursting with admiration
of Braham and wants to say so, then liis
English is not as plain as it might be, and
Ins recommendation should be lost at the
first opportunity.
G. A. Jeffries writes: —
John Kneeshaw, lately Traffic-Manager of Sydney’s
vast tramway system, died at Summer Hill, Sydney,
week before last. He was not altogether popular
with the great bulk of his staff, because they did
not understand him. But the men who worked close
to him both loved and respected him. He was as
near to being a genius as a sane, healthy and
thoroughly balanced man may get. He was a Vic-
torian by birth, but while still an infant his parents
took him to Canterbury, Maoriland. He went to
school at Heathcote, and afterwards to Christ College,
Christchurch. There he displayed unusual brilli-
ance at mathematics, but was as dull as ditchwater
in other respects. He drifted into the railways by
some route this writer wots not of, and from the
jump made his mark as a brilliant accountant.
When there was difficulty over a balance-sheet, it
was: “Oh, hang it —get ‘Black Jack’; he’ll find it
m one act!” And “Black Jack” invariably did. He
was still in his early twenties when he became the
senior and most dreaded auditor of Maoriland Rail-
way Department, and responsible to the General
Manager alone. lie was the first man to dare to
audit the accounts of traffic managers. He bowled
out more swindlers than all the other auditors put
together. Robertson, who had started a fund with
which he intended to go fruit-growing in California
when the time was ripe, decided after the first visit
of “The Raven” that the time would never be riper
than it was then. He had a small printing press,
and printed his own railway season tickets. “The
Raven” could not understand that the amount re-
ceived from season-ticket holders should be so small,
and told him so. He got hold of one of Robertson’s
faked tickets, and gazed at it so earnestly that the
IN TOPHET.
S KULLUS: “Who are they?"
EYEUS : “They are the politicians who main-
tained that the water supply at Canberra
was adequate for all requirements."
artist contracted heart failure. Next week he came
back and started digging again. Robertson got on
his feet and fled with what boodle he had on hand,
leaving his investments behind him. He got clear
away. When “Black Jack” heard the news he
smiled grimly and said: “I knew, that fellow was a
thief—and I’ll bet you anything you like he
printed his own tickets!” This was afterwards
proved to be the case. Had he stayed in Maoriland
he would inevitably have become the General Mana-
ger in all but name. He was a sombre individual
who called few men friend, but those who were so
honored he never forgot. Eddy, of N.S.W., saw
him, and took a great fancy to him. Later on,
David Kirkcaldie, on behalf of Eddy, offered him a
position on N.S.W. Railways. A travelling Amurkan
railway magnate also tried to induce him to go to
Uncle Sam’s land, but he decided to come to Aus-
tralia, to N.S.W.’s benefit. He had a tremendous
memory. He remembered every trait and idiosyn-
crasy of every officer whose accounts he audited, and,
after a couple of inspections, he knew intuitively if
the man was a swindler in what way he would
swindle. On one occasion he bowled out a country
station-master, and the man went suddenly mad
with fear. He drew a revolver and pointed it at
Kneeshaw. “Black Jack” sat down and lit his pipe,
talked the man out of his homicidal mood, did his
best for him in the ensuing trouble, and said nothing
about the revolver incident. He was very keen, and
when 17 years after the event I published a story
which told how a swindler got past him, he called
and asked for particulars. When he heard the
details he said he remembered the incident,
and gave the name of the officer. I believe
Kneeshaw could have told you accurately
the number of the page in liis first school-
book that the blot was on.
* A rather confused statement by “Martin
Luther”
Morrissey, one time Vic. Minister for Lands, is
seen around Melbourne occasionally with Big Jack,
his son. Many years ago, when Big Jack was Young
Jack, and the ex-Minister was a schoolmaster, there
was another Morrissey who dealt in grain—mostly
oats. Schoolmaster Morrissey took naturally to
butter and cheese and pigs and eggs, and gravitated
as naturally out of schoolmastership. He was known
as “Pig” Morrissey, and the grain man as “Gat”
Morrissey. “Pig” Morrissey made a fortune in sell-
ing produce to South Africa, but “Oat” Morrissey
turned back to his early job—which, unfortunately,
was journalism. Much of the other Morrissey’s for-
tune is due to Big Jack —the son aforesaid. lie
looks so much a produce man that you wonder why
he hasn t a cow under his arm and a big frieze coat
with him.
When O’Callaghan was Chief of Police
in Victoria some of his detractors didn’t con-
sider him a very astute sort of man, but per-
haps he was under-rated. In his capacity
as a retired O’Callaghan he recommends the
employment of dogs, as in France, for the
tracking of criminals. Let it be noted most
carefully that O’Callaghan is retired. There
is now no risk of an ambitious dog under-
mining him in his billet.
Thus the Sydney Freeman’s Journal
(19/3/T4), more, doubtless, in error than
in anger:—
The presentation was made by Mr. Tight Ryan,
editor, who spoke in the highest terms of the
recipient’s ability.
The thing would look nasty were not Tighe
Ryan one of the most abstemious of ink-

R. S. Smythe, the Much-Travelled, had
his 81st birthday recently. Smythe abides
at Balwyn, an outlying suburb of Melbourne,
and, when the hot weather is in season, the
old warrior is not much seen around Collins-
street. At these times he is. suspected of
being busy on a book of reminiscences. But
Smythe seemingly has abandoned the idea of
writing the sort of autobiography which
would be so much more readable than the
average book of recollections. He could have
talked very openly about the celebrities he
managed, for they mostly had a quite remark-
able talent for dying a few years afterwards.
The chances are that he will outlive the lot.
“Parisine”: The murderess Caillaux and
her husband —the French ex-Minister of
Finance—are both peculiar. Caillaux’s first
fancy, so far as disclosed, was for the wife
of one of his clerks. That clerk was rapidly
promoted. The, case might be like that of
Premier Melbourne, who put Mrs. Norton’s
husband into the paid magistracy
at £lOOO a year. The clerk, how-
ever, thought he saw a rat floating
in the air, and got a divorce. Cail-
laux married the lady. Then the
case arose which Sardou imagined
in “Divorcons.” Caillaux sus-
pected his wife of undue friendship
for her former husband, and began
a divorce suit with him (the pre-
vious husband) as co-respondent.
His friends joked him out of this.
Next the wife obtained a divorce
on account of his misconduct. She
was granted alimony, and on the
strength of this she married her
former husband, so that everything
was as it had been barring the
alimony. Caillaux then pursued the
present Madame Caillaux, the mur-
deress. She was married to Leon
Claretie, nephew of the Theatre Franqais
director. She got a divorce and married
Caillaux. Now she is in prison for the
slaying of the editor who apparently knew
(and said) too much about her remarkable
and numerous husband —the Minister of
Finance. And an excited populace is throw-
ing road metal.
Fire-fighter Rickwood, just dead rather
suddenly in Adelaide, had put in a lot of
hard work for his country during his 52
years of life. He was the son of a Cornish
coastguard, and was in Australia at 15, as a
middy on the old warship Sapphire. He
was present when British rule was pro-
claimed over the scrap of Papua which Bull
holds, and ultimately became instructor of
N.S.W. Naval Brigade, with charge of all
the coastal life-saving service. Incidentally

he was a torpedo expert, and one of the best
rifle-shots in Australia. Nine years ago he
resigned to become second (which soon
became first) in command of Adelaide’s
Fire Brigades. He had a Napoleonic way of
jumping on a blaze hard and early. He just
threw everything at it without waiting to
see if it was worth the trouble. If it
wasn’t worth the trouble nothing was lost,
and, if it was, the city was saved. He
saved it 11 times in quick succession during
the recent hot weather, and then a weak
heart bowled him out.
Lieutenant-Colonel “Reggie” Clarke, who
has died in Victoria, aged 34, was the
youngest officer of his rank in the Common-
wealth Army. He was a son of the late
Baronet and a grandson of old Peter Snod-
grass. Russell Clarke, M.L.C., and Frank
Clarke, M.L.C. (Vic.), are brothers; and
Baronet Rupert Clarke is a half-brother.
RILEY , BISHOP OF PERTH.
“S.T.S.”: “J.F.H.” misremembers when
lie talks (B. 5/3/’l4) of “Trollope’s famous
William Amelia,” in connection with the
still-living Henry Lucy. That is—without
professing to remember every character
in Trollope’s many books —David Christie
Murray certainly had a Mr. Amelia
in that excellent novel, “Tlie Way
of the World.” And it was understood to
be a satire on Lucy. In fact, lest there
should be any doubt remaining, the hero was
made to write a novel himself. Lucy has
just one such indiscretion on his record,
“Gideon Fleyce.” Very neatly, Mr. Amelia’s
was given the title of “Jacob Zladder.”
“Rangan” : T. J. Ryan, Banana-
land’s Parliamentary Labor leader,
is one of the most interesting speak-
ers in the Assembly and the best
debater. Also, as a lawyer, he has
few equals locally. His spirited
defence of Senator Ferricks in the
Denham libel case was the best
losing fight put up in Queensland
for many years. What is perhaps
less known of him is his open-
handedness. He not only returned
to Ferricks his share of the heavy
costs, but persuaded Denham to
return his share also. Before tak-
ing up law Ryan was a grammar-
school teacher, and a story says
that he used to give more time to
his law books that to his classes,
and several times was asked to
resign. But each time he hauled
down half a dozen law books from the shelf
and showed that he needn’t resign till he
wanted to.
DIED AGAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Emperor Menelik recently died again. This is
the third or fourth demise that monarch has, more
or less successfully, accomplished.
So Menelik has gone—and once again
AVe don the face of woe and garments sable,
And send condolences across the main
And shun the giddy dance and festive table,
So that the news of our consuming loyalty
May comfort the sad heart of kindred royalty.
’Til not by any means the only time
That Menelik has left this earthly portal,
And heaved a good-bye at his torrid clime
And craved admission at the Towers Immortal;
He used to die —with neither rhyme nor reason
(So said the Press) both in and out of season.
Just take, I beg, a momentary flight
(Of course I mean upon the vanes of fancy),
And track his soaring to yon Golden Height;
And then, by that same subtle necromancy,
Just hearken how the Guardian Peter greets him
And notice what astounded welcome meets him!
“You —Menelik? Oh, damn it —This is rough.
Some three times, sir, you know you’ve come
already,
(I quote the Times), and now your spirit tough
Knocks here again with spectral hand unsteady.
Three times before did earthly journals speak of it
And now again ! 0 Menelik —the cheek of it!
“You’ll read that notice, will you, in your fist,
Obituarising all your noblest features,
Forgetting that we’ve got your every twist
Recorded here like those of other creatures;
Now— arc you genuine spectre, Abyssinia,
Or is it just the divilment that’s in you ?
“If I admit you—p’raps within a week
You’re here again upon the old pretences,
AVith shroud on arm and phizog pale and meek
Intent to jump o’er these celestial fences;
1 let you in—some cherub tells the Vast of it,
You come once more —I’d never hear the last of it!
“You Frozen Fraud ! No human undertaker
Is ever certain when lie's got you down;
You’d try your dying jokes upon your Maker,
And bob up here like some demented clown ;
Get back with all this lying printed stuff of you,
And tell those journals that we’ve had enough of
you !”
QUEENSLAND.
F.B.
THE APPROACHING DEBACLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The recent storm did great damage around Parra*
matta. A cow house was blown down On the estate
of -Mr. Joseph Cook. —News item.
Last week the papers told a moving tale
Of Joseph Cook, his cow-house and a gale.
Details were given of the mighty blow
By means of which the structure was laid low;
Yet not a word did any of them say
Of that tornado wild which, day by day,
Creeps steadily upon the Liberals.
In three months time, or less, Cook and his pals
A far more devastating storm shall see—
And that will up against their Duck House be.
N.S.W.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The N.S.W. Miners’ Accident Relief
Fund.—This splendid insurance fund was established
in 1900, and has been worked with remarkable suc-
cess. The idea was to make the disabled miner inde-
pendent of the beggar’s bowl, and the widow of the
miner killed by accident more or less independent of
the washtub. The Act provides for contributions by
workmen (50 p.c.), employers (25 p.c.) and State
(25 p.c.). The workmen pay 44d. a week; or,
rather, the employer has to deduct 4jd. a week from
every workman’s wages. The benefits include pay-
ments of 15s. per week on disablement, 10s. to 15s.
per week to the widow or other adult dependent on a
mine workman on his death by accident, and ss. to
every child under 14, and £l2 for funeral expenses. The
workmen’s contribution is paid to a local committee,
which keeps its eye open for malingerers, and pays
out as far as the money in hand will go. The
balance (if any) is sent to a central Board, which
also receives the lump sums from employers and State,
and acts as general banker for the local committees.
The local committees are paid small fees (last year
the total was £1457, or about 44 p.c. of the work-
men’s contributions), and that and a few pounds for
incidentals and about £ 3 per week paid as fees to
the central Board cover the whole outlay, except for
claims. There are no canvassers, no paid collectors.
Every workman, except at the smallest mines, is a
contributor ; and the duty is thrown on the employer
of deducting the 9d. a fortnight from the workman’s
wages and handing it, without deduction, to the
proper authority. In the case of check-weighmen and
pick-men, the employers now also pay one-third of
these employees’ contributions. The Government pro-
vides office accommodation, stationery, etc., and pays
the salary of the secretary. 'These charges do not,
therefore, figure in the accounts.
The following table shows the total amounts col-
lected, the payments, and the cash balance (including
investments) :

This is the “Barrack-street” savings bank which
makes more or less pretence of being a private
institution, though the State Governor is ex officio
piesident, and the trustees are appointed by him
(which means by the Government) ; also the Gov-
ernor (which again is the Government) may guaran-
tee the bank, though the Legislative Assembly must
say the word before the Government backs its bills
Qw 6 r ( ha ? £*>o,ooo. Sooner or later this and
the State Bank (Government Savings Bank) must of
course, be amalgamated ; it is utterly absurd that the
State should back the bills of a competitor, whose
o™ces are m some instances within a stone’s throw
or its own institution. It is bad enough to have
Commonwealth and State scrambling for the public’s
coppers without permitting this further overlapping
and waste. b
The plain result of the scramble is shown in the
past two years figures. No losses have had to be written
off, although the foundations have probably been
laid for a few; yet the business has not paid, and
the reserves have been cut into in each year In
Too Ann y r r ’ J n order t° whitewash the sepulchre,
£20,000 has been grabbed out of the poor little
Mortgages and General Investments Depreciation
Fund, reducing that account to £33,774—and this
at a time when public securities are mostly shrinking
and when, on account of the boom valuation of local
properties, it is highly probable that some bad
advances are being made. In any case £33 774
is a miserable reserve to safeguard £1,476 537 of
mortgage investments, £98,977 sunk in office
premises, and £7,623,611 in Government stock, muni-
cipal debentures (very sick municipalities some of
them) and bank deposits. Apart from the £123,155
atow cr edit of working account with the Bank of
N.S.W., the bank has £1,700,000 on deposit with
the various private banks. To this extent it merely
acts as a middleman, a mere collector of ha’pence
for more energetic people to use; and since the more
energetic people must make their profit, it is really
astonishing that on the mean of the denosits it
actually paid so much as £3 12s. p.c. interest last
year. Still, it couldn’t afford to pay quite that
much, otherwise the reserves wouldn’t be drawn upon
And that raises the question: What is the manage-
ment going to do about it? If a private bank, in a
time of great prosperity and inflated prices dared
to weaken its very moderate reserves by paying a
dividend in excess of its earnings -for-two years
running, there would be shoulder-shrugs all round
and a stream of its depositors would remember that
they had other use for their money. But, in this
case, why trouble? There is the good old State cow
to bail up if the bank’s own milk-supply should run
out.

When the co. had its beginnings and showed a
rather handsome profit on paper—the only one, by
the way, which it has shown—The Bulletin felt
impelled to throw cold water on the said profit.
An insurance premium is generally the price of a
year’s insurance paid in advance, therefore the pre-
mium a new co. receives a month after it has
started business has still a month’s liability to run
at the end of the year, and the one it receives in the
middle of the year has six months’ risk ahead of it,
and the one it receives on the last day of the year
represents payment for 365 days’ chances, of which
364 days are a liability when the accounts are made
up. Therefore a company that is intensely particular
about its finances sets aside a sum as “reserve for
unexpired risks,” or makes a hidden provision for
this liability and includes it in the general reserve.
If it fails to do this it is almost in the position of
a bank which forgets to include some of its deposits
among its liabilities, for if 12 months’ premium has
been received, and only one month of the 12 has
expired when the accounts are closed, the great bulk
of the premium is really in the nature of a deposit.
There are two kinds of successful fire offices. One
has heavy expenses and small losses because it spends
much money on a rigid weeding out of dangerous
risks. The other keeps down expenses, takes more
risks, and makes bigger losses. The 1.0. A. has the
bad luck to have both heavy losses and heavy ex-
penses. It started with a big bill for preliminary
charges. Apparently, it is loaded up with premises
and officials. It states, with some apparent pride,
that it has, in addition to its head office at Sydney,
branches at Melbourne, Adelaide, Brisbane, Perth,
Hobart. Rockhampton, Ballarat and Geelong, as well
as “chief agencies” at Townsville, Launceston and
Newcastle. These are a lot of establishments to live
on a net premium income of £54,618 as shown in
this table.—

The Insurance Office of Australia has a
small brother which uses the same premises, is run
by the same management, and started with very
much the same shareholders. It is a repetition of
the case of the big Victoria and the smaller Victoria
General in Melbourne. The small co. does a lot of
re-insurance for the big one, and this is its record

The small concern started on a comparatively inex-
pensive basis with only £BO3 of preliminary ex-
penses, and it showed better results all round than
its big relative. As the two share the same business
in the same office, it is just possible that the small
co. has been unduly favored and the large one put to
an undue disadvantage in the division of the ex-
penses. In such a case the division must be more or
less arbitrary. There is a certain element of absurd-
ity in this business of the two companies and two
sets of accounts and stationery, and the one co.
taking re-insurances from the other. If it was one
co. and one balance-sheet and one set of stationery,
and the two capitals were knocked into one, and the
small co. vanished, and the big co. became a bit
bigger and assumed the whole risk instead of divid-
mg it, quite a lot of gum and stamps might be saved.

That needs some explanation. Capital is £295,000,
composed of £195,000 in ordinaries and £lOO,OOO in 8
p.c. prefs., of which 4 p.c. is cumulative; besides
that there are still £172,000 worth of debentures.
The original capital was £260,000, but the pro-
moters collared £lOO,OOO, leaving only £160,000 for
the co. to build its line and carry on with. So
£lOO,OOO more capital was got in, and £200,000
worth of debentures issued as well. With such a
load of debenture interest to pay the ordinaries get
nothing at all, and the preferences occasionally get 4
p.c. as shown in the table. Usually they get nothing at
all, and the co., up to last year, owed them £29,332
in arrears. About the middle of 1913, an arrange-
ment was made whereby the pref. holders should get
some of their accumulated back dividends. They
agreed to forego £14,687 of it, the balance to
be paid thus: all sums under £lO in cash, over £lO
in “Funded Certificates” bearing 6 p.c. to be repaid
in 1923, or before if the co. cares to buy them. The
co. is to put by £5OO a year, beginning in 1915,
towards buying up these certificates, which at
December, 1913, amounted to £14,103; so that
only £542 of the arrears was paid in cash. In the
table on top, this £14,103 is regarded as debentures,
and counted in with them. As this liability now
appears for the first time, things are made to look
worse, though they really are better. Some back
dividend has been paid; and a liability of £14,687
to pref. shareholders is wiped out. An unexplained
addition to the reserve fund is included in the profits.
'The ordinaries are a long way off dividends. Before they
can get a taste the co. has to pay 8 p.c. on pref.
shares (£8000), 6 p.c. on the £14,103 (£846),
£5OO towards repurchasing certificates, a huge wad
of debenture interest, and £10,370 (being the
amount due to the Emu Bay and Mount Bischoff Co.
for the lease of their railway). The two latter, of
course, come off before the profits are shown in the
table. Even then a 4 p.c. div. on the ords. would
require £3900 ; so that for them to get that within
at least five years the co. must make £14,000 of
profit, and it has never approached that. When
the prefs. have had five yearly 8 p.c. divs., their right
to preference ceases, and they rank with the ordin-
aries, which may then get a taste —but a very small
one.
The co. was formed some 17 years ago, when it
leased the Emu Bay and Mount Bischoff Co.’s Burnie-
Waratah line, and connected it with the mines on
the West Coast. Thus it depends almost entirely on
metals for its living. Metals have been booming of
late, but are easing off now. This shows how the
boom has affected the co.:—

Accounts are now made up annually, and during the
past 12 months big changes have been brought about.
A year ago the directors promised to hand out 40,000
C ? n r , lb V.V Ilg , s^ares > P a >d to 10s. in proportion to

more new capital has been got in, presumably in hard
cash, bringing the total from £129,824 to £173,380
Although about £30,000 of this new capital has
been put into mains, etc., the co. has not yet felt
any benefit; but next year’s figures should show
some improvement. The co. still has a large wad of
its capital not represented by any tangible assets—
the Purchase Account,” £35,258, the price paid to
the Brisbane Electric Supply Co. for the goodwill
°vu e ,, some ei &hUyears ago, and against
which there is a “Sinking Fund” of £7500. The
co. adds to this at the rate of £lOOO a year. As a
matter of fact, the alleged sinking fund is not much
more than an ordinary reserve, and the purpose
would be served just as well, beside giving a more
tidy appearance to the balance-sheet, if it were
me !' e y written off the Purchase Account each year
and the written-down amount shown. Amon» the
other reserve funds—there are quite a crowd of them
To.nr e v ! ’, Eeplacenient and Contingency Account,
£2520 which would not be much use if the co.
suddenly found itself called on to make any exten-
sive renewals, etc. The co.’s right to supply Bris-
bane with light expires in 1940, when the City Coun-
cil may buy it out on paying a reasonable sum for
everything but goodwill. If the council decides not
to do business the co. can go on for another 10
years, and so on for 10-year periods until the coun-
cil does decide that the people ought to have their
own electric light. It is perhaps with a view to
smothering up the fattening dividend rate, and thus
dodging hostile attention -that the issue of bonus
shares was made. 'The co.’s cash position is'strong.
Liabilities amount to £12,374 and liquid assets (in-
cluding stock) to £26,064, besides which there is a
considerable amount in freeholds.

The Life Insurance Co. of Australia :
Total Deficit,
xt t „ Out- including
Income. Expenses. Claims, goings. Goodwill.
1912. . £10,989 £18,918 £3715 £22,633 £19,644
Dec.,
1913.. 23,851 19,809 6350 26,159 21,952
This is a proprietary concern with a nominal capital
of £250,000. Of this, £71,500 is issued, and
£44,000 is paid up. Of the £44,000, . £14,952 is
represented by accumulated loss through the out-
goings in the early stages of - the business being
greater than the earnings, and £7OOO is set down
against the goodwill of a very mild little institu-
tion which the co. purchased—the National Provi-
dent Life Assurance Co. This leaves £22 048 of
paid capital intact. The purchase of the goodwill
of the National Provident Life Assurance Co. was
always something of a mystery. There is no sign
at all in the first balance-sheet of any assets trans-
ferred by the purchased concern. It certainly trans-
ferred its business, but the whole receipts that year
were £10,989, and as the new concern paid £18,918
of its own good money in order to earn that
£10,989, it surely earned most of it off its own bat.
Where, then, was the business or assets for which the
new co. paid the National Provident £BOOO in addi-
tion to taking over its liabilities? Somehow the
thing doesn’t look a very good bargain. If the new
concern earned less than £ll,OOO in its first year
by its own efforts and the aid of an established
business bought for £BOOO, and then in its second
year added nearly £13,000 to this (making the
total income almost £24,000) without buying any
expensive, ready-made business—well, it seemed to
get on a great deal better without buying ready-
made businesses.
Seeing that the new concern lost £11,G44 in its
first year (or part of a year) and only £2308 in
its second year (or rather 13 months), it is certainly
improving. But before its position is satisfactory
it has (1) to stop losing money; (2) to accumulate
£21,952 in order to wipe out the accumulated
deficit; (3) to accumulate a sufficient reserve to
cover its existing policy liabilities, whatever they
may amount to. Of the last year’s income £6875
was ordinary life premiums, and they certainly repre-
sent considerable of a liability. The industrial,
provident, sick-pay, accident and guarantee business
which represents the great bulk of the receipts pro-
bably stands for a very small amount in the way of
future commitments. These last constitute a scrappy
odd-and-end sort of trade that isn’t easy to define.
The assets —especially the £lO,OOO in Government
stock, £3500 in bank deposits, and £3004 in mort-
gages seem good enough. But however good they
are, no one is likely to suppose that there was, or is,
any need for the Life Insurance Co. of Australia.
A new proprietary company isn’t founded in this
much-insured country to fill a great, long-felt want.
It starts in the hope of finding dividends for its
shareholders and in the certainty of finding billets
for a while at least, for its staff. It sets another
crowd of people struggling in offices or clamoring at
householders’ doors to take the crust of business
out of the mouths of previous people in the same
line of crust-hunt, and consequently live people are
often racing after and trying to live upon the miser-
able little policy which can’t do more than support
one person. It is a sad, unproductive sort of enter-
prise, and the number of new companies that perish
by the wayside, and sell their bones for a trifle to
the next comer, is lamentable. And, meanwhile, Aus-
tralia is so short of bootmakers that the number of
boots has to import beggars description.

The reserves as at June 30, 1913, were as follows: —

General renewal fund 9,004
Meter renewal fund 1,621
Depreciation fund for works on
leasehold 2,800
Unappropriated profit 2,030

Whether a revaluation was lately made of the assets
does not transpire. If the writing-up was done as the
result of a revaluation, then the items should now
appear differently in the balance-sheet. They actu-
ally appear in the old form —as, for example,
“Plant on freehold, at cost, less depreciation.”
The leasehold property seems to have been bumped
up hardest. The only excuse is that the lease has
16 years to run, and that plant and buildings “remain
the property of the co. at the termination of the
lease,’ though if the lease were not renewed plant
and buildings would no doubt have to be dis-
mantled ; and that would take more than twopence
off their value. The co. may in past years have
been stuffing profits away into extensions, etc., nom-
inally as a set-off against depreciation; and in that
case the capitalisation of hidden reserves might be
justified; but there was no reason whatever to sus-
pect that any profit was being thus stuffed away.
On the contrary, it looked very much as though the
visible renewal funds were the only provision made
to cover wear and tear. In those circumstances the
capitalisation of reserves to such an extent that the
capital is doubled was unwarranted, on the evidence
available. No doubt the co. has been doing verv
well:—
Dec., 1908. Dec., 1913.
Liquid assets £4195 £8877
Liabilities 1112 4814
Surplus .. ..£3083 £4063
Thus, no new capital having been got in meantime,
the co. has out of profits (1) paid its dividend,
(2) paid for extensions and repairs and renewals, and
(3) in five years put by £9BO. Whether that allows
a fair thing for depreciation of plant, etc., depends
on the money spent on extensions; and the only
way we can judge of that is by noting that whereas
in the latter half of 1908 the revenue from gas-
sales, etc., was £4367, it was £lO,BOB last half-
year—a growth which suggests that the co. has
added pretty considerably to its mains, etc. As to
the dividend, the 6 p.c. on the doubled capital looks
easily earned; but it has to be noted that £l4OO
of the alleged surplus profit is absorbed by reserves
for depreciation, so it is doubtful whether the true
profit is very much in excess of the dividend now
paid.

* * * *
Water-Supply Engineers will be interested to learn
that further evidence proving the durability of W. I.
and Steel Pipes when coated by the Asphaltum pro-
cess, has just been obtained by the engineers of the
Melbourne and Metropolitan Board of Works. Certain
alterations were made to the main which supplies the
City of South Melbourne with water, and consequently
some pipes were taken up. These pipes were made in
the year 1889, and were coated with Asphaltum com-
position in the usual way, and they were laid in ground
that was particularly unfavorable to the preservation
of any unprotected metal, being originally swampy
land, containing much vegetable matter, and filled
with tidal dredgings. On examination, these 24-year-
old pipes were found to be in an excellent state of
preservation, and the Board’s engineers were so satis-
fied with their condition that they decided to use
them again. They were, therefore, removed to.
the works of Messrs. Mephan, Ferguson Proprietary
Limited, at Footscray, Victoria, where these 24-year-
old pipes were cleaned, retested, and recoated with-
Asphaltum composition in a new and improved pro-
cess. They are now being relaid in a position where-
they will have to withstand a working pressure of'
1351 b. to the square inch. That the Asphaltum pro-
cess for coating pipes is rust-proof and thoroughly
efficacious is proved by the foregoing.*
H.M.A.S. Australia, now in Australian waters,
is fitted entirely with Babcock and Wilcox Water-
tube Boilers, similar to the H.M.S. New Zea
land and other Dreadnoughts recently built for the
Admiralty.*

* * * *
Lord Roberts, Sailor’s Falls (Vic.), is
the fortunate possessor of a 10ft. lode at No. 1 level,
but it doesn't boast of more than fair gold. This
does not spell good stuff in Victoria. No. 2 level
seems to be as poverty-stricken as little Bobs,
though that co. claims a 15ft. formation. Some
odd reasoning goes on over this show. Because a
leg is met in a crosscut, and because some decent
prospects have been got from surfacing 300 ft. to
the north, the cutting of the leg is a good develop-
ment. Talk of this kind is better suited to the
green-room than the Stock Exchange, yet it gets a
hearing.
* * * *
The Hercules, Rosebery (Tas.), has been
a smudge ever since Blakemore cut down the ore
reserves to 110,000 tons, and since Weinberg didn’t
float it on the Continent of Europe. The assays of
the cores read well, but diamond drills have a nasty
knack of representing the best, and not the average,
of the lode. If the cores are put on the course of
the shoot the drill very often follows the line of
least resistance, and so picks up the rich and friable
ore. Klug and Rutter Clarke seem to have been
glancing round Rosebery, but haven’t told the world
whether they think it might dip into the Ilerculcs-
Rosebery-Tasmanian copper pool. Meanwhile, 8., M.
and Co.’s satellites are converging in nor’-west Tas-
mania. After a wait of 20 years, that district may
have another flutter on the strength of the Howard
Mineral Separation processes.
* * * *
Mount Lyell (Tas.) hadn’t done any de-
velopment since the fire till it started out to tell of
what is happening in the Comstock lease. There a
bore or two, or, maybe, a drive has got into some
good ore over the North Lyell boundary, and there
has been, or is to be, a second advent of Bowes
Kelly and Colin Templeton in Queenstown to measure
up the make of bornite. What the bores fail to
prove is the underlie of the shoot of ore. The pitch
is to the north, and, consequently, into the Comstock
lease. But the pitch and underlie are very differ-
ent matters, for the latter carries an ore body holus-
bolus one way or the other. If the Mount Lyell
plans are to be relied on, the pitch of the North
Lyell shoots are to the east, and it is the absence
of any data as to the underlie —not as to the pitch—
that makes all the press puffs about the North Lyell-
Comstoek development very flimsy.
* * * *
The South German’s (Vic.) miserable
crushing smote the share market very hard. Yet
Maldon revives the old yarn of much gold
IT j the mineral. But there is very little profit left
after treatment charges and other deductions are
met, and whimpering won’t stop calls. The Bulletin
tlr through has insisted that these had to come if
the Baxter shoot of stone didn’t live down to the
bottom, which it didn’t.
* * * *
The Tasmania, Beaconsfield (Tas.). was
thought bv John Taylor and Son and Frecheville, the
big gold expert, to be a mining plum when they
took it over from the old Launceston Co. nearly a
decade back. Over £600,000 in divs. had come out
it , le re^ P°t and a big shoot of gold showed in
the bottom of the east and west lode. So it seemed
.at if a pump big enough to beat the water was
installed, the reward would be a bag full of shekels.
About a quarter of a million has gone in this little
job and in opening up some fresh lifts, but all that
has been learnt is that when Launceston sold its big
wad of paid-up shares it ratted out at the most
critical moment. Manager Heathcote told London
long since _ that erratic and shrinking values and
high pumping cost made things look gloomy. Now
Arthur Llewellyn, of John Taylor and Son’s staff,
adds as a third factor the uplift in wages. So he
won t let London sink another lift to try its luck
deeper down. The remaining ore will be beaten out
and the residues on the surface treated, after which
Beaconsfield will presumably become an orchard
patch.

The bottom of the big paddock cleaned up
by the Pioneer, Bradshaw’s Creek (Tas.), yielded
well and raised the total that this mine has rendered
up to 5046* tons of stream tin. To get this 6,106,500
c-üb. yards of drift have been sluiced. The average
all over has been 1.851 b. stream tin per cub. yard,
whereas last six months the average was 1.791 b.
Costs are bedrock at 5Jd. The next paddock will be
deeper, but there won’t be room for Cecil Ryan to
whittle down expenses much more. As side 'shows,
he has the South Pioneer lease and the Argonaut
somewhere on the East Coast. The latter is a shallow
strip of good wash amid a body of worked ground
that was rich in its day.
