North Mount Lyell has bolted. These are
the published results from a borehole from No. 1
drive at the 1100 ft. level:—
12*ft. averaging 10.5 p.c. copper.
15ft. averaging 15.2 p.c. copper.
10ft. averaging 17.8 p.c. copper.
2ft. averaging 4.6 p.c. copper.
The borehole followed the northern extension of No.
Tl7l. ’ 100 ft. over the Comstock boundary.
'• hether it ended in country is left in doubt. The
North Lyell workings are steadily eating into the
Comstock ground, and rich ore is being won. No.
22 stope, 850 ft. level, is also reported to be gouging
out something above, the ordinary from the Comstock
end. The ore-body, into which the workings are now
advancing, is not the same as that worked by the
former Comstock co. This latter is over a mile away
to the north, and is being probed into to give some
information as to what is best to do with it. Like
the North Lyell, it is siliceous, but is only half as
rich in copper. Open cutting and flotation, however,
may do the trick. The Mount Lyell Co. is in a pretty
solid position, and reports the mines to be in first-
rate trim for a large output. With plenty of labor
available, the costs should again become reasonable.
* * * *
The alleged discovery of the Mount
Morgan lode in the Mount Morgan Extended’s ground
is interesting even if values are not high. The
point to be decided is whether payable ore will be
found in quantity. According' to the map of Mount
Morgan by J M Newman and A. F. Campbell-
Brown, the material being mined by Mount Morgan
is an enriched pipe in a much larger mass of miner-
g*?s®- wh ich is really the lode. The
Extended Co. has only a small corner of the mineral-
ised area lying between the big co.’s holding and
i! e -,? n .^ e west. The section also
shows the granite dipping towards the Mount Morgan
V°:,, s . * ea £ s e It is well known that diamond-
drilling by the Extended Co. penetrated this granite.
Ihe question therefore resolves itself into the pro-
bability of finding a second enriched pipe in the
Extended Co.’s small block of mineralised ground.
The geological work referred to above suggests that
the Mount Morgan pipe gets poor or cuts out at a
comparatively shallow depth, and secondary enrich-
ment is suggested as the explanation of the rich
ore. On the other hand, there seems ample room
for further enriched pipes in the Mount Morgan
Co.’s ground north of the Extended’s holding, though
the diamond-drilling recently done did not find
much.
•.<■***
By this time Mount Morgan has very few
bosses of the Richard regime left. The last two to go are
Dibdin and White, both Queenslanders. Dibdin has
been with the co. for 24 years, and worked his way
up to be chief engineer. But, of course, he didn’t
know as much about Mount Morgan as an imported
Yankee named Garred, so he was asked to take
a back seat. He packed up and left. The
other man, White, bossed the concentrating plant.
He got the shortest notice to quit of the lot —he
dropped dead at the mine.
The closing down of Great Fitzroy (Q.)
draws attention once again to the question of the
efficacy of flotation processes for copper ores. The
class of ore for which a cheap process is required is
one that would provide big tonnagps—being worth
about 40s. per ton (24 p.c. copper and 24dwt. gold)
and containing 00 to 60 or 65 p.c. of silica. The
cost of “fluxing” these ores is high, the mixture after
fluxing gives a big lot of material to be smelted, and
the slag losses in smelting are very high. Lyell
got over the difficulty so long as the “flux” ore from
the parent mine contained sufficient copper, gold and
silver to counteract those objections. Mount Morgan
hoped to do the same by using the Many Peaks flux
ore. Fitzroy has been struggling to make ends meet
by using expensive siliceous ironstone flux contain-
ing no valuable metals. This fact is hardly compati-
ble with the “gag” that the flotation process gives
a product with a high flux value. It might do so
if the ore were ground fine enough, but there are
objections to this procedure: (1) the cost of grind-
ing everything, as compared with taking out a big
slice in a “wet” mill after a preliminary crushing,
(2) the royalty, (3) the gold loss. Then there is
the problem of smelting the fine concentrates. Fitz-
roy installed the Dwiglit-Lloyd 'sintering process,
but experience has generally proved that double-
treatment is required in these machines—which costs
money. Besides all this, smelting losses, say 13
p.c. of the copper, must be added. So one is forced
to the conclusion that a combined concentration and
smelting recovery of 75 p.c. all round would be
pretty good work. On a 2.5 p.c. copper ore 75 p.c.
is 1.9. In other words, it takes 53 tons to make a
ton of copper, and mining, concentration (including
royalty) and smelting, etc., would cost £7O to £BO
per ton of copper. Also, 75 p.c. of 40s. is 305., so
53 tons of 40s. ore would yield £BO. The profit is,
therefore, problematic.
Fitzroy, however, has the Laloki (Papua) string
to its bow. This ore is worth about 60s. per ton, is
a perfect flux —containing only 5 p.c. of silica —and
won’t cost more than 40s. in the bins. Roughly,
every ton of Laloki ore it uses will give it £1 pro-
fit. It will probably use a ton for every ton of its
own ore it smelts.
* * * *
The Beehive Tin Mining Co., whose
property at Red Rock, near Stanthorpe (Queens-
land) promised so well, has been unable, so far, to
market its wealth on account of the lack of water
for sluicing. The plant has been ready for a couple
of months, and the tin is undoubtedly there in large
quantities, but their luck has been out during the
recent Queensland rainfall —the rain has never once
hit the mine in sufficient quantities. Even with a
good wet season, however, the problem does not
appear to be solved, for it has yet to be proved that
the existing dam Will hold water —there has been
no opportunity of a thorough test. A fair temporary
rainfall would give some of the surrounding shows a
chance to develop, and the outcome might be a
combined effort to draw water from the permanent
Severn River—three miles direct —and this would
appear to be the only satisfactory solution of the
difficulty. It is a wonder that some combined effort
has not been inaugurated earlier; it should pay. The
most interesting belt of country close to Red Rock
seems to be capable of rising to the occasion in
common with quite a number of Queensland mineral
areas. In connection with the latest discovery is molyb-
denite. This particular show is well spoken of, and
forms part of a small area of the belt, from which tin
and wolfram (Lord Nolan Co.) and copper, zinc,
lead and silver ores (Silver Queen Co.) have been
worked on a small scale, and there is still plenty
of room for prospecting work.
* * * *
The coal output for Queensland last year
topped 1,000,000 tons for the first time, the indivi-
dual increases varying from 11 p.c. at Ipswich to
40 p.c. at Clermont. In the latter field the Blair
Athol mine, with its immense seam, increased its
output to over 93,000 tons, worth 7s. 7d. at the
pit mouth. The 230 miles of railway to port dis-
counts what should otherwise be the largest producer
in the State. The backward feature of the industry
appears to be a lack of enterprise in coke making.
A little is produced in the Ipswich field, but only
the simplest propositions are tackled, whereas by
judicious amalgamation of coals and up-to-date plant,
much profitable advance could be made. A little
forethought might have secured the smelter supply
for Cloncurry and Mount Morgan, the latter of
which was recently driven to make a five years’ con-
tract with the Illawarra (N.S.W.) ovens.
* * * *
Gyrupie (Queensland) is hoping that a
good find of slate country above the bottom level of
Amalgamated Monkland and Glanmire Deeps will
enliven things. The slate is expected 30ft. above
the level, so it will soon be known whether the
Great Eastern reef will pay in the deeps. It is
only a gamble. No. 3 North Columbia surprised
everyone by crushing 305 tons for 388 oz. gold and
No. 2 Great Eastern crushed 1455 tons from the
Inglewood reef above 2000 ft. for 3040 z. and 66 tons
for 2550 z. from a leader at the 1320 ft. level; total,
5590 z. gold. The average from the Inglewood—
a 6ft. reef —is over 4dwt., and should prove payable,
for the Scottish Gympie pays dividends from sdwt.
stone.
* * * *
Hampden-Cloncurry (Q.) has been deep-
ening, its No. 1 shaft whenever water troubles per-
mitted. Last month it started driving north and
south on a pyritic ore body at 500 ft. A crosscut
was also put out east, and latest report is the dis-
covery of 3ft. of cellular pyrites, carrying subsulphide
of copper, and assaying 5 p.c. The co. has hopes that
the find will turn out to be the East lode, which
was the object of No. 1 shaft’s journey. Earlier
developments at No. 2 shaft showed that this lode,
though patchy, was going stronger than was ex-
pected. The co. urgently needs more basic ore, and
is in stern chase of it.
* * * *
Mount Cannindah Copper Mines (Queens-
land), that shocking old disappointment or sham
which had so many wonderful indications in the air
and such mountains of rich ore in perdition, has
produced another annual balance-sheet —the eighth.
Revenue for the year, £l3. Expenses, including
depreciation, £263. Loss, £250. Cash remaining
on hand, £3B. Accumulated shortage, £7975. Divi-
dends, nil. Hopes, apparently nil. Reasons for
keeping the old thing out of the knacker’s yard,
seemingly nil.
The report of the Queensland Under-Secre-
tary for Mines for 1913 is interesting, but not alto-
gether cheering. The decline in the gold production
( £849,210) brings the State into line with the
rest of Australia, and the position of the industrial
metals is summarised thus: “It is in coal alone that
any pronounced and satisfactory advance is apparent.”
A geological survey staff is attached to the Mines
Department, and does excellent work in compiling
statistics and reporting on any new find, but it
would require a keen search of the records to find
survey reports that would encourage the ordinary
investor to take a copper out of his pocket. It
cannot be that all are duffers. So why this overdone
policy of reservation? The staff may have an enthusi-
astic personal opinion of a new show, but the lugubri-
ous policy apparently will not allow them to say so iti
Government publications. There is always some
boulder to get behind in case things go wrong, and
the “private” expert who is inclined to take a risk
is doubted in consequence. So it is not altogether
the “opportunities in other directions” for investments
that is responsible for the almost entire absence of
business in mining stock in local exchanges. There
must be some enthusiasm emanating from head-
quarters.
* * * *
Efforts are being made to realise on some
of the belongings of the defunct Westralian Gold-
fields Ltd. This delusion was first introduced to the
London public in the beginning of 1911, when sub-
scriptions were invited for scrip in a co. of 750,000
£1 shares. The vendors and promoters’ cut was
fixed at £20,000 in cash, £130,000 in cash or paid-
up preference shares, and 375,000 ordinary shares.
For this consideration the vendors, promoters, etc.,
agreed to part with the right, title and interest in a
series of derelicts scattered over the face of the
West, but as only a few thousand shares were applied
for, the flotation was virtually a failure. Later on
£15,000 worth of first mortgage debentures were
taken up, but by whom is not known. A state-
ment of the position of the co. at the end of 1912
is given as a warning to speculators to look twice
before they leap frequently. W.A. expenditure,
£4897 ; London, £3147 : mine development, £1532 ;
preliminary expenses, £11,750; debenture issue
expenditure, £5091; cash, £6023; creditors,
£3727. Thus out of a total outlay of £30,144,
only £1532 was expended in development. Con-
sidering the shows acquired by the co. even that was
waste of money.

Latest published opinions of Tindals
(W.A.) : “The lode is at least 10ft. wide at No. 3
level, and as it has been proved in places to be 40ft.,
there is no reason why this thickness should not
prove to be the average... .Over 300 ft. of exposed
lode; values are in and out; they range from l-|dwt.
to Boz., the average indicating it to be a mine of
greater intrinsic worth than the South Kalgurli or
Perseverance... .If it was located on the Golden Mile
it would be boomed sky high for its potential
value.... The mine has great possibilities, and if
only an additional £25,000 could be found (etc.,
etc.) it would in two years be one of the great
mines of W.A.” It seems a pity to spoil the effect
of such a pleasing picture; but still, in justice to the
mine itself and those who are working it, it must
be said again that at best it is only a sdwt. show, and
that, like most other Coolgardie mines, the ground
is hard and the gold shoots short, and as they dip
rapidly, mueh money has to be spent in looking for
payable ore at succeeding levels.
The Melbourne-financed Royal Oak Co.
(W.A.) has just taken 200 oz. out of its Black Range
show, and is getting ready for a desperate attempt
to recover some of the money which has been put
into its particular hole in the ground. Several diffi-
culties have had to be contended with. First of all,
“spewy” ground upset the shaft, and for a time the
workings were lost; then _ the water proved trouble-
some, and when the co. did manage to get a bit of
crushing dirt together the State mill jibbed, and was
unable to treat it. The outlook for shareholders isn’t
very rosy, as the mine is small and not over-rich.
* * * *
Whim Well Copper (W.A.) has closed
down its Murex plant on account of dry weather.
Meantime, there is to be a conference between the
directors and the expert in charge. A circular to
shareholders claims that the process has demonstrated
its ability to concentrate the carbonate ore from the
mine, which is pretty ancient history. But the direc-
tors say that they haven’t yet had time to make up
their minds regarding profits. The circular adds that
the majority of the unissued debentures have been
subscribed. This may indicate a lot—in two quite
different ways.
Burbanks Main Lode (W.A.) has come
a cropper. Some time ago the output was increased
in order to pay for machinery out of gold won.
This proved too much for the better class of re-
serves, and more capital is now wanted to open up
a fresh lot. Meantime all hands have been given
the walking ticket, and the co. is making prepara-
tions for another reconstruction.
South Kalgurli (W.A.) has had a bit of
a turn up in its Hainault lease. At the 1000 ft.
level the south drive has been extended 23ft. in ore
worth 425. 6d., and an additional 10ft. in stuff aver-
aging a shade under 275. Bore holes put into the
walls show the shoot to be of big width in places
and to assay better than in the drive. Good pros-
pects are also being met with at 875 ft. in the same
lease. There a crosscut from a drive has passed
through a big body of ore which assays up to 60s.
per ton. Manager John Morgan, who has been rest-
ing in Germany for some time, has retired on account
of ill-health, and entered the employ of the Chaffers
—which is partly run by the same crowd —as con-
sulting engineer.
* * * *
Grave doubts as to the future of the Asso-
ciated Northern’s Victorious leases are expressed by
Professor Liveing in his report to the London
Board. The Professor states that the extremely
patchy nature of the values and the irregular width
of the pay ore make even an approximate estimate of
sulphide reserves quite impossible, and he advises
that more development should be done before
a sulphide plant is ordered. This will take at least
eight months to install, and as the co. has run out
of payable oxidised ore, it looks as if no revenue
will be forthcoming from the mine in the interim.
With regard to the upper levels of the property
and the manager’s original estimate of 200,000 tons
of 20s. ore, the Professor says that it is now quite
clear that the grade was slightly over-estimated. He
admits that with a show like the Victorious the
difficulty of sampling is great, and with ore of this
grade the slightest error makes a serious difference.
In the co.’s Iron Duke (Kalgoorlie) lease one party
of tributors unearthed a good patch of ore where the
co. put up two rises, and failed to find anything.
But nearly all the other tributors have had to scratch
pretty hard for a living. The prospect of finding a
new shoot in the mine is considered by the Pro-
fessor to be too remote to warrant further outlay.

Waihi (M.L.) paid the usual shilling for
the quarter, but slumps again, for it is pretty Obvious
that, taking the February return (£25,520 from

£10,479,294 worth of gold and silver, but the

A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sidney, March 23, 1914.
My Dear Arini, —
News of tlie approaching Sydney citizens’
banquet, as a farewell to G.-G. Denman, will
be received with hostile grunts of dissatisfac-
tion from the average citizeness. She will
only be permitted (perhaps) to be present in
small and carefully-selected numbers.
Herded together in a balcony the citizeness
will yawn behind a heavy portcullis of flower-
ing or densely leafy foliage. Hour after
hour will drag its weary way across the por-
tals of the night. The average male feeder
will make the average after-banquet speech.
He will say lie is glad to be there. He will
say that it is the happiest moment of his
life, only tinged with regret. He will remark
that no doubt the G.-G. is sorry to leave.
Processions of food will be brought from some
culinary tabernacle. Soup will be more
numerous than autumn leaves, and the rest
of the bill of fare quite as interminable as
the Ancient Mariner. Meanwhile the unfed
citizeness (if graciously permitted to look
on), will gradually sink into a troubled sleep
behind the festoons thoughtfully erected to
bide her presence and her lack of refresh-
ment. The Denmans were popular with the
multitude during their brief reign in the old
Macquarie-street house. The multitude
ought to be allowed to take a hand in some
more general form of farewell than the joy
of reading (in next morning’s papers) that
Alderman Smithjones unfortunately overset
the cruet.
That long, delicate-looking streak of the
Peerage, Earl Grey, is going to stalk the
stalactites in Jenolan Caves at an early
date. The three Greys, mother, father and
daughter, will, be assisted in this mission by
Governor Strickland and garnished by an
A.D.C., who will hold the umbrella up be-
tween them and the sun. They’ll be up with
the morning magpie to catch a special train
at 7 a.m. or thereabouts. This bounces them
into the Mount Victoria station while the
Day is young and the hair of Morn is still
in its curl-papers. The party will then be
whisked off to do a certain number of caves
before its component parts are caught and
fed at midday. After taking in refreshments
they will renew their underground explora-
tions, as if they were moles or gnomes, till
they, are again caught, put into cars, and
flurried off to the train. Later the team of
Greys goes westward by train, towards the
flushed countenance of the setting sun.
Ice creams fainted quietly away on small
slabs of crockery while Mrs. Premier Hol-
man’s farewell tea-party to Mrs. Martin
Donohoe drifted round a shady nook of Par-
liament House. Mrs. Percy Hunter, who
was to have had half the goodbye cake, was
not well enough to be present. So the
blithe Mrs. Donohoe, and her saucy dimples,
and her French hat, and her Australian blue
frock, had the floor to herself. Mrs. Holman
rounded up her guests with considerable tact.
The wild scramble for foocL. which is usu-
ally a feature of these alleged festivities, was
missing,, and the party was gently but firmly
put in its place. The guests were neatly
labelled and headed off to the small round
tables at which they were to sit and fortify
themselves with much and innumerable Buii.
In the steamy warmth of last week, a wilder-
noss of Bun was only an added burden to
the flesh, and the evanescent ice cream was
eagerly chased to its last drop.
Mrs. Clunies-Ross (some day I’ll call her
o', 1 - ace or Sevres china) and Mrs
Schmidlin, sisters of the guest of honor at
lr’ s e^oe P al "hy, were distinctly present.
Mrs. Barraclougfl, a cousin of these sisters
three, smiled serenely at a table where two
members of the fourth estate munched the
cake of peace, with Mr. Clunies-lioss as
referee in ease of accidents. Miss Mabel
Walker, an amiable blonde, was another
non-combatant at this board. Mrs. Trefle
tossed some handsome mulligatawny feathers
at a higher altitude of the feast, where Rose
Scott, Ethel Turner Curlewis and other star
guests were.enthroned beside the View. The
view at this end of Parliament House is
mostly lawn. (By squinting hard it is just
possible to look round one corner of the
View and see a politician playing bowls.)
Mr. J. D. Fitzgerald was one of three or
four male persons. Their serious sac suits
gave a ballast to the dressing, which was
summery and frivolous. Mr. Fitzgerald had
a Town-Planni'ng expression in his eye.
Premier Holman looked in at the Madge
Donohoe farewell.
Bondi-Waverley made glad and joyful
noises with a brass band on Saturday after-
noon. The event was the opening' of the
new School of Arts in those hyphenated sub-
urbs. Some of the inhabitants stood on the
footpath to drink in the music of the brazen
instruments, while as many more as could
summon admission dockets to their aid
passed round to a sandy backyard, or School
of Arts hinterland. Here they gripped a
chair and sat down to wait patiently the
arrival of the local nobility and gentry and
politicians who were to reign in great maj-
esty on the platform. Premier Holman, who
was as late as two-tliirds of the company,
was handed a gold key to unlock the front
door of the premises. This key, by the wish
of the committee, is to be forged or ham-
mered into a brooch for Mrs. Premier Hol-
man. (The useless foundation-stone trowel,
by the way, might also be melted down into
some kind of gaud.)
President Loewenthal, who beamed behind
a large, blue committee tag, was on his legs
during the greater part of the afternoon. I
forget the exact number of speeches in which
he thanked nearly every man, woman, child
and dog in Waverley-Bondi for the noble
part they had played in rearing the “mag-
nificent building that stood before them” (it
couldn’t run away, of course, and it couldn’t
stand behind them, unless they turned
round). Finally, in his 43rd round, he
breathed the wish that he could write
the name “Woman” in electric signs or some-
thing across the sky. (Wouldn’t this upset
the Navigation ActV) The resonant voice of
Holman made a few brief remarks and an
allusion to the pastime of making £5OOO out
of nothing—as the committee appeared to
have done, with the help of a mortgage. D.
Levy, M.L.A., was mistaken for Albert Goldie
at this ceremony, by the way. In a dash
of orators for the front, Macarthur Onslow,
M.L.A., as member for the School of Arts—
I mean the district—dredged a mild speech,
presumably from the depths of his trousers
pockets, with both hands, to tell the N.S.W.
Premier how delighted he was to see him.
Naturally, he would be.
Sydney’s Mayoress, Mrs. R. W. Richards,
is president of the Olympic Ball Committee.
This august body meets in council in the
Town Hall on March 26. The object, of
course, is to get cash. Said cash is to be
added to the £lO.OOO required for the exhibi-
tion of Australian brawn and muscle at
Berlin in 1916.
Mr. Scott, Premier of a spot called Sas-
katchewan (Canada), is at present exchang-
ing ideas with the local politician. Sas-
katchewan is a frozen place, and got its
name owing to the sound the inhabitant
made when he acquired a cold, which was
always, or even more numerous than that.
The benefit matinge last week in aid of
the Royal Hospital for Women and the
Renwick Ditto for Sick Children is one of
a cycle that is trying to bury a debt of
£12,000. It is a big try, and the local
mummer is nobly doing his bit, and a good
deal over, to help the Cause. The last out-
break occurred on a Turkish-bath afternoon
in the Tivoli, which the H. D. Mclntosh
management lent for the purpose. A Town
Hall contingent simmered gently in front of
the d.c. (even a Lady Mayoress has to enjoy
herself in the perspiration of her brow),
and an infant with large and healthy lungs
wept loudly and often in one of the boxes.
At the top of its voice it said that all was
vanity and vexation of baby.
The Spencer Bruntons’ dance the other
evening was a small, informal affair to cele-
brate daughter Evelyn’s birthdav. There
were no programmes, no bores, and lots of
carefully-edited Tango and ragtime. The big
ballroom at Elystan swallowed the handful
of joyous revellers till about 1-2 of the clock.
Pale pink—the flush-of-dawn tint that meets
the unappreciative glance of the early milk-
man —-was much favored by some of the
nicest-looking young persons present. Miss
Sylvia Purves, who has been recently gad-
ding in America with mamma, wore one of
the pink witcheries in the material that
looks like a slice of rose-tinted cloud. Miss
Evelyn Brunton was another flush-of-dawn-
gowned damsel, and handsome Ad&le Bren-
nan floated round in gossamer draperies in
the same distracting shade. Dorothy King’s
white frock had a pale blue bodice' (if you
can call these airy-fairy draperies by that
solid mid-Victorian title), which is said to
be the last drowning gasp in style. The
Bruntons have sent forth invitations for a
big dance on April 2—the hop the other even-
ing being a mere snack of Tango.
Commander Simpson-Baikie, R.N.R., of
the St. Albans, recently acquired the Royal
Naval Reserve decoration. The British
Admiral on the China station handed over
this bauble to the popular sailor.
The departure of our recent visitor from
U.S., Dr. Starr Jordan, of Stanford Uni.,
leaves another gap in the trail of Peace
Lecturers. The ’Murlcan disciple of ami-
able arbitration is going to look in at a
British Conference on that belligerent sub-
ject, and intends to examine the same argu-
ments in Berlin before he again puts on or
resumes liis. mortar board in California.
Lut Berlin just now is quiet and homelike,
and is eating its sausage in a mild spirit. An
apostle of peace has chances in Tripoli, where
the strife still lingers; in the Balkans,
tor the war is still alive thereabouts; in
Albania, which is welcoming its new King
with a gun; and in Belfast. It is up to
some peace apostle to flee to Belfast and see
Ed. Carson. The man who goes sneaking on
a mission of tranquillity to Berlin to-day is
carrying coals to Newcastle, with a safe-
conduct and a military escort.
The British-Australasia n has tracked
H. Wright, of the Mitchell Library (Syd-
ney) to his London lair, and remarks that
he is collecting anything handy in the shape
of early Australasian history he can put
his hands on. The same authority mentions
the name of Dr. Georgina Sweet (Mel-
bourne) as busily engaged pursuing nodules
in beef. These wild animals will lead the
Australian a dance through Europe, Asia
and America on behalf of the Common-
wealth Government. Nodules may be of
any size provided they are not larger than
the beef, and their presence indicates some-
thing or other which I have unfortunately
mislaid, but I believe it is serious and I
know it is very difficult to spell.
Harold Colonna, referred to as “the young
Australian tenor,” was married in London
during the early part of February, The
weddinv was as quiet as the Paddington
Registry Office could keep it. “The bride,”
says Phyllis, “is the last of an old and
wealthy family. She is Miss Cynthia (etc.)
King, the daughter of the late Walter George
King, of Loxwood, Sussex. She is in her
very early twenties.” One hopes, as young
tenors are not usually capitalists, that Mrs.
Harold Colonna has some of the savings of
her old and wealthy family tucked away in a
silk stocking, or a tank or two.
In these days when the high cost of living
is served up with the suburban ham and
eggs every morning, the Vancouver boats do
a very fair trade in carrying Australian
manufacturers over to the United States to
find out how they manage to pay much higher
wages there and still become millionaires. The
Makura on its last trip carried two of a
trade —manufacturer McNeilage, who makes
bottles in three or four States, and Emil
Resch, one of the numerous Reschs who fill
them. Both of them are higli-wage men by
inclination, and they want to know how the
thing can be managed. After “doing”
the U. S. they go on to Europe. And the
Niagara, the next of the fine Union boats
on that line, is already booked almost to the
hatches.
The day or so before Emil Resch left by
the Makura, eldest brother Edmund re-
turned to Sydney from Europe. Thirty
years or so ago Edmund landed in Australia
and presently started to brew beer at Wil-
cannia—where, a little later, Thomas Heney,
now editor of the S. M. Herald, started to
brew leading articles. Presently Silverton
“broke out,” and the Reschs began to coin
more money out of the vigorous thirst of the
silver miners. In a few years Edmund
came to Sydney, and bought a brewery or
two, one of them for an old song. Inci-
dentally he bargained for Swifts, the
elaborate harbor-side residence of the
Tooth, and bought the forty-odd thousand
pound white elephant for under £15,000.
Other Reschs set up breweries elsewhere —
one is at Cootamundra and another at
Broken Hill—and when the big combine
was arranged in Melbourne, Emil, the young-
est of the first generation, took a hand in
the subsequent proceedings.
Artist Florence Rodway, just back from
Tasmania, via Melbourne, is wearing her
peach-bloom complexion in its accustomed
place. She buttons on her ovterall and
settles down to work this week.
The N.S.W. Benevolent Society flaps its
ancient wings over the charities just men-
tioned, and president Neville Maymdn is
goading any soft-hearted persons he can
catch to go on doing things to exterminate
the able-bodied debt already indexed. Mrs.
Mayman sheltered in a box with Mrs. Hol-
man. An army of raiders looted every man,
woman and child who had a threepenny-bit
hidden away to take them home, and the
threepenny went to the cause, and the donors
walked home. Honorary treasurer Mrs. Mal-
colm Stephen and Mrs. J. E. Paul and hon.
secs. Mrs. Felix Booth and Mrs. Louis
Packer are possibly bailing out the profits
as I write. With all odds and ends and
corners scraped out they ought to make about
£5OO.
Mrs. Harry Rickards is moving off the
edge of this continent shortly, on her annual
flitting to Europe. Only a small percentage
of women can count more strenuous careers
than this once extraordinarily busy woman,
whose judgment in the vaudeville business
is said to have largely built the Rickards cir-
cuit in Australia. I fancy she misses the
bustle of overlooking the old show turns, and
just keeps moving on to make her thoughts
circulate. A woman who travels much can
always reckon on being pretty busy losing
some of her luggage now and again, or miss-
ing her trains, or catching cold, or mislaying
her keys and her thimble. And she can’t
feel lonesome when she is interviewing foreign
Customs officers.
Soprano Lynn Mills is included in the
migration epidemic that attacks a good many
of our musical folk at this time of year.
The plump little warbler, who is a daughter
of Mr. J. Y. Mills, will give her farewell chir-
rup in St. James’s Hall. March 30 is the
date.
Baritone Herbert Ratcliffe leaves shortly
for London, via a stop over in Melbourne.
In the southern city he is going to sing at an
Oratorio Festival on Good Friday night.
Ella Caspers, the Goulburn contralto, is
returning to tour her native land. Her first
concert in Sydney is to occur in May. After
a hurried scramble through a few Common-
wealth Town Halls the singer rushes back
to Europe. There she is engaged as the only
assisting artist for a tour with fiddler
Ivreisler.
Pianist Gertrude Palmer, A.R.A.M.,
tripped off to the other side in the Tahiti
on Saturday last.
Mrs. Percy Hunter, whose health has
again fallen away from Satisfactory, is
booked for England, with her two boys, by
the Ascanius. The Blue Funnel craft leaves
on March 28.
Director George Wright, one of the com-
mercial tetrarchs of Farmer and Co., was
included in last week’s exodus to the other
side of the globe. Mailboats and general
passenger craft have been laden this month
with wandering Australians, who time their
departure to catch the British summer (a
frail and fleeting wraith of sunshine, which
suggests that someone has struck a match in
a fog.) So there must be a trace of super-
fluous cash still left in this State.
James C. Bendrodt, who used to stand at
the top of the marble stcns of the Imperial
Rink (foot of William-street), to gently push
the pushes off the select sheep track, is again
in the old address. This time he is manag-
ing director of a Dance Academy, under the
Imperial shingle, with Geo. C. Irving as
general, manager, and a pot of money behind
them. The classic shade of the immortal
Turveydrop—the D ncing one, not the De-
portment one —shot: 1 writhe in the ghost of
a russet-green frock coat, at the heights of
luxury by which ti e modern dance assembly
lures the fleet of foot. The Imperial man-
agement has been spending over £2OOO on
re-decorating and re-furnishing the ex-Rink
building. Both the dancing and the dancers
will be rigorously censored. No blush will
burst through the face powder of the Old
Person, and He or She who doesn’t Tango in
the Right Way will be escorted to the marble
steps already mentioned and cast headlong
over the battlements of Paradise, same as
Mephistopheles, who was never again quite
sound on that foot. This haughty palace of
the hop will open its portals about the second
week in April.
The skeleton recently discovered in Ger-
man East Africa, with something like 150,000
years behind it, casts no new light on the
fashions of that day. Possibly no anthro-
pologist would demean himself so far as to
inquire into the style in split skirts at the
time when these old bones were clad in
flesh, and, possibly, grass kilts. And what
good if he did inquire? Adam’s vertebra re-
main, if they have been interred in preserva-
tive circumstances, likewise the top of Tubal
Cain’s stone axe (the handle, alas! is gone),
and Abraham’s rusted twmpence. But the
old hats and dress suits are dust, and the
bonnets have fled, and the high-heeled boots
have taken to themselves wings. It seems
to me that a corset from before the Deluge
would throw more light on the battered old
facts of history than all the bones of B.C.
The recent squabble in the M.L. river-
side town of TV anganui, re rival carnival
beauties, proves that the referee in these
affairs must have a mighty uneasy time. The
report of the Wanganui struggle is hard to
follow in cold print. The ballot for the
appointment of a “Queen” of the carnival
festivities was taken by ticket. It was a
thrifty proceeding, for the voters paid for
their tickets. A Miss McDonald won by a
majority that apparently wiped out the
hopes of any other competitor. Next day
came the whirling news that a lady, who is
described as a Maori, one Mrs. McGregor,
“had not been credited with £5O worth of
votes.” In consequence, she was promptly
crowned queen of the carnival. Then the
other ex-imperial dame’s friends and sup-
porters plunged into the battle. The can-
nons were loaded, and the dogs of war let
loose., and someone shouted “havock” (with
a “k”), and. the fresh-water horse marine
girded on bis armor. The Telegraph’s
M.L. correspondent concludes by saying that
the affair has caused “much feeling.” That’s
the stuff that makes one Perfect Lady long
to smack the face of another Perfect Lady.
Merchant James Craig, of the big Auck-
land firm of J. J. Craig and Co., is passing
this way. on the long, wet track to London.
The Craig firm dabbles extensively in ship-
ping, and owns some sailing craft that trade
(or used to) between this side and the Domi-
nion. J. Craig, who is reputed to be one of
M.L.’s most prosperous citizens, will be able
to tell the astounded low-wage Britisher that
rich folk are plentiful in high-wage Austral-
asia.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.

is a young inan of amorous propensities, always on the lookout for adventure, and in his cruises

“South Sea Shipmates, 9 * By JOHN ARTHUR BARRY.
Very few old salts knew the South Seas better than Captain Barry, and few could equal him in the
spinning of yarns. The yarns contained in this book are personal experiences, bearing the impress
of truth on the face of them, and therein lies their value and their virtue.
Sodg'er Sandy's Bairn.
Price, 1/-; Post Free, 1/1.
By A. BATHGATE.
With six illustrations by Harry Julius,
anti a cover design by Percy Spence.
Mr. Bathgate is new to Australian readers, which is unfortunate for both author and readers,
for “Sodger Sandy’s Bairn” displays such an amount of ability that it seems a pity he did not ven-
ture sooner. He gives us some splendid pen-pictures of Scotch and colonial types, describes the
early gold rushes and their attendant excitement with wonderful vividity, charms us with his insight
into human nature, possesses a command of the Scotch vernacular that reminds one of .T. M. Barrie
or lan Maclaren, whilst his fund of pawky humor is rare and refreshing. Scotch people especially
will welcome this book and want to know more of its author.

MELBOURNECHATTER.
Melbourne, March 23, 1914.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The Denman lady recently called a meeting
about the Repertory Theatre Ball, which has
been postponed from May 1 to May 6. The
cohorts of the Best Fed duly responded to
the summons, and the council chamber in the
Town Ilall was filled with a good working
mixture of plain Culture in plain clothes,
fashionable culture chasers in beautiful
clothes, and yearning Repertory actors and
actresses. Her Ex. sat at the head of the
table. She had Gaud Mayoress Hennessy for
company, also secretary Cornish, of the Mel-
bourne Club, who is really giving the job his
serious support. (Good of him, isn’t it?) A
whirring electric fan made white hat feathers
to writhe and coil serpent-wise round the
chairlady’s head, until she bore some resem-
blance to Medusa. However, she was merely
chuckling to herself at Alfred Cornish’s diplo-
matic advice (given very confidentially, of
course) : “Get them all together an’ give
them tickets —and more tickets. Load them
with tickets!” That, in a gentler form, was
Lhe text of the chairlady’s discourse. She
-advised all hands to give dinner parties and
ipresent the guests with tickets —or get them
ito dinner and enlist them as ticket-sellers
*after dinner. Mrs. Consular Bouvet at once
made her Toorak home a centre for ticket-
iselling. Beatrice Day, who is visiting her,
• offered her services in the good work. Mrs.
'Gregan McMahon implored people to hurry
nip their sets and get in early on the photo-
graphic list. Chamberlain Nevill sat quietly
.and took stock of the situation with the eye
•of a general who has astounding moves up
Ihis sleeve, but committed himself to nothing.
MStead pervaded the meeting, and other men
irdlled up. Eighteen plays—some of them
'Shakspearian—have been marked off as sets.
'The various sets are to pose in tableaux
ibefore they dance, and, as the chief mummers
thereabout are interested, the shows should
ibe really good. Anybody in any kind of
fancy dress can join the whirl, for it is not
urecessary to belong to a set. The Denman
ilady has not yet divulged what character she
lhas chosen, so the Most. Loyal are waiting
Ibreathlessly for a cue. Boffard is ladling out
iiniformaifion to those who require it, and
everybody of the slightest importance has
tickets to sell.
The new Stanley lady was initiated into
the obscure mysteries of the Austral Salon
last Thursday, but she is expected to get
over it. She took up the presidency, along
with heaps of be-ribboned vegetables and
many pints of heady talk. (I don’t know
how they manage to bottle such stuff, but
they do.) The Madden dame wafted the
guest into place and kept as a dark secret
the fact that she had just been farewelled
out of the job. In fact, the farewell bou-
quets were at the moment tucked away out
of sight in the back regions. The Magpies
orchestra (female) made suitable soft music,
and the show voices of the minute were
paraded for her Ex.’s diversion. The Salon
dotes —and bloats —on budding artists, and
has given them a chance from the time of
Amy Castles’s infancy.
Dorothy Sternberg, the dazzling flapper
daughter of Sam Sternberg, of Sydney, and
a niece of Bendigold Sternberg, M.H.R., has
said “Yes” or “I suppose so” or “Oh, this is
so sudden!” to Leslie Benjamin, son of the
late Benjy Benjy, who was a knight and
Mayor of Melbourne. Leslie is a beautiful
young yachtsman in his spare moments. The
two met for the first time at a dinner party
three weeks ago, and the engagement is re-
garded as being one of the fastest on record.
Jim Grice, son of the Moondah Grices at
Frankston, is engaged to Daisy Bree, of
Hamilton. Jim evidently finds single cussed-
ness rather lonesome now his sisterly chum,
Nancy, has changed her name and gone off
to live in another man’s house.
Doris Cooke has written a song and com-
posed the accompaniment, which Falkiner
Smith has accepted for publication. The
small girl was patted on the head by Mar-
garet Cooper, when that lady visited Mel-
bourne, and was given considerable encour-
agement to go ahead with her singing-music.
Dr. Emery Gould’s widow is dead. She
and her conspicuous embonpoint will be very
much missed, for her friendliness and en-
couragement to young musicians was warm-
hearted, and noted ex-pupils of hers are scat-
tered all over the world. The late Mrs.
Emery was a Miss O’Shannessy, and came
to Australia from Ireland when she was only
a youngster. She always had a voice, and
she and Melba were fellow-pupils of Mar-
ches!. She never lost her intense enthusi-
asm for musical affairs, and was always
cheerfully to the front at concerts, with her
dignified robes spread carefully about her,
and her eyes bird-bright with eagerness. Her
face was as free from lines as a child’s, and
her complexion was the envy of many. Cheer-
ful through many big griefs, she made moan
continuously about her too-abundant flesh,
which compelled her to move with care, in-
stead of darting about as she would have
liked. But dignity was Mrs. Emery Gould’s
keynote. Her husband, dead this many
years, was a noted Collins-street medico, with
a strong leaning toward art in all its
branches.
“Reggie,” the youngest son of the late
Janet Clarke and Baronet William, refused
to go home and be nursed when he caught
cold in camp. Later on he was taken to
hospital—but it was too late. He was
buried with all possible pomp, and stray bits
of artillery rattled about the streets for
hours after the funeral.
Otto Schmidt is going back to Germany
to sort out the affairs of his late father,
who was well known in commercial circles.
Otto is carrying back a Will Longstaff por-
trait of the departed, to hang in a German
gallery.
The Alexandra Club females excitedly
ruffled their feathers and clucked round the
Prout-Webb-Brandon pair, who had a wed-
ding breakfast at the exclusive Chookery
after their marriage. The bridegroom sub-
sequently rescued his wife from a raging sea
of hats, and carried her off to North Banana-
land, where he does something with mining.
The pupils of Weber and Rice’s Health
and Strength College had their annual night
out at the Auditorium last Thursday, with
the Madden Waxellency as medal distributor.
Among influential patrons of chest inflation
and dumbbell exercise sturdy John was ever
to the fore. Now that girl graduates of the
college are figuring freely on the platform,
his fatherly interest seems keener than ever.
About two dozen girls the other night re-
lieved the monotony of the exhibition by
giving a display of swaying movements,
“bath” exercises and means of filling up
“salt cellars.” Gladys and Ida Buller did
star feats and poses, and the men—especially
Weber and Rice—were wonderful. But the
introductory speech of the beauteous Clarence
was too long, and the stage management
left a lot of professional snap to be desired.
Kathleen Watson’s “Later Litanies” has
come out, and, what is more, it promises to
remain out, in company with the “Litanies
of Life,” which the authoress published in
London long ago, before she changed her
name to Deardon and came to Australia.
There were five Litanies in the parent book.
There are four in its belated offspring, the
first and least persuasive of them being “A
Seouel to the House of Broken Dreams.”
The strength of the new book is in the two
middle pieces, for “Ursula” is almost as
good as K.W.’s previous best, whilst “If
Love But Knew” is distinctly better. The
woman in this case is a divorcee of many
moods, not so loaded with self-pity as the
sorrowful Litanic spinsters of yore who fell
into indiscretion quite easily, or repulsed
temptation with scorn, or sometimes be-
moaned the lack of “one stain of scarlet sin”
in her life. The divorcee of the Later Litany
finds compensations, and ends her glowing
monologue hopefully. All the poetry of the
authoress’s prose is in it: her word painting
has some beautiful bits of color, and the
composition of the little domestic melodrama
is equally gracious and artistic in its un-
reality.
The Grey-Egerton dame has been indulging
in the appendicitis operation since her visit
here with daughter Amy Clarke. Dr. Doug-
las Shields did the removal act. Douglas
now has his private hospital in full swing in
London. The Dudley patronage was re-
sponsible for that move.
One of the Cudmores, who circle socially
round three States (their station is perched
somewhere about the top edges of Victoria
and S.A., and drinks out of the river Dar-
ling, in N.S.W.), is to be married on Satur-
day, to George Stogdale. The ceremony
is to be at St. John’s, Toorak, and the sub-
sequent spree at Mandeville Hall —the Ross-
Soden white elephant palace that is now a
paying guestery.
Manisty, of the Naval Department, left
Australia last week in a cyclone of adieux,
directed at himself and his wife. Manisty
originally came out as secretary to Admiral
Henderson, and was retained here to organise
the secretarial work. The job is now handed
on to an Australian. The departed official
had made good in Australia. He became a
great favorite and was sent off with full
honors. The G.-G. Denmans lunched him
and the missus at a special farewell party,
and the Admiral Creswell couple played about
with them all the week. Mrs. Manisty got
her own particular send-off from the Quamby
Club, while her husband was being dined by
the Naval Department. The stateroom
aboard the Osterley was fairly littered with
complimentary vegetables. All Mrs. Man-
isty’s relations came in a body from Sydney
and put up at Menzies’ for a week’s fare-
welling. She, in the first instance, was
heiress Flo McCathie, the very rich daughter
of the late Mrs. McCathie. An Australian-
born son, John Collingwood Manisty, is
being taken proudly to England with a nurse
escort and the luggage of a young prince.
Lady Patey and her children, coming hither
to join the Admiral, are at time of writing in
quarantine. The Orsova, their travelling
ark, brought along a case of s nall-pox, so
they could, not get ashore at Melbourne, and
the Government House visit had to be cut
out.
Gregan McMahon and his Repertory
ininions will again tread the Royal stage to-
night (Thursday), when “Rutherford and
Son” will divert the third Repertory audience
of this week. McMahon is taking advantage
of a break in the big theatre’s date and put-
ting on evening shows.-
Collins, president of the local Automobile
Club, is off to Brisbane for two years. Vic-
torian roads will quite miss the spirited and
original objurgations of the cheery little man,
who generally has half a dozen cars in com-
mission at the same time. The Automobile
Club tendered him a farewell dinner, during
which numerous long-distance speeches were
made. The remarks were mostly of a
friendly character. If the guest has any
carping critic he didn’t turn up to carp that
evening.
The Opera House matinee, in aid of -the
Austin Hospital, was a fashionable squash.
Hugh Mclntosh lent the building for the
good cause, and every theatre in Melbourne
contidbuted a turn. Mrs. Ethel Warwick
Potiphar brought over her crystal, her snake,
her love-god and the few clothes she wears
in the temptation, and gave the big thrill of
the religious melodrama. It is scarcely
credible, but Eugenie Duggan Anderson actu-
ally recited “Curfew Shall Not (sob) Ring
To-night.” She wore a lace dress that would
have been torn to shreds in the acrobatic bell
act, also a velvet hat that could not have
survived one swing. Comedian Lupino, of
the panto, wore tights made out of a hot-
water-bag-cover (or it may have been a leop-
ard skin) under a pink tulle ballet skirt, and
perpetrated a song and dance that made the
boxed vice-regals sit up and stop yawning.
Lady Denman, in a favorite green outfit,
came along with Help Pollock in tow. Lady
Stanley, behind a lorgnette in the opposite
box, had Captain Wilson to yawn with. The
two Gentlemen Helps in opposition camps
signalled merrily to each other. The pretty
programme and sweets girls (many of them
were Society flappers just recruited from the
high schools) bushranged Bourke-street. The
“talent,” that lounge everlastingly by the
kerb, said “Blimey!”—or words to that
effect —when they were politely elbowed aside
by self-possessed girls in dainty frocks, who
had no use for any idle starers. The “talent”
are only used to large policemen, for whom
they make room for a second or so, till the
path-clearing mountain of muscle passes.
Then they step back and obstruct the foot-
traffic again. The vice-regal boxes were
draped in silver tissue and roses. Expensive
white satin boxes of sweets went with the
bouquets, and free tea was conveyed in haste
at refreshment time, while the general public
stretched out its necks and waved its good
money vainly in the air.
The Mclntosh niece has- come from Sydney
and she turned over the Flat to the busy girl
volunteers for the tea-brewing. The Gaud
Mayor gazed at the Tangoing from centre
circle, and seemed to be favorably impressed.
At any rate, he didn’t rise and forbid the
band. Mrs. Major James looked after the
show from a secretarial point. The proceeds
will go to fit out a stall, in charge of Gaud
Mayoress Hennessy, at the forthcoming
Austin Hospital bazaar. The money will
thus get a fresh spin and multiply exceed-
ingly.
Max Pirani got a houseful for his com-
plimentary concert on Saturday night, in the
Auditorium. The very young man has been
taken up with tremendous enthusiasm by the
Best People, and they are assuring one an-
other that Max will be great when he gets a
chance. The clever, 14-year-old youth is so
nearsighted now that he peers at his hands as
if they were strange insects. (There is
nothing else to peer at, for he doesn’t use
music on the platform.) On Saturday he
played a “Petite Suite” of his own, a highly
technical, old-worldish —or maybe it is next-
worldish—piece of work. When he was some-
where about mid-channel, a ghostly voice
whispered “Faster!” and the serpentine hiss
undulated through the orchestra benches, dis-
tressing to the female relative and friend,
who was keyed up to hysterical pitch. Alberto
Zelman and his orchestra sawed off a sooth-
ing accompaniment for the prodigy when he
attacked a Beethoven Concerto. Margaret
Murdoch and A. C. Bartlemann were merely
on the programme to sing Pirani songs,
which were all short items, and accompanied
by the composer. State Governor Stanley,
clad in a new season’s assortment of stars,
orders, cancelled P.N.’s, etc., attended with
his wife and showed symptoms of pleasure.
I really believe he is fond of music. Well,
he’ll get all he wants if he stays long enough.
I watched his face intently from a distance
of one yard and shorthanded his expressions.
Most of the time he wore the restful, dreamy
look of the real concert devotee, but his
pleasant countenance tied itself into an agon-
ised knot of distress and capsized when a
flurried solo violinist played acutely sharp in
bis double stoppings. (This isn’t a dentistry
item, as_you might think. It’s just a scrap
of music, jargon I had by me.) Lady Stan-
ley wore a dainty air and a big, red rose, and
she followed the music, note by note, to the
melodious end. Help Gale, tall as a giraffe,
looked as gentle as a lamb, and coiled himself
up on his chair like a faithful and fasci-
nated python. Premier Watt squired her
Ex., and Mrs. Watt sat alongside the Gov-
ernor. Gaud Mayor Ilennessy beamed
through eyeglasses ab the young pianist,
and Mrs. Ilennessy listened politely in a
frock of cherry-embroidered silk. The most
intense and soulful crowd of Toorak’s ex-
clusive musical families filled the stalls.
(By “exclusive people” I mean the special
Toorak rabbits who burrow so deeply into
the suburb that motors and carriages are
necessities, if they are ever to get out of
their hole and frisk. Using the word in this
sense, _ a lonely selector’s wife is the most
exclusive lady in the State.) These hermits
won’t come to town for nothing, and it was
a triumph to drag them in. The metallic
result of the concert should give Pirani a
good long lift on the road to knowledge.
He left for Europe, with his grand piano
under his arm, on Tuesday.
Ernest Williamson, now the occupant of
a pleasant billet in Adelaide, will cross
the Victorian border next month for the
usual orange blossom ceremony with cousin
Hilda Williamson. The sentimental affair
began when the bride-elect was in short
socks; yet the young pair were quite sur-
prised to find that the announcement of
their engagement surprised nobody. Ernest
is a personable, fair-haired young man who
has popularised himself in the best ball-
rooms, and his thatch will contrast nicely
with Hilda’s. She is a brunette and cousin
to the W. ,T. T. Clarkes, but whether she is
a brunette because she is a relation or a
relation because she is a brunette, I do not
profess to know. Probably neither fact in-
terferes with her playing of the piano, if she
is addicted to the use of that instrument.
She may not be. Life is full of chances, and
it may be that the mouth-organ is the only
thing she plays.
Dr. Reggie Morrison is tearing himself
from the corner patch on the Toorak-road
where he took root many years ago. He will
replant himself in the heavily-brassplated
part of Collins-street, with his wife and trio
of sons. Dr. Percy Brett, brother of lawyer
Brett, will trundle across from Glenferrie to
fill the vacated house.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The widow of that John McKinley who went ex-
ploring when our history was in its infancy died at
Gawler the other day. It is a nice, quiet place to die
at. She left £20,000, the which sum goes to her
brother —one John Pile.
The Coles noblewoman is leaving Glenelg for a
couple of years’ travel. I gather that the trip is in
the nature of a rest-cure. The blithering excitement
of life at Glenelg had begun to get on her nerves.
Mrs. Maxwell Fowler is here —or rather at Glenelg
—again, in the wake of her naval husband. She
spends most of her working hours playing golf.
Mrs. Alfred Stokes —she was Miss Barbara Jessop
—-has achieved a male infant —an astounding child
reputedly, rich in charm and intellect; of extra-
ordinary physical strength and exceptional beauty;
and with a moral character second to none. Not
even the most carping Stokes or the most pessimistic
Jessop is prepared to deny that the new baby is a
neat combination of Napoleon, Apollo and St. John
Chrysostom.
Mr. Russell Booth is slowly getting better in the
North Adelaide Hospital.
The inter-State Tennis Tournament has been raging
at Adelaide Oval. I use the word raging deliberately,
on account of the heat. Most of the weather was
the sort that makes one comprehend the point of
view of the gentle Indian who pays others to take
exercise on his behalf.
The Bakewell-llenderson wedding is arranged for
April 22. The nuptial feast will be spread at the
John Downers’ house.
The Tango Teas continue to draw vast crowds to
Hughie Mclntosh’s Hall of Song. In fact, not to
put too fine a point on it, Adelaide has gone stark,
staring balmy in this particular direction. The
holiest of our citizens are regular patrons of the
new diversion. They say that they go to see that
nothing improper occurs, but as to that I have my
lingering doubts. Each tea is a shade rortier than
the last —the merest suggestion of a shade, yet still
the difference was there. Nevertheless, the attendance
increases. Several of our most estimable citizens are
doggedly learning the Tango. They say that they are
determined to prove by personal experience whether
or no it is as undermining to the character as some
folks say.
The Dudley Turners’ house in Stanley-street is
having the last touches put to it. It represents the
final shriek in houses. In brief, it is a bosker. The
fortunate owners are at Kingsmead, superintending
the shifting of their furniture.
Dr. Mawson has persuaded the University to grant
him leave of absence for 1914 so that he can go to
England and wind up the affairs of his expedition.
(This is my polite way of saying that he intends to
try and collect enough money there to pay the expe-
dition’s debts.) Frank Stillwell, of Victoria, will
act in his absence.
Superintendent Rickwood, of the Fire Brigade,
who died on Monday after a short illness, had been
in Adelaide since 1905 —prior to which he held a good
naval billet in New South Wales. He was a Cornish-
man. You have heard of the Cornish wreckers, of
course? Well, Mr. Rickwood was one of them. He
wrecked fires like a tradesman.
We had a mild railway smash of our own on
Saturday. No one was kiiled, unfortunately for the
newspapers, but several people were bruised and
shaken up generally. The local trains crawl for
safety, but it isn’t always a success. The tortoise
may not be fined for furious driving, but he may be
overtaken by a caterpillar, and trampled underfoot.
Yours sincerely,
JOHANNA.

ALITIES.
THE S ECO ED ROBBER DRAWS A
BLANK.
THE STRONGER HALF: “My dear wan, you're
too late. The landlord collected the rent
this morning."
“L.S.” : Here are the plans and specihca-
of a true fairy-godmother tale:
The Vic. bushfires on the anniversary of Black
Thursday swept away a few homesteads, and the
family humpy of Bill Bellamy went while he was
getting his invalid mother out of her little home a
few chains away. The old lady strongly objected to
being rescued; but finally she was dumped into the
middle of a potato field, and left with a guard of
grand-daughters to keep the sparks off. Bill got
badly burned, and couldn’t use his hands even to
SSt « th“ e o.7w" A there
till his burns healed up, wondering how on earth he
was going to start another home. By the time he
was able to get up he had settled it all. He
would borrow from the Guv’ment, and buy Bob
Monk’s four-roomed tenement that was “for sale for
removal,” in the township. Bob was a friend of his,
and wouldn’t open his mouth too wide. Mrs. Bel-
lamy thought it was a good plan, and if the money
would run to it, a little furniture could be got.
Bill resolved to get up next morning and limp over
to the site, and see if the blocks were good enough
to use again. All that night he was dreaming of the
new home, and could even hear the carpenter’s ham-
mers fixing it up, and once he could have sworn he
heard Bob’s voice urging his minions to “get a blanky
move on.” Soon after daylight he struggled up, drew
on the only pair of pants the fire had left him, and
went out. He turned towards the place where his
house had been, and—it was still there! But it was
different, somehow—a trifle bigger, and newer look-
ing. Bill struggled over to the building, and then
recognised it, and half realised the truth. Bob Monk,
an old pal, had pondered over Bill’s parlous cas<\ and,
if you know the bushman, you can guess the rest.
Bob did not open his mouth too wide, either. In
fact, he refused to extend that orifice at all.
* * * *
“Viti”: After 40 years of so-called work
ip. Fiji, the missionaries have very little to show in
the way of practical Christianity amongst the native
population. True, they have established numerous
mission schools and built dozens of churches (with
the Fijian’s money), have persuaded the natives to
put on more clothes, and taught them to sing and
recite, with the understanding mostly of a parrot.
But, at bottom, there is very little change for the
better. The young men and women, when they leave
the mission schools, generally consider themselves
much better than their neighbors. The men there-
fore flock to European settlements, acquire all the
misfit vices of the white .man, and generally end up
in a bad way. The girls, for the same reason, do
the same thing, and often end up worse. Fijians
are born singers and slaves to show. Take the
singing away, and you would empty every native
church and mission school in Fiji. Take their gay,
tawdry European clothes and ornaments away, and
you would drive almost every Fijian back to the
hills and out of touch with the white man. Euro-
peans who have lived in Fiji for the last 40 years,
and are not missionaries, will tell one that if it were
possible to remove every foreigner from the islands,
and the natives were left to themselves, they would
be back again within 10 years to the habits and
customs of 100 years ago, so thin is the veneer of
civilisation and Christianity upon them. Every day
one sees bits of the savage crop out. If a Fijian
has to dress a fowl (they are very fond of poultry)
he will probably pluck it alive; and if he is called
away during the operation, he will simply break
its legs and leave it thus until he has time to
finish the job. Pork is much prized food, and pigs
are to be found in every village. As often as not,
these unfortunate 'animals have had their eyes
taken out, to prevent them straying away from the
village. The islanders are -still the same, supersti-
tious race they have always been. I can illustrate
that with an instance. A cutter was crossing from
one island to another.. The occupants were a party
of native mission teachers. A storm got up quickly,
as storms are apt to do in the tropics. All on
board were terrified, expecting the little craft to
founder any moment. Instead of doing what good
Christians might have been expected to do, they
huddled up in terror. At last the head teacher pulled
himself together, and addressed them, asserting that
some of their number must have committed a great
crime, and the gods would surely wreck the boat
if the guilty one did not confess. Almost immedi-
ately a young woman owned up —and the favored
gentlemen were among the party. Evidently that
squared things, for they reached the shore safely.

“Peter Culex” : The revised version of the
Epic of Douglas Pitt, according to “Bullswool”—no,
“Wul-Mul” (B. 26/2/’l4) —is interesting
reading, but suffers from blind totters in
its facts. This scribe has no desire to
belittle an excellent swim. If Douglas
had swum 98 miles in a typhoon, and
then crawled another 37 miles under
water, holding on to the rocky bed of
ocean by means of a set of patent, alumin-
ium-jointed false teeth, and securing his
back hair with the hand in which he was
not carrying a numerous family and a
kedge anchor, and the entire furniture of
a two-storey brick house, “Wul-Mul” would
have had some real cause for his indigna-
tion. Also, had Mrs. Pitt’s medal been
presented by Nicholas the Greek to the
decrepit maiden aunt of the White Terror
of Badu, the recriminations of the mili-
tant “Bulswool” would have been thor-
oughly justified. But the real facts —not
the “Wul-Mull” variety—are given in my
version. They came from the amphibious
pair themselves, from Pitt’s family, from
men who were in the rescue expedition,
and from official documents. Next time

“Eddyson”: I paid my second visit to Red-
;rock a few weeks back. My first visit to Redropk
'was in the Easter of 1908, when I heard the divert-
ing history of the domestic .disturbances of the
Treshams and the Golfoils. Tresham and Golfoil were
sitting in mute and miserable companionship on a
three-rail fence as Witt and I drove past, and Witt
told me. “Them’s neighbors,” he said. “That’s Gol-
foil with the ginger chin whisker. The other bloke,
the one with the blue wart on his eyelid, is. ’Enery
Tresham. They’re cobbers, ’n’ when you see them
sittin’ there, it means they’re swoppin’ sorrers, their
missuses bein’ up.” “Up?” I queried. “My oath!
Up in arms. It’s this way with Bob Golfoil. There
was a time when he could lick, his missus, and done
it when necessary. Don’t you go gettin’ virtuous
about it. There ain’t no call. Bob’s Ann could
put up a good fight on her own. She wouldn’t want
Societies for the Prevention buttin’ into her affairs.
Whatever ’appened was atween her and Bob, ’n’
was part iv the marriage contrac’. If Bob swatted
her with a hoe, she spanked Bob with a pan in mutual
affection. ’Owever, Bob was a handy man in a scrap
where all was in, from spades t’ clubs, ’n’ was master
in his own house —till that Sunday when he ’n his
missus disagreed about the suet duff. Bob hated
duff. Ann got insolent, ’n’ Bob corrected her with
the mutton. I don’t hold with ’ittiri’'- a good wife
with roast leg o’ mutton, especially hot, but every
man to his fancy. Golfoil swatted Ann from the
kitchen door to the pigpen with his Sab.bath meal.
That assault ’n’ battering led to disclosures. In 15
minutes Mrs. Bob was back at scratch, and she had
Mrs. ’Enery Tresham with her. Mrs. Golfoil fired
point blank at Bob with a scrubbin’ brush, ’n’ near
hooked the ear off him. Mrs. Tresham took him on
the left flank with a small piece of fencin’, and in
30 seconds Bob was conscious iv his failure. He
caved in ’n’ apologised. The situation was not fully
understood till Tresham, havin’ occasion to correct
his Lucy with a trace belt, presently found Mrs.
Golfoil workin’ in with two-thirds of a kitchen chair
in her two hands. Tresham was whipped. He had
t’ kneel up ’n’ beg pardon, ’n’ promise to mend his
ways. What had ’appened was a sort iv offensive ’n’
defensive alliance atween Mrs. Golfoil ’n’ Mrs.
Tresham. The ‘ontong cordyil’ they called it. Well,
that ‘ontong’ put the fear iv ’igh Heaven into the
’e'arts iv Golfoil ’n’ Tresham, ’n’ each took second
place in the family councils for some considerable
time after the fift’ consecutive attack iv the allies.
Then Tresham ’ad a brilliant idear. He tore off to
Golfoil with it afore it could go cold on him. What
he had to suggest was an opposition union, him ’n’
Golfoil to form a combine fer their mutual protec-
tion, ’n’ fer purposes of domestic aggression when
same seemed advisable in the interests iv peace ’n’
good govinment. Well, nex’ time the combined
forces iv Ann ’n’ Lucy got at Golfoil, Tresham come
in ez reinforcements, ’n’ there was a fine sample
scrum in Golfoil’s kitchen. But the male element
predominated. Ann ’n’ Lucy was licked with great
slaughter. Also on a subsequent occasion they was
outed. In further engagements the ‘ontong’ got it
good ’n’ ’ard, ’n’ was glad to capitulate. Then there
was more or less silence and domestic peace fer two
months, pendin’ the arrival iv Mrs. Smith, which
said lady was Mrs. Golfoil’s mother. The ’usbands’
union was not good on tactics. It hadn’t suspected
Mrs. Smith’s visit as what’s called a hovert act, but
her appearance was followed almost immediate by a
fresh outbreak, and Mrs. Smith threw her influence,
also two flatirons, agin the union, ’n’ in the interests*
iv the ‘ontong.’ The ‘ontong’ won. Golfoil was in bed
a fortnit. Mrs. Smith is still at Golfoil’s; any at-
tempt to shift her is regarded ez a ‘causes belly’ by
the ‘ontong.’ ’Enery ’n’ Bob, havin’ no power to add
to their numbers, is in a hopeless minority, ’n’ have
’umble pie fer dinner ez oftin ez seven days a week.
When you see ’em out on a fence like this, sittin’
quiet ’n’ silently ciinmiseratin’, you can bet the
‘ontong’ is up and assertin’ itself.” As
stated, I have recently returned from my
•'Second visit to Redrock. The Tresham-Golfoil
complication was still fresh in my mind, and
I went seeking further light. Tresham and
Golfoil were 'not on the fence. A great peace
brooded over the two farms. In the distance I saw
Tresham pointing imperiously, and Lucy, with a
large paij"in her hand, going obediently to the pen.
I trembled fof the “ontong,” and made haste to run
Witt to earth. “How is the Tresham-Golfoil affair
going oij?” I said. “What’s that?” he asked.
“Why, you remember the ‘ontong cordyil’ ?” “Oh,
yes, yes,” he replied. “It's bung. Mrs. Smith’s
dead, an’ the day after she died Tresham and Golfoil
whipped their wives an’ fixed things up peaceable.”

“Jack S.” : Have heard some strange frog
music during my bush wanderings—but have decided
to award the prize to the Queensland tree frog. He
appears to work on some clockwork system, winding
himself up till the spring jams and then releasing
all the startled works with a whirr. It is strangely
like that irritating din which greets the weary tele-
phone user on obeying the dulcet injunction, “Insert
the coin, please.”

BINGHI OF MORNINGTON.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An appeal was recently made by Dr. Mjoberg, the
Swedish scientist, that no white people should be
permitted to settle on Mornington Island, in the Gulf
of Carpentaria, where there are about a thousand
aborigines, who have so far been untouched and
unspoilt by civilisation....
Mr. Hall went, on to say that, of course, the wear-
ing of loin cloths would be encouraged for decency’s
sake....“l don’t say that they can ever be made to
clearly understand the doctrine of the Trinity. But
how many white people understand it? What I do
say is that. . . .Christianity makes a complete change
in their lives.” —- Interview in the Salvation Morning
Herald.
Brother Binghi in his native state is a happy aaid
careless cuss —
He has few ills, and he pays no bills, and he doesn’t
miss the ’bus,
Because there’s never a ’bus to miss where Brother
Binghi dwells ;
He’s black and Avild, and a simple child —as the
missionary tells.
The missionary’s a worthy man, according to our
lights—
lie’s full of cares, but he never swears, and (of
course) he never fights;
But he’s grieved to the heart when he thinks of
Binghi up there in the North,
Without any woes, and without any clo’es, and, so—
he’s going forth !
The scientist—who, of course, can’t know—desires
black Bingfii’V weal,
In the worldly sense, but it gives offence to the
modern Christian’s zeal;
For why should Binghi be let alone, with his pica-
ninny and gin,
In pristine bliss, and therefore miss his share of
sorrow and sin ?
No, no! That’s not the way the Church conducts
things nowadays—
Let souls be saved and the Plate be waved to holy
hymns of Praise!
Though Binghi, frightfully out of date, has never
heard of The Fall,
And Noah’s Ark, and Jonah’s shark — he’s going to
get them all!
And, just as Adam and Eve found out—oh! ever
so long ago—-
The First of Woes, the use of clo’cs, shall come to
Binghi and co.!
With Rum, and Gospel, and Work, and Sin, and
Cant, and dread Disease
We’ll fill his -life, and that of his wife—as she’ll be
when they pay the fees!
Brother Binghi has dwelt Up North since Adam was
a boy—
But now “The Call” (per Reverend Hall) will fill
his heart with joy!
The white folks’ Eden was closed and barred many
thousand .(.ears ago,
And now The >Kirk will do that work for Brother
Binghi and co. 1
N.s.W.
O. C. CABOT.

Norbell”: Some facts about the rabbit-
eating proclivities of sheep were produced at a
meeting of the Pastures Board at Gundagai
(N.S.W.) recently. Director Hassett stated that he
had seen sheep “chase each other for a dead rabbit,
like pigs after a cob of corn.” It is only a matter
of time when sheep will be included amongst the
more savage carnivora, and visitors to the Zoo will
be thrilled with the ferocious baa-a-ing of the man-
eating merino, as he devours ensanguined horseflesh.
An added attraction to the circus will be the an-
nouncement that “at the matinee to-day Professor
Wade, the dauntless and intrepid sheep-tamer, will
enter a cage containing five full-grown wethers.”
State school children will be instructed in what to
do in case of sheep-bite, and Australian sheep runs
will be the hunting ground of daring big-game
slayers from all over the world. The commonest
newspaper headings in those days will be “Savage
Attack on a Shearer. Mauled by a Hogget.” And
Bulletin bards may add a tragic thrill to their
verses on “The Death of the Lonely Boundary-rider.”
His faithful tame sheep will be watching his corpse,
the animal having slain 145 crows, two foxes, 10
wild dogs, a priest, a Levite, a horse, two eagles
and a debt-collector in defence of his owner’s re-
mains. He will be regretfully shot as the only way
of letting the undertaker get at the ruins of the
late lamented. And in those days the lion that tries
to lie down with the lamb will arise and run six
miles when he finds what he has been lying down
with.
“Masarcus”: Can anyone tell me where
the water-hyacinth came from? All I know about
it is that the spread of it is making the men on
the N.S.W. North Coast shiver. It is a sort of fresh-
water priekly-rabbit curse which some fool introduced,
which nobody wants, and which could easily have
been cleared out in the beginning if the authorities
had not left the job till next week. The l’omki
Shire Council is buzzing around looking for a suit-
able weapon with which to kill it. The buzzing has
been getting hurried of late, but still it isn’t half so
hurried as the spread of the hyacinth up the Rich-
mond River. Soon the boats will not be able to
run, and the people who ought to have taken precau-
tions years ago, but didn’t, will have to dodge
quickly if they don’t want to be brained by frenzied
cockies who can’t get their cream to the factories.
“S.H.G.”: The man with grass-seeds in
his whiskers is a whale on “records.” If he can’t
make one any other way he’ll do it by straining his
waggon to death and wrecking the bush tracks. The
present is the season for this sort of lunacy. At
Mirrool (N.S.W.) a farmer, Staniforth, with a
waggon weighing 3 tons 18cwt. 2qr., carted to the
station two loads each of 250 bags of wheat, the
first with 17 horses and the second with 10 ; distance
eight miles. Another, Hodge, at Forbes, landed 225
bags behind a team of 11, one of the leaders being
a staid prad of 22. Mostly the man who practises
this sort of trick with his plant has to do a bit of
unloading and reloading on the track, and it doesn’t
pay; but that’s of no consequence—he gets all the
satisfaction he wants by being talked of for 12
months as the “gun” of the season.
THE POINT OF VIEW.
CITY BOY: “Take milk?"
COUNTRY BOY: “No thanks ; no condiments."
Gouger”: I have met live things hiding
in many curious places, but the most fantastic lair of
all was in a tiny seam of mud, 25 feet beneath the
solid rock surface. He was a little brown toad, and
his tiny eyes never even winked in the candle light,
the first light he must have seen for countless years,
perhaps it was the first he had ever seen. Pressed in
by millions of tons of rock, without air, without
food or moisture, unless it percolated to him from the
surface through the seam of mud, this ill-shapen
castaway had life. I put him out of harm’s way, and
went on sinking. If he liked to live in such an out
of the way place I guess it was no business of mine;
if he got his living there without work, so much the
more power to him. I know I couldn’t. The shaft
proved a duffer.
