you've got a job, but when you wake you'll have none!”
“Y.”: “Bathurst,” said the Rev. M. J.
O Reilly, the other day, “has the habit of giving
the lead to the rest of N.S.W.’’
He referred, in particular, to the
two Bathurst priests who went on
the platform at John Redmond’s
first Home Rule meeting in Syd-
ney. There were other things
that he might have mentioned in
support of his argument. Bathurst
has had the wildest aboriginal
fights witnessed in the white man’s
lifetime. Its gaol was at one time
the worst managed in all Aus-
tralia. Its gaol riot was the
biggest and most furious on
record. Eighty prisoners were
killed, and 10 of the survivors of
the fray were hanged together.
Spider, the blackfellow, some of
whose bones are believed to be
under Deniehy’s monument in
Waverley Cemetery, was a Bath-
urst man, and was suspended on
his native heath. Bathurst
evolved such curiosities as Dan
Mayne, and Taylor, who went in
his halfmast-high trousers and
fought a law case before the
Privy Council. And finally it
gave N.S.W. a lead in the matter
of the discovery of gold. It
tried to hide the fact that there
was gold in the neighborhood, but
failed.
“Oibi”: A very much prized shrub
amongst Kiwais (western villagers of Papua) is
gamada. It has a leaf resembling tobacco leaf in
shape and size; has a notchy stem as in sugarcane;
grows to a height of 10 to 12 feet, and is exten-
sively cultivated for its remedial properties. The
name “gamada” is really the native word for the
drink that is prepared from the root or stem of the
plant. This is cut and sliced into pieces, and, after
washing, compressed for the much-prized juice. The
liquid is of a light brown color, and a wineglassful
is a fair nip. The intoxicating effect of gamada on
the natives so perturbed the missionaries of Western
Papua that they threatened to induce the Govern-
ment to destroy the plant. The threat put- the brake
on for a while, but on appeal to the magistrate it
was found by Brown Brother that the gun wasn't
loaded. I believe the gamada is existent in the
Solomon Islands, but, there, the islander, instead of
preparing a drink as above mentioned, chews the
stem of the plant a la sugarcane.
P. J.M.”: Re the efficacy or otherwise of
the divining rod. Bob Lane, an old Tamworth
(N.S.W.) identity, has been a Arm upholder of the
forked stick for locating hidden deposits of the
precious fluid for quite 30 years past. His experi-
ences at Goonoo Goonoo, the 313,000-acre holding
of the Peel River Land and Mineral Co., are worth
recording. With the assistance of the divining-rod
he selected no fewer than 20 sites on this co.’s pro-
perty where bores were put down. The country
operated upon is undulating, and rock was encoun-
tered at a depth of from 20ft. to 25ft. from the
surface. The bore went down varying depths from
100 ft. to 130 ft., and in 18 cases water was encoun-
tered giving a flow of from 500 gals. to 600 gals. per
hour. In the remaining two instances water was
struck, but the supply was insufficient. Geo. King,
general superintendent of the P.R.L. and M. Co., will
verify these statements. The co. was so well satisfied
with Lane’s work that it sent him to the Currawil-
linghi (Q.) station property. Here he picked out 10
sites in country varying from open blacksoil' plains
to sand ridges and scalded ground. Water was
tapped in every instance at depths ranging from 90ft.
to 130 ft., and the flow in each case went from
300 gals. to 400 gals. per hour. The Peel River Co.,
at any rate, goes nap on him as a diviner.
TAKE OUT YOUR TEETH!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He ordered her to remove her teeth.
—Evidence in assault case.
Because she would not be my love,
I wanted to destroy her;
I did not want to kill my dove,
But simply to annoy her.
My scorned heart thumped my vest beneath
I told her to take out her teeth.
She shook her head. I roared, “Take out
Your teeth this very minute!”
Too long with love I’d fooled about,
And there was nothing in it.
I longed—so twisted were my wits
To make her take herself to bits.
Because she’d been my idol sweet,
I longed to see her yammer.
I shouted for her pearly teeth.
To smash them with a hammer
That was my purpose. (Do not start.
I’d finished with my work of art.)
\
Behold young Love! When scorned is he
Through his own foolish doin’s,
Above all things he longs to see
His goddess laid in ruins!
Lay on Affection’s tomb a wreath!
Take out your teeth. TAKE OUT YOUR
TEETH!!
N.S.W.
THE RELIGIOUS EDITOR.
The Conversion.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THIS is the story of Inesterton's curious
turn. Inesterton lived at Sugartree.
and was a bad man, a notoriously
bad man; big in drinking, and great at
swearing; a gambler and a contemner of
churches. He scoffed at the loud preachers
and scorned the singers of hymns. It was
ne who on a memorable evening took a large
hammer, upended three elders and a Sunday-
school superintendent, and broke the bell of
the Sugartree Wesleyan Chapel because, he
said, its ringing interfered with the calcula-
tions of his card party. A great, swaggering
ruffian was David Inesterton, a thundering
atheist who disbelieved in the devil, to the
great scandal of his neighbors, to whom
Satan and his furnaces had been a lifelong
comfort and consolation. But Inesterton had
land and lucre, consequently it was held by
many authorities that he had some right to
manhandle, the established religions and maul
sacred objects—a right which would not
apply in the case of an impecunious scamp.
One day Inesterton went quietly through
his house, and invited all therein, family,
servants and guests, to assemble at 11 in
the apple orchard. The party, numbering
fourteen, gathered as requested. It was
reinforced by Twin, the rouseabout, a shep-
herd, and a chance sundowner whom David
had entrapped in the yard.
Inesterton did not keep his congregation
long in doubt; he presently appeared carry-
ing a larere box. He set the box on end, and
out of it he took a two-stone family Bible,
which he opened at random on the top of
the box. He next brought forth a rifle, which
he laid beside the Bible. Next appeared
two revolvers. One revolver he placed on
top of the Word, the other he placed in his
belt. “Now,” said David, “you unregenerate
vipers, prepare to be dashed well saved!”
And he uttered a loose yell, and leaped
several feet in the air.
An evil-liver named Scaddan, a guest of
David’s, burst into a roar of coarse laughter.
“What’s the game?” he said. “The game,”
replied Inesterton gravely, “is the salvation
of your immortal, blue-mouldy soul, you
degenerate infidel. Kneel, or I’ll shoot tun-
nels through your heathen ribs.”
Scaddan did not like the look in David’s
eye, and. he was impelled to turn and run;
but David Inesterton fired, and shot him in
the hat, and Scaddan fell, and came back
humbly to the fold on all fours. “Good !” said
David. “There is more joy in heaven over
one sinner who repents than over five hun-
dred dozen righteous ones coming on for
their rewards. Beloved, we are met and
assembled here this blessed day to seek atone-
ment for our blooming sins. Down on your
knees!” David took up the gun. “Down
on your knees, ye unrighteous! Down,
Twin, you pestilent drinker, curser and
swearer, Sabbath-breaker, evil-thinker, and
stealer of sheep. Down, you damn scoun-
drel !” Twin went down. The whole crowd
went down.
Then David Inesterton began to read
Scripture to his tremulous congregation. He
read on and on in a loud, monotonous voice,
straight from the beginning, on through the
family history of Adam, and the thrilling
account of the great begettings, to Jacob’s
coming to the well of Haran, and the births
of Reuben, Simeon, Levi and Judah, and
still on and on out of Genesis into Exodus.
At the point where Moses fled into Midian
the sundowner tried to creep from the place
of worship, but a rifle bullet hitting up the
earth under his nose brought him back. Now
and then David paused from his reading to
expound, and to point out wherein the special
text hit home on the immoral horde to whom
it was his precious mission to bring grace and
light.
David Inesterton preaching was not unlike
David roaring at a bunch of stubborn bul-
locks in a bog. His language was that of
the heathen in his blindness, but there was
no disputing the. excellence of his intentions
and his deep. religious fervor. Also he was
plainly dangerous, and there was murder as
well as piety in his eye. All through the
long, hot day he read the Word. By sun-
down he was over in Deuteronomy. When
he found it too dark to read, he made the
haggard congregation light bonfires. By the
illumination of the fires he resumed service,
and presently burst right through Judges
info the frontiers of Joshua.
But one man, Smith by name, had broken
from the flock, and ridden like mad to Scho-
field for police, assistance and medical atten-
tion. And while Inesterton was just on the
edee of the first book of Samuel five men—
Jones, Murphy, Donoghue, Briggs and
McTaggart—fell upon him from behind.
David put up a great fight for religious
libertv, but was overcome and removed in
custody, clamoring to be permitted to stay
and take up the collection.
Inesterton’s present trouble is religious
mania. He converts millions weekly with
the aid of a large Bible and an imaginary
Maxim gun. His case is considered extra-
ordinary. No '-•ne can understand where he
picked up the idea.
SILAS SNELL.

Pourri-Pourri.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
pourri-pourri (devil business for
I short interpretation) of Papua pro-
yides uncanny experiences for the
most sceptical white. Some of my own have
been weird enough, but they can be counted
as minor hoodoo in comparison with the ex-
periences of people of longer residence and
wider roving in the Possession. Neverthe-
less, it will be more satisfactory to leave
hearsay evidence on one side and set down
my own observations of the science of black
magic or whatever it is.
Between Lakekamu and the other side of
Samarai, Micky W was on gold, and
the best “boy” in his mining team, a splen-
did worker, suddenly went sick. Micky
brought to bear all the medical skill acquired
while a wardsman in a big city hospital, but
the “boy” merely became weaker. It was clear
that, if Micky wanted his employee, the
doctor was urgently required. But when
the doctor had completed his examination
he declared there was nothing the matter
with Ra-liu. The puzzled boss then started
a cross-examination, which eventually
brought out the information that a big
pourri-pourri man, who lived in a village
the other side of Samarai, had pourri-pourried
Ra-liu because he (the devil expert) wanted
a certain kind of very rare and costly shell,
and unless he got it within a given period
Ra-hu would pass hence. It must be under-
stood that there was no communication of
any kind between Lakekamu and Samarai,
except by a coastal steamer, which called at
the mouth of the river (two days’ journey
from the camp) at long intervals. She was
just about due at this time after an absence
of six or seven weeks. So Ra-hu hadn t got
his information concerning the requirement
of the pourri-pourri citizen by earthly means,
as the saying is. His master, looking at
the position from a commercial basis, apart
from his regard for the sick brown brother,
concluded it would pay to obtain a shell
according to specifications and hand it in
to have the pourri-pourri called off. He,
therefore, gave his head boy £2O, told him
to catch the steamer and get a move on tor
the locality of the marine product, thence
hustle to the pourri-pourri operator. Ten
days later, on the first Sunday of the moon,
Ra-hu, who had been dying all day, and
could scarcely hold up his hand, suddenly
walked into Micky’s quarters and said ‘bis
sick was finished,” and he was ready to go
to the claim and dig, next daylight. Sure
enough he did. When the head boy returned
he told how, after much searching, he had
gotten the required specimen of shell, on the
first Sunday of the moon, though he had not
delivered it to the pourri-pourri blackmailer
(who lived 50 miles inland) until some
days later.
Then, there was Sarua, of the armed
constabulary. He went sick one Saturday
morning, and, fearing he might have beri-
beri, the Commandant sent him to hospital.
The doctor sounded h;m all over, and de-
cided that there was nothing wrong; but,
as a change of locality and surroundings
might do good, he ordered him to stay in
hospital “for observation” for a day or so.
Sarua seemed in great pain, and said there
was a stone in his side. Later in the day
Vagi-mari, the head orderly, told me that a
pourri-pourri man on the Mambare, seven
days’ distant by steamer, Ird evil-eyed Sar.ua
so that he must die on Monday at sundown.
We therefore gave Sarua strong medicines
to “kill” the stone, and fed him on foods
most approved by Papuans. Monday came,
and he was walking about apparently quite
recovered; said he felt “proper good. Lut
just as the sun set, lie put down the mug of
cocoa he was drinking and fell back dead.
Yet, the post-mortem examination showed
that he was well nourished and perfectly
normal in all parts of liis body. When I
went round to the hospital next morning,
all the patients were chattering excitedly
together. “Boys” from all parts of Papua,
who couldn’t speak each other’s dialects,
Malays and other Outlanders, all told how
the dead police “boy’s” devil. (ghost) had
come to the hospital door during the night,
clad in uniform, and cried and screamed to
them to let him in. In describing the
ghost’s uniform, they all stressed the fact
that the red constabulary belt was missing,
and although they could not know it, it is a
fact that the belt had been stolen from the
deceased and secreted. One of the Malays,
an old man unafraid of Papuan natives,
opened the door a little, whereupon the dead
man tried to push past. The crowd thrust
him off with sticks, for human hands, they
explained, cannot feel nor touch “devils.”
The men of the Maiva people have the
power of withdrawing their spirits from
their bodies and transferring them to a
ghost or devil village. Walking for some
days into the bush, they eat particular roots.
On the second night of the moon they fast,
and, before lying down to sleep, lave their
bodies in the water of a running, stream.
For three weeks after that they live with
the ghost people, conversing with them, and
walking in their company, although they
cannot touch them. No woman, and no
man who is married, may visit the ghost
village. Those who make the trip are known
to their friends by the peculiar odor that
clings to them for months afterwards.
Finally, this is how I lost my little tur-
quoise ring, and how pourri-pourri brought
it back. I left it on a table in one of the
hospital wards, and returning some time
later found that it had After
careful but unsuccessful search, Kukane,
then head boy, said: “Suppose I make one
pourri-pourri along findum ring you wild?”
I assured him I wanted the ring however it
was found. Next morning it was handed to
me. with the explanation of Kukane. He
and Lai (a boy from another district) had
walked about on a hill and found “weeds,”
which they carried home and infused in hot
water. Whilst the native patients (some
501 were asleep a few drops _ were
sprinkled on the pillow of each. Kukane
and Lai drank what remained of the decoc-
tion and rolled up in their blankets. Whilst
they slept the “devil” of the thief came and
showed them where the ring, wrapped m.a
rag and buried under a stone, was hidden.
The thief, a Purari cannibal, who couldn't
understand a word of their “talk,” cheer-
fully admitted that he was the culprit.
Pourri-pourri is a punishable offence in
Papua, but it has its uses.
SIDNEY S. SEVERN.
In the Surf.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
EIGHT o’clock on a silver-grey morning.
The sea is of the hue of a sword-
blade breathed upon. The great,
slow waves roll in almost silently, and
break into vaporous blooms. Bridal veils
of finest spray are thrown caressingly upon
the grey rocks in the distance. On the
beach, where the little waves play with vel-
vet paws, like little coquettish cats, there is
a delicate, curving line of silver braid. Head-
land beyond headland lies with feet in the
waters like some giant beast of pxekistoiic
days, turned into stone while drinking. The
nearest headland is dark and grim as the
Tower to which Childe Roland came, the
farthest is misty and faint as a castle in
Dreamland. Far away on the horizon is a
phantom ship. The sun comes from behind
the gauzy clouds for a moment and trans-
figures her. She is the Spirit of Poetry walk-
ing the waters. The place of vision is the
Fairy Bower at Manly at eight o’clock on a
silver-grey morning.
* * * *
Three figures of men in bathing costume
emerge from the surf and run rapidly up the
beach to the fence on which their clothes
hang. These are the heroes, the stalwarts
of Manly. With stubborn courage, not un-
mingled, perhaps, with a little bravado, they
breast the breakers every morning, summer
and winter, sun or rain, warm or cold, calm
or storm, and come forth with faces of the
color of boiled plums. They deserve honors,
and should have their praises sung in lotty
verse. The following lines may pass for
want of better :
In the days antique
Gracefully the Greek,
Who in marble dreamed,
To each Hero deemed
Worthy of renown
Gave a parsley crown
And a statue fair
In the public square.
If Australian Art
Means to play its part
In this land of rare
Blue-and-golden air,
Here are subjects grand
For the sculptor’s hand 1
Make a diadem
Green for each of them.
Lacking parsley —pooh!
Thistle-crowns will do.
But to do them justice they are quite able
to sing their own praises. The 10.10 boat
to Sydney resounds with their boasting.
That is after they have had breakfast, now-
ever. When they come out of the water tney
give one goosefiesh to look at them.
All the rest of the familiar faces and
figures have vanished. Where art thou now,
sweet Undine, with the slender form anu
feet of ivory, and where art thou, O snowy-
bearded Neptune, who couldst tame the wild
sea-horses once? Where art thou, Danie
O’Connor? There is no answer from tne
deserted beach or the lonely waters, lkey
have all departed, all the familiar figures, to
their homes in the inland suburbs. By the
way, the real residents of the \illage look
upon Sydney as a suburb of Manly. In
their geography the former is a Pl a !-® chiefly
noted for its export of visitors. Manly is
self-centred —“round and perfect as a star.
It has its own council, and its own bowling-
green, its own Cardinal’s palace and its own
steam merry-go-round. What more does a
little town require to be happy?
* * ■» *
Still, one misses the women in dressing-
gowns, walking back from the beach in the
mornings, carpet-slippered or barefoot as
goddesses. Also the men with towels around
their necks, who drop into the hotel for a
drink on their way home, to breakfast. And
the laughing and dancing in the surf, that
seems itself to laugh in sheer gladness ot
life and motion. And the men and women
together, clad in decent bathing suits, diving
into the sparkling breakers, and rejoicing,
untrammelled by convention, in the golden
sunshine. It is a sight to make an old man
young again. Bald-headed men, who when
they go to the city an hour or two after-
wards, sit in their officers as solemn as
sphinxes, have thrown aside the cares of
business for a time and are capering around
and ducking each other like boys, ret there
are sour-faced, narrow-minded Puritans who
see harm in this, little knowing that the sea
is the finest of moral disinfectants. Let
them gnash their teeth.
* * *
Women are never so natural as when they
are in the surf. They are then free from the
cramping slavery of corsets, and seem to
revel in their temporary liberty. They are
not troubled about their figures. Stout and
lean, they enjoy themselves equally with the
few who have fine figures by grace of Nature.
And, to tell the truth, these latter are very
few indeed. I would not advise a man in
search of a wife to go to Manly Beach in
the bathing season to choose one, if beauty of
shape be the chief feminine charm in his
eyes. Let him stay in the city and cherish
his illusions.
* * w
I want to start a company to work a
water toboggan at Manly in time for next
season. The idea is to erect a lofty platform
on the beach above tide-mark, and to have
two lines of rails running out into the sea
beyond the breaker-line. Cars to run on the
rails, each car to be capable of seating, say,
half a dozen persons. One set to run down
while the other runs back. A breathless rush
through the breakers and back again without
effort! What do you think of it? _
VICTOR J. DALEY.

A HOME-MADE HAIR GROWER,
THAT DOES MARVELS.
During the last few years announcements
have appeared occasionally telling how to
make a really good hair tonic that would
actually make the hair grow, and it gives us
great pleasure to learn that so many who
tried it have obtained such remarkable
results. One lady writes that immediately
after reading the formula, she went to her
chemist and had him put up, as directed,
1 ounce of Lavona de Composee, i dram
Menthol Crystals, and 3 ounces Bay Rum.
She took this home and, although her hair
had been falling out terribly, and she was
threatened with baldness, she was astonished
and delighted to note that the falling out
of the hair stopped immediately, and the
hair itself began to grow in an amazing
manner. In fact, she states that it has
already grown nearly 8 inches in a little over
two months. Moreover, her hair is now
brighter, gh :sier and more luxuriant than
ever, and is still growing. She rubbed the
tonic into the roots twice a day for the first
three weeks, but now applies it only once a
day. It is especially interesting to note
from this and other reports that this home-
made hair grower not only makes the hair
grow most marvellously, but it also destroys
the dandruff germ and keeps the hair roots
and scalp in a perfectly healthy and normal
condition.*

NEVER FAILS TO PLEASE.
POLITICAL
POINTS
The latest theory of the anti Defence and
Peace Society men is that wars are made
solely by the influence of great cannon-selling
firms, like Krupp’s and Armstrong’s, and
that but for their craving to sell a few more
cannon there would be universal peace. So,
presumably, Krupp or Armstrong or Maxim
sooled Samson on to the Philistines, and sold
him the famous jawbone.
“A.H.,” on Defence :
It looks as if both Federal parties are going shaky
on Defence ; and if they go further they’ll both fare
worse. The solid men in botli camps, and in no
camp, are satisfied that the Defence scheme is the
one really vital thing the party system has ground
out for the last few years, and a multitude of them
would leave their old friends rather than let a hand
be laid on it. But the little men —men like Palmer
of Echuca and Mathews of Port Melbourne —are turn-
ing capacious ears towards the noisy peace societies
and red-rag Socialists. The Liberal organisers of
Melbourne held a conversazione, or bun-fight, recently,
and amused themselves by voting on the “most un-
democratic action of the Fisher Ministry.” And the
compulsory clauses of the Defence Act took a high
place on the poll! On the other side, Mathews and
Fenton, M'sII.R... foregathering with their constitu-
ents recently, joined in the dolorous yelp at the
“oppressiveness” of universal training; and the
Broken Hill people have not yet given up making
asses of themselves over alleged “martyrs of free-
dom.” Meanwhile the Melbourne Peace Society is
noisier than ever, and it lately imported Yankee Starr
Jordan to heln raise a bigger and more blinding
dust. One mob objects to compulsion, another to the
cost, another to “militarism” of all sorts ; and among
them they make enough commotion to set oppor-
tunist politicians philandering. It is up to someone
to give these people fair warning that they’d better
wait and see what happens to the first political crowd
that monkey's with the system It will go right
out into the wilderness, even as Ilagar went from the
tents of Abraham; and it won’t come back for a
long time. The scheme has got hold of the imagina-
tion of Australia as no other has ever done. The
shoe may pinch a little, but we are not going bare-
foot a "ain ; and sooner than throw our defences back
into confusion and ineffectiveness, Australia will see
both parties split from top to bottom, and cast out as
rubbish. Henry Stead may write to London and tell
them that a “great change of opinion” is taking
place. But let Henry come out at the next election
on a No-Defence ticket, and the only opinion that
will he changed will be his. This continent is going
to remain Anglo-Saxon and White, even if we have
to pay a lot more than we are doing to keep it so.
THE FRIEND.
Our obligations with Japan provide against an
increase of the European squadrons in the Far East.
The bond between Britain and Japan means the
effective protection of Australasia.
— First, Lord of the Admiralty.

The Posidonia having apparently been
murdered —that is to say lost —with all on
board, the owners, which are the Anglo-
Foreign Fibre Co. of Adelaide, are reported
to have ordered a new sea-veliicle to be
butchered —we mean despatched—from Hol-
land. Reading the Scripture in a
dim light the other evening, this scribe
thought he came across the parable of
the Unjust Dredge and the widow. It proved
to be a Judge, not a Dredge, but that only
shows that the Jews weren’t a seafaring folk.
The connection between a widow and an im-
ported dredge would strike any nation which
knew the sea.
The petition of politician Walter Bennett
against the return of William Brown, as
member for Durham, in which he (Bennett)
addressed the Governor as “our trusty and
well-beloved Sir Gerald Strickland, our Gov-
ernor of our State of New South Wales,”
was challenged in the Assembly last week
by member J. J. Cohen. The point of order
taken was that the petition was not couched
in decorous or respectful language. Cohen
said he “could conceive of nothing more arro-
gant or impertinent than for any subject of
the realm to use language which could only
be used by the King or his representative.”
Speaker Meagher ruled that he would not
prevent the petition being considered by rea-
son of a misapprehension or clerical error.
Then the House let its bated breath go with
a blow-out, and the wheel of business rolled
on.
The Fabian (Eng.) Research Committee
has discovered a ghastly ill result of the
present working of the Compulsory Insur-
ance Act in Britain.
Millions of families that are living on less than
£1 a week are practically always sick. The Act
extracts a loaf of bread weekly, from bare cupboards,
starving them farther into illness.
LARGE THINGS AND SMALL.
The Chicago Meat Company, with a capital of
£15,000,000, intends to open branches in Sydney,
Melbourne and Adelaide. The company, which
already has a branch in Brisbane, will do extensive
cattle-buying. —Sydney Telegraph.
T HE CAMEL: “ Now, who's afraid of yer
bloomin needle ?”
It is up to some humane Government,
which doesn’t want to have too many
drowned bones on its mind, to put a 200 per
cent, duty on imported dredges. The dredge
that never returned is a butchery, a grave-
yard, a scandal, an unpleasant ghost, and
some other things which are crowded out.
The old sea-songs want to be re-written from
the standpoint of the hopeless tub of steel
which goes forth with its coffin under its
arm, in the sure and dreadful hope that it
will never reach the other end and see its
best girl again.
The other day Senator McColl said of the
Commonwealth Bank (an institution of
which he, as a Minister, is in position of a
director) :
This bank, which was going to do such great
things, was about £50,000 to the bad already, and
was likely to grow worse.
To which ex-Frime Minister Fisher re-
plied :
That statement is a lie, and is such that had
McColl made it about a private institution he would
have found himself in immediate and serious diffi-
culties. Before he made it, the Ministry of which
he is a member knew that, for the last half-year,
the bank made a net profit of £1517 9s. 4d. ; so
either Mr. McColl is not trusted with information
known to his colleagues or he has deliberately and
maliciously tried to mislead the shareholders of the
institution of which he is a director.
Which is a fairly solid charge to lay against
a public man. The amazing thing is that so
far McColl has maintained what is usually
known as a Guilty Silence. He hasn’t even
called Fisher a Lying Hound.
Treasurer John Forrest reports a falling-
off in the Customs revenue. In that case
what is the matter with an import duty
(say 10 per cent.) on foreign loans, and a 5
per cent, export or wharfage duty on in-
terest? It is difficult to imagine anything
more beautiful than a wharfage duty on
interest.
Prime Minister Cook is all right oratori-
cally while it is a mere mater of yelling
“Tripe!” but he seems to
flounder when plainer Eng-
lish is required. Last week
the daily paper young man
called round to ask if there
was anything in the state-
ment that G.-G. Denman was
flying from the country prin-
cipally because he (Cook)
and Iceberg Irvine had tried
to bustle him over the double
dissolution. “There is not a
scintilla of foundation for
any of the statements,”
pompously replied Joseph
Tripe. “Scintilla” is defined
by Webster as “a spark;
giimmer; gleam.” Cook
might have said with equal
dignity that there wasn’t a
flash of pedestal for the
rumor.
A lament from Freetrade England
published as recently as last Sunday:—
Social workers have discovered babies of three
and four years old linking up hooks and eyes in the
factories of Birmingham, and toddlers five years of
age making matchboxes, paper bags and artificial
flowers in London.
Child employment is rampant in the lace-finishing
industry in Nottingham.
In one instance specially mentioned, from Birming-
ham, a woman and her five children, aged from three
to 12, were working together carding hooks and eyes.
It was quite common for the children of poverty-
stricken families to commence work at six years olil.
“Barnacle”: Captain W. 11. Mason, Gov-
ernment Shipping Master at Sydney, is off
to England on a holiday. Formerly he was
skipper of the N.S.S. Sobraon, the old
hulk moored in Port Jackson, which for
years served as a training ship for refractory
hoys. When the N. S. Wales Government
traded the Sobraon off to the Australian
Government Mason lost his job, and the
shipping master’s billet being vacant, the
Ministry ran him into it. As tradition
requires that a master mariner should aet
as shipping master, Mason was called Cap-
tain, though his solitary command was the
ancient training hulk.
Britain’s inglorious climate is playing up
with Marshall Hall in these latter days.
Twenty years of so of life in Melbourne has
made him very susceptible to influenza and
things. He has had two attacks of ’flu’
this London winter, and the second one kept
him from discoursing on “Music in Aus-
tralia” at the Colonial Institute. If poor
old Marshall Hall could only be transported
back to Melbourne just now, his musical
soul would have a lot to say about ragtime
from a Wagnerian point of view. Ile’d
foam at the mouth. Probably the Prof.’s
ailments have been aggravated by the rag-
time-tango epidemic in the Smokopolis.

GENTLEMEN!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. H. McKenzie, M.L.A., has been visiting the
Kyabram portion of his electorate. At a pig sale
(our Kyabram correspondent reports) he met a
number of friends and constituents, and was con-
gratulated on his recovery. Before the sale Mr. McKenzie
mounted the fence, and stated that he wished to
contradict a rumor that he was retiring from public
life. —Melbourne Argus.
See, also, heading to The Bulletin’s “Political
Points.”
Gentlemen! a politician,
One who values his position,
Stands, with easy confidence,
Here before you on the fence.
For he knows full well, good friends,
All your aims and all your ends;
And that these you may attain
He will strive with might and main.
Gentlemen! my sole ambition
Is to see that your condition
Shall continue to improve;
Wherefore I shall shortly move
For a special grant to buy
Extra bedding for your sty—
Force it from the Government
For the folk I represent.
Gentlemen 1 You crave nutrition ;
And I hold my high position
By your will and by your votes.
Pollard you shall have, and oats!
And I know you’ll vote for me
In elections yet to be,
While I cater for your needs,
Promising yet further feeds.
Gentlemen! The Opposition,
By its frequent repetition
Of base lies would have you think
They’d increase your food and drink.
Friends, their secret aim, I know,
Is to cut your rations low,
And, while they but sneer and scoff,
It is we who fill your trough 1
Gentlemen! This talk of “Nation”
Is a vile abomination!
You are asked to sacrifice
Food and swill, and pay a price
For a shibboleth like that!
You are asked to give your fat
That your children, by-and-bye,
May possess a better sty!
Gentlemen! The aspiration
To build up a mighty nation
Is a question far too big
For an ordinary pig.
Truly, we don’t care a damn,
When we’re bacon, pork or ham,
What the fate of pigs may be.
Let ’em root the same as we!
Gentlemen 1 This tortured question
Gives you mental indigestion.
Such vague things you do not heed.
Food in plenty is your need.
In my place in Parliament
It is you I represent;
And I’ll face all vile affronts
For your sakes! ( Delighted grunts.)
Gentlemen! The proposition
For the honest politician
Is: “Can I secure more oats
For the folk who give me votes?
Can I fill their troughs, and give
Mush to them, that I may live?”
To that end he should employ
All his art. ( Loud squeals of joy.)
Gentlemen 1 A politician
With my knowledge and position
Knows full well that such as you
Take the plain, right-thinking view;
For himself each fatted pig,
And for all the rest —a fig!
Gentlemen, I greet your ranks,
And accept your grunt of thanks.
VICTORIA.

daughter had been ailing for some time, and

SPORTING
NOTIONS
On December 25, 1913, it was suggested in
this column that there is no law in N. S. Wales to
prevent, the installation of a totalisator on a licensed
racecourse; Last week Joseph Carruthers, sometime
State Premier, whom this paper has occasionally
suspected of being more intelligent than he looks,
arrived at the same conclusion. The argument put
' forward by The Bulletin was that, on a licensed
racecourse, betting is legal. The Act does not
. specify how the betting shall be carried out, or with
'Whom bets shall be made; It merely legalises betting,
.Therefore the Tote system of betting must be legal.
'Carruthers arrives at the same conclusion by a slightly
different route. His argument is that the Tote is not
,a betting: machine, and neither gives nor takes odds.
;The Tote is merely a recording machine, an automatic
clerk, or an elaborate calculating implement. The
.betting is done between the people who back the
Various horses. They do it without having to come
together personally, because the concern, which is
partly a cash-box and partly an abacus or abacus
Pvthagoricus, acts as intermediary. It is in the same
category as a house agent or commission agent. It
cannot be illegal even if the law prohibits the use of
betting machines, because it is not a betting machine,
such as, for instance, a roulette instrument. Premier
Holman, who is anxious to get the Tote installed on
account of the revenue it will bring him, is so im-
pressed with the idea that the matter has been
referred to the Crown Law officers, and it begins to
look as though the Tote is going to have a big run
for its life in N.S.W.
It is said that there was a frightful scene
in the N.S.W. Government caucus on the question of
introducing a Bill to legalise the Totalisator. Some
of the members put forward some amazing reasons of
the “tainted money” type against the introduction of
the Bill. But the one that proved fatal was a pro-
posal that thd proceeds of a Tote tax should be ear-
marked for public charity. At this the Premier
kicked with both feet, and his supporters also put in
the verbal boot with great emphasis. “Tainted
money,” snorted one indignant member, “I guess the
Tote revenue won’t be half so tainted as the money
some of you have taken to oppose the motion.”
Fierce uproar happened. It died down after a while,
but started again when another member inquired
bitterly, looking at the ceiling over the heads of
some of his friends opposite: “Yes, how much are
(you getting for doing this?” Of course, the men sus-
.pected may have been quite uncorrupt, and have been
merely the tools of others who have been bribed —but
iit is up to some of them to avoid the appearance of
■evil. And it seems a strange thing that the only
| alleged tainted money objected to is that which would
come from the Tote. tax. The purists who objected to
.that made no effort to prevent income-tax being
.charged on the profits of people who are known to be
.living by prostitution and the letting of houses of
. illfame. The incongruity is too great.
; On April 4 the Adelaide Racing Club will
lyse a new machine known as the “Gaut and Ward
[ Totalisator.” Gaut and Ward are Sydney men, and
iUie invention has been fathered by the Australasian
• Totalisators Limited, a Sydney company. The machine
has been put to most exhaustive tests during the last.
; few months, and has emerged triumphantly from them"
> all. A feature is that the ladies’ ticket stalls are fully
[2ooyds. away from where the male punter invests liis
I “dough.” But they are linked up with the main
: machine, and the automatic recorders work perfectly.

up £l9 15s. as a Tote dividend. In the Forestville

H. Gray, the M.L. jockey who steered
Wallalo to victory in the Australian Cup, was well
rewarded for his hurried trip from the Rocking Isles.
He was laid '£looo to nothing that he couldn’t win,
besides several other inducements to get first past the
post. As he also managed to pilot the fleet-footed
descendant of Carbine into first place in the valuable
Essendon Stakes, the M.L. boy must have raked in
a tiny mountain of cash as the result of his brief
visit to Australia.
* * * *
A great struggle is going on just now
between the leading M.L. horse pilots for premier
position this season. The four leading riders so far
arc R.: Berry, 39 wins ; B. Deeley, 34 wins ; H. Gray,
31 wing; and W. Bell. 30 wins. It looks as if it’s
roirg to be a neck-and-neck scramble; but Deeley,
who pilots for that mighty racer, George Clifford,
will probably be astride the best horseflesh, and
should therefore have the best chance.
It’s not often that anyone puts money on
a prad at the lengthy odds of 2000 to 1 and col-
lects the cash ; but this happened in Fogland when a
horse called Form showed a field of seven the wav
home in the Four-Year-Old Selling Hurdle Race at
Hurst Park early in January. The winner had
belied his name to such an extent that a disgusted
bookmaker named Johnny Haynes offered to lay
£lOO to a bob about him ; and the owner of the
misnamed moke took the bet. To the surprise of
everyone, the horse flew home, and the booky paid out.
The frequency of falls at the Rosehill Race-
course, near Parramatta (N.S.W.), on Saturday last
makes one take thinking exercise. Three horses went
down in the Hurdle Race, one in the Carlingford
Mile and eight in the Rosehill Cup. The latter was
a very bad smash, and resulted in Olive Green, the
mare, having all four legs fractured. There was
nothing for it but to fire a cannon at the unhappy
animal and send her to Heaven. W. 11. Smith, the
rider of’Aurofodina, was desperately injured. In addi-
tion to shock, all the ribs on one side were stove in,
some of them being broken in two places, and the
jagged ends tore his lungs and lacerated his liver.
At time of writing his condition is considered pre-
carious, and it well might be. The frequency of falls
suggests that the course, which was saturated by the
heavy downpour on Friday night, was not fit to be
raced on. It is a slippery place in wet weather; and
the club carries a heavy moral responsibility for
allowing the meeting to take place under the circum-
stances. Of course, a postponement would have
meant much denunciation on the parts of the owners
and some of the gentry who carry the bag—but
better that than the thing which happened.
The real contests for this year’s billiard
championship—although no title or match money be
involved —will be when Reece comes to Australia
presently and plays a series of games with Fred
Lindrum. Lindrum, who has been lying low of late,
is said to have improved his game, and between the
styles of him and Reece there is not a point of
attractiveness to choose. They are both excellent.
* * * *
Something of a sensation was occasioned
when Norman IT. Murray resigned the position of
Commodore of the Royal Prince Alfred Yacht Club
(Sydney) last week. He has been a flag officer for
years and years and years. It is generally under-
stood that the resignation is the outcome of the rejec-
tion of his proposal to establish a one-class, one-
design type of yacht for Sydney Harbor. At consider-
able expense and a lot of trouble he secured from
the well-known yacht architect, Fife, complete plans
for an eight-metre yacht; and offered them to the
club. That creature rejected his scheme and his
plans, mainly on the ground of expense, and adopted
the Tasmanian one-class, one-design type of boat. So
Murray handed in his papers.
The winners of the Oxford-Cambridge
boat race, due on the 28th, will have to consider a
challenge from the rowing crew of the Sorbonne.
The Frenchmen are willing to meet the English
University crew on the Thames course.
There seems to be no earthly reason to
waste a year to get a taste of life in the Antarctic
or the Arctic. All that is necessary is to get on
board a trusty aeroplane and climb 15,000 feet
towards Heaven. A fellow who recently did it in
England found the temperature 38° below zero.
His petrol froze, his face was frost bitten, his eyes
turned black, and his plane became a mass of ice.
Then the engine stopped and he started back to earth
at an awful bump. But he kept a cool head, which
was easy in such circumstances, and when he got
clear of the upper strata his planes responded to the
levers, and he volplaned down to safety with his
effects still thick upon him. Then he started for
hospital. It is only a question of time till some
cloud scaler crashes down 25,000 ft. on to a train,
or through the roof of a pub, or collides with a
parson driving to church. Then there will be a row
if it is ever decided what it was that fell from
Heaven. By the way, a recent magazine story dealt
with the case of a flier who came down from a vast
height with his head bitten off. The event moved
another man to investigate 30,000 ft. up. He didn’t
come down at all, but he dropped his notebook, in
which he stated that flabby air animals with beaks
were all round him, and had been shepherding him
for hours. TTe wrote that it was a horrible, horrible,
horrible way to die.
Malt Wells and Tom McCormick do a little
billygoating at Sydney Stadium.
The commencement of Sydney’s 12th round
of First-Grade Cricket was a very sodden business
which spelt disaster for batsmen. Just consider some
of the bowling figures. Toby, of Sydney, smashed
seven Petersham batsmen for 17 runs. Macartney,
Gordon’s cherub, took six Waverley sticks for seven
runs. Norman, of Balmain, bagged six Middle Har-
borites for 20. Shepherd, of Waverley, blew out five
Gordonites for 35. Massie, of University, slew five
Redfernites for 48. At batting Redfern was easily
best, with 180 and three University wickets for 31.
Next came Paddington with 172 and no North Sydney
wickets for two runs. Western Suburbs accumulated
128 for the loss of five wickets off Glebe. Gordon
dismissed Waverley for 67, and then accomplished 70
for the loss of eight men. Balmain outed Middle
Harbor for 81, and battered 109 on the board for
eight wickets. Sydney blotted out Petersham for 54
and then smote 81 for three. The highest individual
score was the 55 of Pratten, of Western Suburbs.
Coffey, of Sydney, was next with 50; and Ward
Prentice, of Western Suburbs, with 44, third. But in
spite of the Noah’s Ark conditions there were only
14 ducks abroad during the afternoon, although the
number of men who never got past single figures was
legion.
* * * *
Harry Hilliard, the oldest Australian
representative cricketer, was last week bowled by
Father Time after a well-spent innings of 87 years.
The dead veteran had a place in the first inter-State
match between N.S.W. and Victoria 57 years ago.
Almost to the end Hilliard was an ardent cricketer,
and he regarded it as a severe misfortune if he missed
a single inter-State game.
*•***.
This scribe hates to heave bricks at the
N.S.W. Cricket Association, but when it starts to
butt its head against the Australian Compulsory
Training scheme he rises to kick it right on the
chin. The executive deplores the inroads the Defence
movement is making into cricket on Saturday after-
noons. Also, the executive has taken steps to place
the matter before the Defence authorities. Now let
us get to the root of the matter. If Australia has to
choose between Cricket and Defence, then Cricket must
go overboard without time to say its prayers. We
depend upon rifles, and not bats and balls, to keep
out invading rifles. Chasing inflated horse-hide and
smiting a leather sphere do not train boys how to
defend the honor of their mothers and sisters against
people armed with guns ; and the Cricket Association
makes an infernal and unpatriotic donkey of itself
by protesting against Defence training because it
interferes with a mere game. One doesn’t want to get
bitter, so some nasty things that might be said will
be left unwritten.
* * * *
During a lull in the Sydney rain deluge
on Saturday N.S.W. bowls pennant finals struggled
through on heavy greens. In the “A” grade Chats-
wood wiped the mud with City by 90 points to 83, in
spite of the fact that City threatened to make a
runaway win, and led by 21 to 12 and 38 to 29 in
the first half-hour. Chatswood pulled itself together,
took off its shirt, tightened its belt, moistened its
hands, and tramped right over City in three rinks out
of four. In the “B” section Parramatta put Dubbo
through the sausage-machine by 82 to 57, winning in
two rinks out of three. And therewith the Summer
Competition fell in the mud and expired, and left the
greens clear for the enthusiasts who struggle on the
cold days to the cheers of rheumatism, lumbago and
sub-acute arthritis.
* * * *
Cecil McVilly made small spuds of Fred
Coverdale in the Tasmanian amateur sculling cham-
pionship on the Derwent, on Saturday. The tussle
was important, as Coverdale holds the Australian ama-
teur championship, while McVilly, as holder of the
English Diamond Sculls, probably considers himself
something akin to a world’s record. Mac’s success
probably indicates that he will be the next Australian
amateur champion.
* * * *
James Clabby, who was to have been in
Sydney in time to box Jeff Smith on Easter Monday,
has cabled that he missed the boat, and will not
leave the United States of George Washington till
April 7. So Stadium-manager Keesing has fixed up
anew campaign, and at the end of it there will be
a Middleweight Championship of the World battle
between the man who gets the best of the campaign
and Clabby when he does arrive. On April 11 Dave
Smith will have a chance to redeem his pennon from
McGoorty, at list. 61b. at 2 p.m. in the afternoon
of the fight. On April 13 (Easter Monday night)
Pat Bradley will engage Jeff Smith at list. 41b. Then
the winners of these two battles will meet on a date
to be fixed, and the conqueror will fall on Clabby,
and knock saucepans out of him immediately he can
get fit after his arrival here. Dave Smith declares
that when he saw the serap Bradley put up against
McGoorty (Smith had previously beaten Bradley) he
felt an indignation with himself that made it unsafe
for him to look into a street mirror. And then when
he considered the showing of Jeff Smith —well, he
felt the last trace and shadow of the Indian Sign fade
right out. It sounds all very well, yet, somehow,
writer thinks it had been better to have matched
Dave Smith with Jeff of that ilk. But, of course,
David was anxious—most anxious to meet Eddie
McGoorty. Bradley and Jeff Smith are about as per-
fect a match as could be made. They should put up
a scrap worth going a long way to see.
Georges Carpentier, the French champion
boxer, struck trouble when he met the Afro-American
Joe Jeanette at Paris recently. Asa boxer Carpentier
seems to tower over Jeanette, and he hit the black
on the jaw as often as he liked, but failed to hurt
him. He knocked the black right off his feet in the
first round with a straight left to the jaw, but Joseph
arose as though nothing had happened. In the eighth
Carpentier had Jeanette all but out, but the gong
saved the negro. Apparently that was Carpentier’s
final effort, and the unwinding of it had burst his
boiler. For the next seven rounds he got weaker, and
Jeanette’s greater weight told increasingly as the
rounds went on. The fight came to an end in the
15th round, and the referee gave Jeanette the ver-
dict. “Snowy” Baker, who was present, cables that
the decision was putrid.
* * * *
The absurdity of a boxer “making
weight” was in evidence again at Brisbane on Satur-
day night. We saw a very bad exhibition of the
evil effects of it in the Sydney Stadium, when Herbert
McCoy beat Mat Wells. Wells had to waste
to reduce himself to the specified poundage,
with the result that he was too weak to
fight properly after 10 hurried rounds.
Morey, the Westralian, had to do the
same thing to get down to the weight
limit to meet Frank Thorn at Brisbane.
Same result—he went weak, and practi-
cally lost 15 out of 20 rounds.
The fight at Melbourne Stadium on Satur-
day night saw Lee Johnson, the colored featherweight
of Tahmmy Burns’s kennel, up against Australian
Charlie Simpson. It was a fast and furious battle, in
which Johnson utilised the ropes in a remarkable
manner. He would fall right against them, and as
he did so poke out his left towards the Victorian’s
jaw, and then the rebound of the ropes \#>uld send
him in (woosh !). But Simpson was clever, and he
worried our Timbuctoo brother badly. He hunted
him as a terrier hunts -a rat, but the trouble was
that when he caught his unresisting victim, the un-
resisting victim raised Cain. He suggested a Quaker
gone baresark. At the end of a fast fight referee
Marre could not see what the* difference was, and
wisely declared it a draw. The public agreed, which
was the only bad feature in the business. The public
ought to be prohibited from agreeing.
* * * *
When Matthew Wells, of Old Jewry,
passed into the welterweight division, he. requested
to be allowed to bash Tom McCormick, who had
wrested the championship of the British Empire from
the hard-fighting and prayerful Johnny Summers.
McCormick, or somebody on his behalf, replied scorn-
fully that Wells had better first prove himself able
to fight a welterweight. Wells took it quietlj', and
put it all over Ray Bronson, and then came forward
and reiterated his request. So they met last Satur-
day night at Sydney Stadium; and, in spite of-the
deluge, the huge place was comfortably packed to
see what everybody knew would be a stirring scrap.
For Mac, when he does fight, fights with all his heart
and soul, both fists, and his brain. The first two
rounds quickly convinced McCormick that at long-
range fighting he was no match for the polished
Hebrew glove artist; so he abandoned long range
for close work. He crowded in on Wells like a rail-
way collision. Wells, who didn’t want to be tele-
scoped, blew across the ring like a fleeting shadow,
trying to get room to swing, and McCormick leaped,
bounded and flapped after him. Wells turned and
drove a left of some importance into McCormick’s
face. Mac reeled, but as he did he smote three times
with both hands, and slogged occasionally with the
right; and then he got hold of Wells, who tugged to
get away. It was a mad scene, in which blows flew
so fast they could not be seen. But they could be
heard, and also the grunt with which McCormick let
them fly. A voice said from the back: “This is A
FIGHT!” Determined to accomplish a victor}' with
the same short-arm jolt that had proved so disastrous
to Johnny Summers, McCormick bored in all the time.
But he had no hope. The quick-brained Hebrew anti-
cipated him every time. He knew exactly what
McCormick was after, and knew how to prevent him
getting it. They went round and round the ring
together, bent down, head to head, each smiting,
guarding, smashing, looking for a chance for a knock-
out—a chance which never came. The first five rounds
were fought at a terrific pace, which, had it been
maintained, meant they would both drop dead inside
16 rounds. But in the sixth Wells commenced to
slow down. Many thought his strength was begin-
ning to go, and that McCormick would wear him
right out, and then do a great act of obliteration.
McCormick looked as if he had already selected the
house he would buy with the prize-money. This head-
to-head fighting was interesting to those who could
see it, but it was so close only those in the near seats
saw anything, and at the back the crowd howled to
the new referee to “break the cows!” Still, there
was a good deal of wrestling, which detracted from
the spectacular effect; although it was wild and fierce
enough to satisfy any glutton. During the fight there
was a terrific downpour of rain, accompanied by
flashes of lightning that filled the Stadium with a
blue glare. The roaring of the crowd, the crash of the
rain on the roof, and the booming of the thunder
made pandemonium. The fighters became hasty, dis-
ordered and inconsiderate. Sometimes they were even
previous. Harold Baker had to tear them apart, and
immediately he ripped one off the other they mixed
again in a malignant fashion. Up to the 12th round
it had been a fairly level riot, but in the 13th Wells
began to attack with systematic fury. He kept out
of clinches when he could, and smote McCormick with
all the books of the major and .minor prophets. He
had slowed down a lot, and was fighting far more
scientiflcially than Mac, whose one desire seemed to be
to catch him; but when he did catch the Hebrew he
could do nothing with him. Wells was obviously sav-
ing himself for a final blizzard. The 18th found
McCormick on top of the elusive Jew in bulldog
fashion. In close fighting he rained showers of
short, righthand slaps on to Wells’s neck and cheek ;
but they apparently produced no effect, except that
One of Wells’s ears began to swell like a balloon.
McCormick’s nose was cut, and both were bleeding
slightly from the mouth. But although McCormick
was always on top, it was Wells who scored in the
break-away. In the 19tli Wells unfolded his blizzard.
He bashed with the right and followed it up with
the left; and, driving McCormick across the ring
with a regular bombardment, landed twice with the
left and once with the right. McCormick tried to get
hold of him, to force him into some desirable posi-
tion, but Wells poured him off and handed him
several more that Mac could easily have done without.
Then came the 20th. The wallops that Matthew
let fly were fearful to see. He climbed over McCor-
mick and made him look a regular novice. He smote
till he seemed almost as numerous as the Public Debt.
McCormick was in no danger of a knock-out, but he
was getting a considerable depreciation, and a lot
was being written off for wear and tear. When
the gong went the sombre-looking Harold Baker placed
his hand on the head of Wells, and the thunders of
applause drowned the storm.
It has been described as an uninteresting fight.
Writer found it wildly exciting, and the way Wells
guarded his jaw in the close battling was wonderful.
The more one sees this shaggy Hebrew fight, the
greater becomes the admiration for his cleverness.
He has now quite abandoned the galloping style of Par-
thian conflict that he showed against Hughie Mehe-
gan. He goes right in with all the delight of a
vainglorious chicken charging a mud puddle. He
hits a harder blow than McCormick, whose fighting
is spoilt by his over eagerness. Mae was not satisfied
with the verdict, and his manager felt he had been
outraged or disembowelled or something. So there
is going to be another scrap between them, and it
should draw a vast house. Meanwhile, Matthew Wells
is welterweight champion of the British Empire.
“Famous Fights at the Stadium.” This
is the title of a bright, breezy history of the great
glove battles at Sydney Stadium from the Burns-
Squires battle till the Eddie McGoorty-Jeff Smith dis-
turbance. Told by C. A. Jeffries, and reprinted from
the Sporting Page of The Bulletin. Chock-full of
portraits of ring celebrities, including some of the
old-time giants, such as Peter Jackson, “Griffo” and
Bob Fitzsimmons. Good terms to booksellers. To be
retailed at One Shilling. Order from The Platypus
Press, 117 Bathurst Street, Sydney.*
Violence is so cheap that it is a wonder
more people don’t go in for it instead of
hockey, football and so forth. A market
gardener, of Elsternwick (Vic.), lately de-
cided that his missus needed a little correc-
tion. He offered her a cup of whisky, of
which she drank a little to please him. Then,
because she would not swallow the lot, he
started his performance, the programme be-
ing as follows: (1) He called her foul names
and struck her on the side of the head.
(Just here the cup was returned at high
speed, but unfortunately missed.) (2) He
struck her several times on face and head.
(3) She ran out of the house, and he hit
her and knocked her down. (4) She got up
and ran—he knocked her clean off her feet.
(5) While she lay on the footpath, he
kicked her on the breast and body. The
Law’s price for that generous allowance of
language, stoush and boot was a mere £5.
And a little more boot (say an extra ss.
worth) might have resulted in a dead wife
and a hanging. Either murder should be
made cheaper, or else the Jaypee should be
forced to revise his trade price-list. At
present the discrepancy is too great.

From an old hand on the bridge:—
Some feeble protests have been made against the
flicker ads. that blaze out round Sydney Harbor, but
I am surprised there hasn’t been something more
decided and emphatic from brother-skippers. The
ferry-men who live with these lights, may get used
to them; but the inter-Stater, who makes Sydney
every two or three weeks, finds them absolutely dan-
gerous. It’s not so much that they get mixed up
with the ordinarv guiding lights, and they sometimes
do that. It’s the impossibility of ignoring them
that gives us the great trouble. The eye of the
sailor is involuntarily attracted to a moving light;
that is why occulting lights are so much better than
fixed ones for nautical marking. In Sydney Harbor
a shipmaster needs to have his eyes at his full dis-
posal all the time —not to have them dragged con-
stantly aside by a flaring ad. when .lie should be
watching for a crowded ferry boat dashing out from
the Quay. These .things bring Sydney’s big ferry
smash very much nearer; and you get a hint every
few weeks that it’s pretty near already.
From London: —
More and more do we find persons of unimpeach-
able birth plunging into the sordid morasses of trade.
According to a local sheet the Marquis of London-
derry sells coal; Lord Raleigh’s dairies are famous ;
scores of peeresses and their daughters run hat shops ;
the Duke of Mficklenberg-Schwerin is a “motor
agent,” as also is King Peter of Servia. The King
is the Belgrade rep. of a leading French automobile
firm. He also owns the leading barber’s saloon
and drug store of Belgrade. The Kaiser is the proprie-
tor of a restaurant at Potsdam, and one of his sub-
sidiary monarchs, King William of Wurtemberg,
owns a couple of hotels, one in Stuttgart and the
other in the Black Forest. It is still impossible, I
understand, to get shaved by a king, or be served
with soup by an emperor, but these joys will doubt-
less come when the money-hunger gets a little more
violent in royal circles. It is already the simplest
thing in the world to be cheated at cards by a duke
or even a prince.

Are Your Takings Insured Against Loss?
EVERY business man insures his stock and his premises
against fire —why ? Not because he thinks his place
will be burnt down, but because it migHt. It
is because of this ever-present possibility of loss that he
considers it worth paying a big premium each year to
feel safe. Yet, statistics show that only three shop-
keepers in 1000 sustain loss through fire.
Your money is more important than your stock,
because yon sell your stock to get money— to make
Profit. Why, then, do yon throw your money into a
drawer, uncounted and unprotected, when it is subject to
mistakes, carelessness, etc., that burn up your profits and
lose your money ?
You are probably more than paying the premium for
an up-to-date National Cash Register which will insure
your money —stop mistakes and losses and make your
assistants more careful. If these claims are true they
certainly mean money to you, you will admit. AsK \is
to prove them.

Business, Robbery, etc.
“Wallaby”. When I chided “Mil” for overdraw-
ing (B. 6/11 /’l3) the prospects of the Torres Syn-
dicate in its pearling and trepanging venture 1
wasn’t far wrong. The syndicate chartered the small
steamer Waihoi, and after fooling round for an age
or so set off on the mystery cruise for lame and
fortune. It returned with neither. Since then the
boat has been laid up in Darwin, and the skipper
and engineer are returning to Sydney to battle for
the recovery of their salaries. Yet hundreds of
Malays and such like are making good money at the
game with pearlshell, trepang, etc. The aforemen-
tioned skipper tells me he has hopes of getting a Sydney
syndicate with coin behind it to send him back, as
lie is convinced that, if properly managed, the
bottom of the Northern seas still offers a tidy little
fortune for those hardy enough to hang on and dig
for it. #
St. Mary’s, C.E., North Melbourne, is building eight
workmen’s" cottages next door to itself on a block of land
which it owns. North Melbourne is a working man’s
suburb, so the spec, should pay, just as offices, stores,
etc., pay around St. James’ C.E. in Melbourne city. It
is a wondrous soft proposition when one comes to eye
it carefully. The State provides the land ; the State
Savings Bank will find the money; and practically
all the Church has to do is to sit down and draw
the profits. It is even easier and more lucrative
than passing round the plate.
* * * *
The Bulletin’s old friend, “Bleeds” Fitchett, of
Life newspaper, has launched out at as a sort of agent
for the Standard Oil Trust. He is booming in his
journal the Red Cross Paraffin Co., which supplies
an infallible cure —the usual old infallible cure —for
internal disorders. Says Fitchett artlessly: “As a
matter of general interest, the publisher of Life will
be glad to learn of the experience of any reader
who tries the new paraffin treatment.” Presumably
the idea is to lure out of some unsuspecting absorber
of the mixture revolting details as to his or her
disorders prior to the taking of the cure, together
with descriptions of the way the thing operated.
Then, as like as not, Mr. Fitchett will incorporate
all such disclosures in a hook entitled “Oils that
Saved the Empire’s Inside,” and draw a stupendous
profit therefrom. As he is selling his oil at 2s. 6d.
and 4s. 6d. per bottle, he should make a very fair
fortune before the material for his book is collected.
A sound business man is Bleeds.
* * * *
An apparently successful trial has been given, out-
side Adelaide, to a fire-cart, for discouraging mallee
shoots and such things, that spring up in
lately cleared ground, and make a mess of crop
and reapers if left. The fire is'on a grating, and
as it is driven along strong revolving fans drive the
flames downwards, and anything on the ground gets
the experience of being struck by lightning. The
inventor claims that the blasted things will never
grow again. Any use for prickly-pear ?
The Vic. Government lately sent three depart-
mental officers to the Western District to look at
18,000 acres of well-grown red-gum. The timber lies
along the Glenelg river above Horsham, and is worth,
at present prices, about £200,000. It appears that
certain philanthropic persons have spoken softly to
certain Ministers, and that there is a possibility of
the block being thrown open for selection. As the
land, sold in that fashion, would bring the State
less than £20,000, the fact that the Government
considered the application for one instant longer than
it would take to kick the applicants into the street,
looks like prima facie evidence of senile decay or
fatty degeneration of the brain on the part of those
responsible. There is one other possible explanation,
of course, but it is the sort of thing that a kindly,
well-disposed journal hates to mention.
* * » *
“Kink”: With my ticket in Tatt.’s sweep on the
Hobart Cup I received a programme of 22 events
or gambles for this year. Some of these are sub-
scribed four and six times over, and the 10s. 4d.
sweep brings to the promoters £50,000. At the
lowest computation the post office thus handles per
annum £575,000, devoted to a purpose which its
own laws and regulations declare to be illegal.
Ten per cent, of that— i.e., £57,600 —goes to the
promoters, £28,750 is seized by the Tasmanian
Government as a tax, and the remainder is divided
among the prizewinners. All the Commonwealth gets
out of it comes by the sale of a few thousand pennv
stamps. Why should the Australian Government not
take the business over and grab that £57,500 profit?
! It would make a very respectable endowment for the
pprt Augusta-Port Darwin railway.
A STATESMANLIKE POLICY!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The debt of the Ma State is officially returned at
£110,000,000 odd. Holman cheerfully estimates a
coming annual loan expenditure of between
£8.000,000 and £9,000,000. „ . ,
The interest bill of the Ma State is about
£4,000,000 per annum. .
Mr. Cook, Prime Minister, says (or implies) that
the Commonwealth is going to have a D— tight
year of it in finance. He “doesn’t know where the
money is to come from.” The “great dailies advise
Mr. Cook to borrow.
The total Public Debt of Australia is very nearly
£300,000,000. . , , .
The population of Australia, counting in babies,
deadbeats, old-age pensioners, niggers, lunatics,
criminals and Wowsers, is estimated at 4,818.000.
—News items compiled by ME.
When a State, or a nation, gets into a hole through
the shameful neglect of its leaders —
When the system of bribing by public dole finds
frequent and eloquent pleaders—
When journals that boast of their conscience and
soul make frenzied appeals to their readers,
To rush to the Jew
With a fresh I O U,
And add to their interest-breeders ;
The question arises—or else it falls flat —
What’s the best thing to do with a crowd like thati
When a poor little populace, wilfully blind and cal-
lously deaf to all warning,
Goes hugging itself as the best of its kind all
“foreigners” loftily scorning—
And, utterly careless of what it has signed, hopes
there won’t be a bill in the morning;
Or, if by bad luck,
For the cash it gets struck,
Trusts the Three Balls will still be adorning
The same glad old entrance, and same joyous mat —
What’s the best thing to do with a people like that?
When the only expedient that’s known to the State,
or the nation, or people, or party—■
Their shield and their buckler against hostile Fate ;
their charm against Nick and Astarte —
Their art and their passion, absorbing and great as
the passion for bridge and ecarte —
Is to raise a new loan
From some Hebrews unknown
With noses enormous and warty—
One can only inquire as he takes off his hat:
What’s the best thing to do with a party like that?
Yet there’s one consolation remaining; we choose in
rotation the persons who borrow
Year in and year out from the Lombard Street Jews,
anl ] — y OU m ay be that person to-morrow !
The method is simple; just mention your views of
finance, and say ’tis with sorrow
You note the amount
To our debit account
Is paltry ; we really should BORROW !!!••••
Pooh-pooh all the dead-and-gone boomsters who’ve
gat,
And ask. “What’s the use of a small loan like that?
K.S.W.

23 and 25 HUNTER STREET, SY»NEY.
The Dandenong (Vic.) Foreshore Trust
has eliminated a lot of joy from local seaside
life by • the regulation that “No person
clothed in bathing costume shall sit, lie,
loiter on, or run along the beach or seashore,
but shall proceed in a direct line to-or from
the dressing place.” Even mixed bathing
loses half its attractions under- these strin-
gent conditions. The touch of Tango parade
is wanting. The “loiter” clause is especially
severe. Recently a dreamy girl in a nice
new outfit was summoned for having paused
to wonder whether she was proceeding in a
bee-line to her dip or had diverged a trifle.
“Hurry On”: He has gone now. Most
probably he is dead of heart disease ; but I
never will forget how he used to run for his
morning boat with his whiskers fluttering
in the wind and the perspiration ruining liis
freshly-starched collar. We early risers
used to see him charging afar off, as the
tram rumbled along the last stage to the
ferry wharf. If lie had been 10 years
younger, decently slim and clean-shaven, the
sight would have been less alarming ; but it
is terrible to see an elderly man throwing
himself through the atmosphere and only
hitting the pavement occasionally with his
hot feet in order to shoot himself forward
with greater force. Morning travellers who
affected to know, said that he occupied a
high and unnecessary post in the civil ser-
vice and could have roamed coolly into his
office at any hour. But he chose to rise late
and annov less energetic people by flying
and bounding for our absurdly early ferry.
I "think”" the malediction"that finishedhim was
launched by the lame man who does all the
growling for the suburb. “D —n him!” he
whined, one windless' summer morning.
“D—-n him! there he goes again! The
sight of that brute sweating for nothing on
a morning like this is enough to, drive a
man mad ! I hope to G —d he breaks his
neck !” The remark was received with chill
and decorous silence by all hands. But in
our hearts we thoroughly agreed with the
lame man.
It is still possible to be sent to gaol in
N.S.W. for debt— 1 -though the law euphemisti-
cally calls it “disobedience to an order of the
Court.” Also, one may be gaoled for “costs”,
and for not cashing up libel damages. If the
proposition be accepted that imprisonment
for debt is a barbarous anachronism, then,'
plainly, what N.S.W. wants is a clear,
definite legal position, making it above argu
ment that a creditor who trusts cannot, un-
less he is able to show fraud, employ court
machinery and the prison cell to collect his-
money. Nearly 100 years ago Sydney was.
grievously troubled over this question. Magis-
trates and prison officials were turned freely
into creditors’ thumbscrews. After a period
of violent kicking, N. S. Wales got
a law passed which caused the cred'tor
to abate his fury. The Act of March
30, 1825, laid it down that “debtors
imprisoned by their creditors must be
maintained by them during their confine-
ment in-gaol.- and, in default, must be dis-
charged.” It was a backhanded wav of
meeting the grievance, but it was splendidly
successful. When the creditor ran down
George-street (the gaol was then in George-
street), with his debtor’s dinner in covered
dishes, he was, if a few minutes late, in-
formed that the prey had been discharged.
“Snell” : The pictures of the Balkan war,
now being made visible in Australia, satisfy
us on one' point. Modern war* doesn’t offer
much scope even for the operator who is
willing to push his barrow into the thick of
it. So far as human eye can see, there is no
thick of it. It is a matter of great spaces.
It is thin, like clear soup or ghosts. Enemies
miles apart throw hunks of metal at the
fellow who is 125 farms away. You see no
blows —only puffs of smoke and scattered
bodies of men moving in a matter-of-fact
way on the hill-sides. Some day even smok-
ing will be prohibited, and a notice to that
effect will be nailed on the side wall of every
battle. Later on there will be a notice re-
questing soldiers not to give tips to the
enemy, and stating that any enemy fpund
accepting 6d. will be sacked by the mjfnagc'
ment. Looking closely, you obserye- ' that
small, brown objects fall out of the lines,
and take it lying down. They are shot
soldiers, but they don’t look a bit like it.
They just look like dropped baggage. It’s
only the faked American war film that actu-
ally looks like fighting. But the effects of
war are all" there in the' Balkan films—the
battered towns, and the butchered and
broken men. The latter you get in the am-
bulance ' scenes, ’ poor wretches villainously
mangled. And in. the end there is admira-
tion only for the deftness, speed and re-
source of the doctors and nurses who are
busy on the. repairs. -
THE CIVIL TOISTGUE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Professor Jordan, pacificist, of the Pacific Slope,
now in Australia, puts much faith in the civil tongue
as a means cf averting war. Canada and U.S.A., he
says, have fallen back bn the civil tongue, and he
is "of the opinion that if Australia is sufficiently ..civil,
it may get along very well without either army ■or
navy.
It is a simple gospel, boys,
This learned fellow carts around.
If blessed Peace we would enjoy
We need to learn a most profound
And courtly bow, to touch the ground
With brow obsequious
We’ll have to make a fuss
In pure politeness to the foe
Who may come in a year or so.
Instead of taking little boys,
And drilling them in burning suns,
With shouting captains and the noise
Of rude explosions from the guns,
Let’s lead indoors the little ones
And teach them suavity.
On humbly bended knee
The grim invader’s conscience rake
With offerings of tea and cake.
You’ll And if you are thrown among
A greedy Asiatic horde,
That every' time a civil tongue
Cuts deeper than a shining sword.
If yellow legions from abroad
Como on with frantic hate
You open wide the gate, .
■ ■ Anil smile, and sweetest welcomes sigh, .
And they will go away and cry!
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.

The, quickest wav to ascertain
this, WITHOUT EXPENSE, is
to write to me describing vour invention, enclosing
a small sketch where necessary. Nicety of drawing
is not essential ; all that is necessary at first is to
give me . YOUR IDEA.
I will at once inform you whether the idea is
patent-able, and, if so, give the necessary instructions

expected.
