IN BANANA LAND
“Rangan”: Bananaland’s Local Authori-
ties have followed the example of their
Government and gone on a severe borrowing
burst. To date they owe £3,270,000 to the
Treasury, which, for a population of about
600,000, is good going. The yearly interest
and redemption eats up £200,000 odd. The
Brisbane Water Board heads the list with a
debt of £781,000; followed by the Rockhamp-
ton Harbor Board, £305,000 (incidentally
this body recently suspended all operations
bar collecting revenue to pay up some back
debts) ; then the Townsville Harbor Board,
£304,000. Compared with revenue, the debt
is absurdly large. Thus Gympie’s income is
£5OOO and debt £39,000; Rockhampton Har-
bor Board’s income, £30,000, and debt
£305,000; and there are a dozen others whose
interest and redemption eat anything up to
50 per cent, of their income. Naturally, the
interest is, in many cases, much overdue.
For instance, six out of the 32 central sugar
mills are in arrears to the extent of £87,000.
That’s not much, of course, especially when
all you’ve got to do is book it up to pos-
terity.
A Bunch of Grapes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cluster of purple lanterns, prisoning
The filtered sunshine. Rubies red a-swing
Dropped from some rushing genie’s treasure
load.
Or just a pedlar-fairy’s saddle bags
Hung from the back of an indignant toad
Who, ’spite the thistle-rowelling, sourly
lags.
Or a mere bunch of grapes. A hard, green
thing
That swelled throughout the soft grey rains
of Spring
And slowly caught the sunset’s blush, or
stored
A molecule of sunlight from each noon,
Thus, every mist-expelling morning, scored
Against the cold white searching 6f the
moon.
Press with your finger! Burst the pedlar’s
bag!
A strip of green flesh oozes like a rag,
And one red drop that stains your finger’s
white,
Where to the guarding knuckle slips a ring—
Is this all it has woven of the light?
Is this all that it holds of lavish Spring?
Ah ! no! Just shut your eyes and I will tell.
Across the windless desert sounds a bell.
Like echo from some hidden pasture lands
A wooden bell with sunset in its call;
And cutting black triangles in the sands
From the grooved pyramids the shadows fall.
Here the dun camel with his swaying head,
The little pointed tent, the carpet spread
Where prone upon his beard the Arab fell.
Do you think Allah laughs to see him
crouch
Thanks abject, for scant portion in the well
Or few dry raisins from the goat-skin pouch?
What else the grape heart holds? A sloping
hill;
Straight vine poles in an acre, sun-steeped
still,
A black-browed girl with gold rings in her
ears,
Brushing from her bare breasts the brown-
winged wasps
Lured by the purple rings the grape juice
smears,
Down to the waist a swarthy lover clasps.
And yet again. An arbor in the South,
A lady with a wine stain on her mouth,
So still she lies in that deserted place.
In chequered fancies sun and shadow meet.
A rose blows down to kiss her fixed white
face;
A man’s glove blurs the marble at her feet.
Is there aught else the magic bunch can hold?
There comes a clink of glasses. Thatched
and old
The wine shop drowses in the summer haze,
Behind the lazy creaking of its board.
What devil made him pipe the “Marseillaise”
To ease his throat. . . .and stifle on a sword?
Cluster of purple lanterns, prisoning
The gleanings of full many a golden Spring;
The kiss of Egypt on her lover’s brow
Ere slid the pearl from wine-wet finger
tips....
Gather the close-packed jewels ! Spend them
now
To quench a summer drought upon your lips!
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, March 21, 1914.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
The Governor has been lying low on the
vice-regal bow lately. Even the celebrations
on the Saint-day of the most popular snake-
charmer in romantic history failed to draw
him into the open. I presume he is busy
packing up his kilt and sporran and his
Hielan’ bonnet preparatory to heading for
the West. He is not expected to return to
headquarters until the end of May.
Bouchard, M.L.A., and his proprietary
lady lent their charming old garden last
Thursday as the scene of a “coin tea” in aid
of the West End erbehe and kindergarten.
It was a sultry afternoon, so the cool, tree-
shaded lawns looked very inviting, but the
moneyed crowd didn’t turn up, or didn’t shell
out, for all the “coin” that eventuated
totalled £7 odd.
That same afternoon the Governor and
Lady Mac., attended by that ever-useful
handy-man, Private-Secretary Foxton, paid a
short-notice visit to the Women’s Residential
College. The executive connnittee had no
time to iron out its best (in fact
some of it hadn’t time to get there at all)
before his Ex. was laying it over the door-
knocker. However, he liked all the arrange-
SPECIAL ATTENTION.
ANTONIO (giving order): “Steak and the oys[?]! Spesh! For de cop—Peter de Murph!"
ments very much, and the fact that there is a
nice little laundry set apart for those girls
who care to wash out their own frillies
greatly charmed him. He wants women to be
domesticated.
Yesterday tliat same Executive entertained
at tea the University Professorial Staff, the
Advisory Board and all the feminine belong-
ings of the same, at the Women’s College.
In fact the University has had rather “a
grand slam” this week. It has been much in
the public eye since it started work on
Wednesday. Emmanuel College, the happy
hunting ground of the Scotchbyterians, had
an opening on Wednesday afternoon.
Prayers and a religious address followed by
tea. I did not stop to sample the tea, the
foregoing proceedings being very filling.
A lucky young man of the name of B. D.
Peren (who by the way is a grandson of the
late Sheriff Halloran) has persuaded Miss
Madge Woolcock to become Mrs. B. D. P. on
April 22, and go help him to grow apples at
Stanthorpe, where he owns a fruit Eden.
The tall, chic Madge has called her future
home “Tango”—l suppose she and the happy
man are devoted to that dull saraband. Miss
Woolcock is the elder daughter of barrister
John Woolcock, who was once upon a time
considered a likely candidate for the Wool-
sack, or whatever it is tliat judges are sup-
posed to camp upon when' they become Lord
Chancellors.
Being Lent, there are no society weddings
to record, but immediately after Easter the
knot-tiers of the Anglican Church will be
kept busy putting the old question: “Who
giveth this woman?” or “Who chanceth this
woman?” or whatever it is. On the 18th
of the month Dr. Love’s dainty little
daughter Marion will be handed over to
Mr. Williams. As he is somewhere about
6ft. 6in. (perhaps more) and she is quite
l|ft. shorter I don’t anticipate that he will
need any assistance. Still, you never can
tell. It is to be an evening wedding, and an
evening reception is to be held a few days
before the ceremony.
Our country town of Warwick has been on
a jamboree! The Picnic Races and two or
three balls all took place in the space of a
few days, and the smartest of the squat-
tocracy from Toowoomba to Stanthorpe
gathered together to eat, champagne, and hoe
things down generally. They are a cliquey
and exclusive lot, these Darling Downs squat-
ters, and consider themselves “county,” my
dear. Mr. Pikedale White is the President
of the Picnic Race Club, and his wife in a
gorgeously beautiful frock, handed out the
trophies to the owners of the winning Buce-
phaluses during an interval between dances
at the Club ball.
The races on St. Patrick’s Day attracted
the same old crowd, but the dressing was not
up to the usual standard observed on the 17th
of Ireland. For one thing it was too hot for
autumn finery, and for another, many of our
Smart Set are still in your inundated city.
Miss Macgregor put in an appearance clad in
a white silk frock, and a black velvet toque.
She was under the chaperonage of that pretty
young matron, Mrs. A. 11. Whittingham. A
striking gown was worn by a well-known or-
nament of the vaudeville stage. It was of
violet crepe de chine, much slashed and giving
a view of very dainty black satin shoes and
silk -stockings. With this the lady collabo-
rated a velvet coat of the same shade edged
with fur. This outfit, Thargomindah, on a
day when the heat-calculating machine was
humming at 90 degrees in the shadow. Still,
what is life without notriety? With that
reflection in my mind I must tell you that if
the King of the Belgians had happened along
last Tuesday, he would have found plenty of
work for his needle and cotton, in sewing up
split skirts. One sweet young vision
strolled about in blissful ignorance of the
fact that the indiscretions of her skirt re-
vealed a large hole in her well-filled silk
stocking.
The races this afternoon drew a very good
crowd to Ascot. The Governor was present,
accompanied by Lady and Miss Macgregor,
and attended by Captains Foxton and Talbot.
His Ex. gave a tea-party to about 16 of the
Quality, including Mrs. Real, Mrs. J. T. Bell
and one of her pretty daughters. Speaker
Armstrong, Hon. A. H. Whittingham (wifey
didn’t show up), Mr. Ramsey, Mr. and Mrs.
Archer and others. It was a very hot after-
noon, but again fur-trimmed garments were
donned by a few women. They were not
really smart garments, being of the “little
dressmaker” order. Fur trimming really re-
quires to be put on by the hand of an artist.
On Monday evening, at Rowe’s caf§, several
hundred strenuous folk, who fill in most of
their time teaching the young idea how to
go forth and meet its financial and social
obligations, gathered to express their un-
biassed opinion of the scientific Dr. Shirley
and his fragile-looking missus, and to load
them with a substantial addition to their
family plate-chest. His whack was a cabinet
of cutlery, and the lady received a case of
fish knives and forks, also dessert ditto.
Acting-Premier Barnes was prominent in the
scene, and said nice, flattering things to Dr.
Shirley, as also did Mr. R. If. Roe.
The energetic and popular Mrs. Papi, w r ho
has been secretary of the South Brisbane
Croquet Club for 13 years, has retired from
that position. Last Monday the members gave
her a silver jewel casket to hold the family
diamonds, a life-membership of the club, and
a steady job as vice-president.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Rangan”: Bananaland’s Prickly Pear
Commission returns this month after a tour
of the world. By 4he latest reports they
only appear to have been further convinced
that the porcupine shrub is the devil of a
proposition. The only definite method of
extermination received was given gratis by
a West Indian planter, and was to this
effect: “Put a fence round the , and
pray for a hundred years’ drought.” He
hadn’t tried it himself, but he cordially re-
commended it. The prickly pear, by the
way, is a Mexican contraption. It has
entered into the souls of the people, hence
their frequent rows. In Mexico the biggest
cactus becomes president.
“Rangan”: One of the objects of the
Papuan expedition under Clarke is to get
past the 610-mile mark on the Fly River,
the record put up by Governor Macgregor,
of Bananaland, in 1890. Macgregor is often
quoted as a fossil, but bis life has been far
more useful than that of the average emer-
gency royalty. In Papua he was always on
the go and personally helped in the exploit-
ing of the land. This scribe remembers being
amongst his party on a shooting expedition
there. A duck was swimming amongst the
reeds at about 60yds. Macgregor drew his
revolver and shot it clean through the head.
Mac. was the only one who expressed no
surprise. He calmly returned the revolver
to its place and motioned for one of the
“boys” to get the bird. No other Governor
has seen so much of Bananaland as Mac-
gregor, and he has the distinction of being
one of the two Government officials to have
ever set foot on Mornington Island, where to
the scandal of Minister Appel, the natives
wear no clothes and smile at all creation in
the Altogether.
Justice Tom a’Beckett, who is to act as
Vic. Chief Justice while John Madden is
away on his year’s leave, was, like his spruce
old friend, born in the purple of the law.
His father, T. T. a’Beckett, was admitted
as an attorney in England in 1829, and in
Victoria in 1851. The new Acting C.J. was
“called” or howled at in England in 1859,
and admitted in Victoria in 1860. So he
has done 54 years hard labor, mostly in the
Equity Court before Judge Molesworth.
Mr. Robin Kerr Clark, an Australian
squatter, has been selected by the
British Unionists to fight the Poplar
by-election on their behalf. Poplar is
a working-man’s constituency; but the
sort of British working-man that inhabits
it is such an incorrigible ass that
he is quite likely to support Mr. Clark.
His place is in N. S. Wales. He divided liis
time, while in Australia, between toiling
lightly on his station and performing the
more strenuous job of aide-de-camp to vari-
ous Governors. He married five years ago
Lady Beatrice Moore, the pretty sister of
Lord Drogheda, and went to live in England.
Summarised description of Australia’s new
Gov.-General, Munro-Ferguson, from two
columns by Sydney Sun’s London corre-
spondent :
Can wear gold lace.
Will bring to bear upon Constitutional and party
disputes the knowledge acquired in the House of
Commons.
Knows the Federal history of Australia because he
is an omnivorous reader.
Also the social life of the inhabitants, because
T. Carmichael, while Governor of Victoria, corres-
ponded with him. , ...
Likewise, the productive and industrial activities
of the continent, because he has seen letters sent to
Leith from sturdy immigrants.
He resembles the aforesaid T. Carmichael in his
chief characteristics and ways.
Has the same pawky humor.
Has a large, leonine head.
Although born of independent means, always
walked humbly before the Lord.
When canvassing perambulated the docks wearing
a rough jacket and a cutty pipe.
Men mistaking him for a workman voted for him.
Is elder of the parish church.
Was one of a crowd of 25,000 at Ivirkaldy to see
a football match played.
Now we know what we’re getting for our
£lO,OOO a year.

IN LONDON
will be glad to know of the numerous
amenities and pleasant sides of hotel
life presented by the Hotel Russell.
The visitor feels more like coming
into the atmosphere of English home-
life at its best than entering an hotel.
c There is that domesticity of service
and general environment that make for supreme comfort and homeli-
ness. The cooking is faultless ; the style and scheme of furnishing
luxurious but restrained ; the tariff modest.
C At the centre of London’s throbbing life,
it is yet peaceful and quiet, overlooking the
park-like Russell Square. A penny ’bus fare
from almost any spot of interest in London

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MSS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.
Jackeroo: If this catches your eye (you
are the man who supplied a par in The Bulletin of
17/2/1910, and your address has long since gone
astray), this paper would be glad to hear from you.
A highly respectable legal firm seeks you as a wit-
ness, having recognised that on certain matters you
are an expert... .Worker: (1) Mr. Higgs, by his own

said to the chairman, “Will you protect me?” Which

war-horse, whereas he was only a young loser of the
’bus, somebody had a right to be annoyed. . . .Dux:

“T. Tufthunter” : A visiting soap baronet
advised Australia the other day to get its
“donkey work” done by colored labor, and
suggested that the white man would then be
a gilded person of great consequence.
Queensland, a little less than 40 years ago,
had nearly achieved the soap baronet’s ideal
of an earthly paradise. But it somehow
didn’t work oiit right for the white man. In
the bush you' niet Chows on horseback and
white men humping bluey. The discolored
persons were everywhere, on stations, even
on the Darling Downs. I never saw them
on Felton (Tyson’s), but they were on Clif-
ton (Tooth’s), adjoining. On the Burnet,
Dawson, McKenzie, the Chow was supreme;
and he had the aborigines poisoned with his
opium (charcoal) everywhere. Even the
washerwoman was cut out by the Chows. It
was quite common for a white man, when
asking for a job, to be told he was
the wrong color. In Brisbane almost
every sort of little business was run
by discolored persons, and you would
see Kanaka tigers flunkeying on the car-
riages of the plutocrats. A poor white man,
instead of being a person of consequence, was
merely trash. I worked on a sugar planta-
tion in 1874, and there were about three
Kanakas to every white man. The sugar
boiler was a white. So was the engineer.
He got 30s. per week ! There was a German
overseer of the field Kanakas, and he had to
be very civil, as cane knives are sharp. There
was only one Kanaka woman among the lot,
and they all pigged together. The wages
of the white trash were at the rate of £4O
per year and rations —plenty of black sugar
(crawling with maggots), flour, beef and tea.
Any thing else was extra. When, therefore,
the Levers tell us to get colored people in to
do our “donkey work,” we can tell him that
we tried that scheme when he was a
youngster in short pants, and an ounce of
hard experience is worth a ton of soft soap.
Lever never tried niggers in England, so he
is a mere amateur at the game.

PRELIMINARY ANNOUNCEMENT.
The management wish to inform the public
that they will shortly open the Imperial as
a Fashionable Dancing Academy, and con-
duct it on even more exclusive and select
lines than characterised its operation as a
Rink.
EXCLUSIVE AND FASHIONABLE.
We will rigidly exclude anything that
might be termed, by discriminating persons,
as objectionable, whether in our clientele,
our management, or in the styles of dancing
we will allow.
REFINED AND SELECT.
All the popular dances, both old and new,
will be featured in our programmes, and the
most rigid propriety insisted upon at all
times. Only those dances which have re-
ceived the approval of polite society will be
presented to our patrons.
DANCING DE LUXE.
Our establishment will bear no similarity
whatever to anything of a like nature which
may have nreceded it. The building is
rapidly being converted into the most mag-
nificent “Palais de Danse” in the Southern
Hemisphere, and will contain the most lux-
urious and palatial ballroom in Australasia.

A Natural Remedy
Time was when disease was thought to be due to the
direct influence of evil spirits, and exorcism and magic
were invoked to cast it out.
Science has taught us wisdom. The evil spirits exist
still. We call them “Disease Germs,” and they also
must be cast out. Once lodged in the stomach or
intestines, fever with its hallucinations, or biliousness with
its aches and pains, is the result.
ENO S FRUIT SALT’
is the approved remedy for driving out disease germs.
Its action is quick and thorough. It clears the intestines,
rouses the torpid liver to new life, stimulates the mucous
membrane to a healthy action, and cleanses and invigorates
the whole digestive tract.
Be prepared for emergencies by always keeping a
bottle in the house.

The Inky Way.
The other morning Sydney Telegraph gave pro-
minence to the statement of its pet protege, Charles
Gregory Wade, that “Australians were quite as
brainy as any other people in the world, and the
effort should be made to keep their own men with
them always.” Meanwhile the Telegraph was
eagerly awaiting an imported editor from London to
take the place of Dr. Ward, who is about to retire
from the chair.
“Hamer” : The birth of the Labor daily has again
been put off, this time till November next. All was
in readiness for the event, barring the cash, and
the organisers realise that the cash proposition is a
big one. The A.W.U. members have decided on
another 10s. levy all round, and if the rest of the
unions don’t do likewise, there will be profound
grief, also surprise, in militant Labor circles. The
idea of an evening daily has been abandoned. The
new venture will arrive with the metropolitan milk,
or it will not arrive at all.
“Bullaganang”: I know what I’m going to do
when the Japs fall upon us in the North. I’m going
to cover my vulnerable parts with a copy of the
Dalby (Q.) Leader. Have a look at this:—
“Friday is a particularly busy day for the local
newspapers, as the Court is held regularly on that
day, and once every six weeks the Wambo Shire
Council meeting. Then again it is our publishing
day and more often than not it is, quite impossible
for us to get all the day’s proceedings in the one
issue. Might we suggest that the necessary action
be taken to have the Court held on some more con-
venient day ?”
What kind of shrapnel would be likely to penetrate
that ?
“Old Reporter”: The old Melbourne Police Court
in Swanston-street, which was like a small stone store
compared with the place just opened in Latrobe and
Russell streets, was a sort of club away in the ’6o’s
and ’7o’s of last century. The Rainbow Hotel on
the opposite corner of Little Oollins-street was the
bar-room, to which all hands but one —William
Handheld Whelan —adjourned when the business of
the . court was . over. Those were the. days when E. P.
Sturt and F. Call and Prendergast Haekett were
Police Magistrates, and Sergeant Pewtress the court
officer. Haekett took the civil side. One of the
bright spirits in the press box was William Lewis
O’Hea, of the Argus, who sometimes wrote things
worthy of Dickens. But the Argus affected an irri-
tating gravity, and O’Hea’s exuberance was frequently
snuffed out. Between “Billy” and Sergeant Pewtress,
who was a Londoner, but ought to have been born
in Tipperary, and the Irish policeman who kept the
door of the police van, the “parlor” of the Rain-
bow heard some wondrous yarns.
Earl Grey speaks sympathetically of one man in
London who told him that he became a member of
the Imperial Institute because it was a place where
he could always get hold of The Bulletin.

With grateful tears our eyes are dim.

O Lord, how could they spare himf

Chorus of Telegraph Directors:
The lily needs but little paint;
A gallon is enough.
(A show of dignified restraint
Assists a column puff).
“If I went out to Australia it would be in the hope
and with the intention of remaining permanently and
becoming an Australian.” — The prodigy himself
Sings ;
But, in spite of all temptations,
To belong to other nations,
I resisted splendid-lee,
Till I got a humble offer from the simple old
D.T.!” (Solo dance.)
Choir of Australians:
Though he found it hard all his tics to sever,
He hopes to Heaven he’ll stay for ever —
This London stranger so nice and tame.
(Perhaps his friends are hoping the same !)
(lively dance.)

THE NEWER JOURNALISM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A fire broke out in a grocer’s shop owned and
occupied by Mrs. Margaret Olson, at 59 Wortley-
street, Balmain, early this morning. The outbreak
was marked by a sensational rescue by Mr. Andrew
Olson, who carried his mother through a .
• —Evening paper.
Too long in phrases cut and dried
To be sensational we’ve tried —
To galvanise the reader’s hair
With tales of fire and flood and air.
We’ve toiled with words to reproduce
The dreadful scenes, but what’s the use
When by suggestion we can gain
Effects without the slightest strain.
“Last night, some passers-by observed
Flamgs issuing from 53
Brown-street. The owner was unnerved,
On waking ’midst a raging sea
Of flames. But there was just one hope.
He fastened to the bed a sheet,
And, sliding down his snowy rope,
He landed safely in the .”
“While passing by a waterhole
A workman heard a cry for help.
In sudden haste he blessed his soul,
And gave a lusty, answering yelp.
But, when he reached the clayhole’s brim,
He saw but bubbles on the pool.
And just because he couldn’t swim,
He called himself! ! ? ! ! t”
“The residents of ?? street
Were last night when heard
A loud explosion x x x feet,
Police regard corpse %% — absurd.
Rain of fragments fell;
Widow heard to wildly shriek,
‘Oh, !! ! xx Hell!’
Fined ten shillings or a week.”

Do Your Meals Fit
Do you feel snug and comfortable around
your waistline after a hearty meal ?
Did your last meal taste deliciously good
to you, and did you eat all you wanted?
Could you have patted your rotundity in
glee and felt proud of your appetite and
of your good, strong stomach? Do you
feel rosy now because your last meal
gave you no inconvenience whatever? If
not, you have Dyspepsia in some form,
and probably never realised it.
If you have the least trouble in your
stomach after eating, no matter how
little or how much you eat, there is
trouble brewing, and you must correct
it at once.
Most all stomach troubles come from
poor, weak, scanty gastric juice, that
precious liquid which ought to turn your
food into rich, red blood.
If you have Nausea, your gastric juice is
weak. If you have sour risings or belcli-
ings, your food is fomenting; your gas-
tric juice is weak. If you have loss of
appetite, your gastric juice is weak. If
you have a bloaty feeling of aversion to
food, your gastric juice is weak.
You need something in your stomach to
supply the gastric juice which is scanty,
and to give power to the weak gastric
juice.
Dr. Sheldon’s Digestive Tabules do this
very thing.
Now, think, one grain of one of the in-
gredients of these wonderful little tab-
ules digests 3000 grains of food. They
are several times more powerful than the
gastric juice in a good, strong, powerful
stomach. They actually digest your food
for you. Besides, they increase the flow
of gastric juice—just what you need to
get all the good possible out of every-
thing you eat. You will never have that
“lump of lead” in your stomach nor any
other stomach trouble after taking Dr.
Sheldon’s Digestive Tabules. Then every-
thing you cat will be digested; they will
give you strength, vim, energy and a
rosy dispositon. You’ll feel good all
round your waist line after every meal,
and they will make you feel good all
over.
Dr. Sheldon’s Digestive Tabules will
make you feel happy after eating a good,
hearty meal. Take one or two after eat-
ing. You’ll feel line, then your meals
will fit, no matter what or when you eat.
You should always have a tin of Dr.
Sheldon’s Digestive Tabules on your din-
ing-table. They will make you get all
the good possible out of everything you
eat, and you’ll enjoy it.
Make Your Stomach Strong 8 Healthy
Dr. Sheldon’s Digestive Tabules are sold everywhere at 1/6 per tin of 36 Tabules,
and 2/6 per tin of SO Tabules. Buy a tin, eat a good, square meal, and then take
the Tabules according to directions, and note the result. You will forget all about
it if you are not careful, for there will be No Pain or disturbance, and the food will
be Digested just as it used to be when your Stomach was Well and Strong. Fur-
thermore, your stomach will soon be restored again, if you keep on taking them,
just as thousands upon thousands of other stomachs have been, by the sole and
exclusive use of Dr. Sheldon’s Digestive Tabules.

The Bloques of Elysium.
THE APPEARANCE OF EVIL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
TIE President of our bachelor estab-
lishment was a man of affliction,
being alternately consumed with fiery
energy or overcome with indolence. Pre-
sumably the periods of inactivity were due
to exhausted nature settling down to recover
from the orgies of work in which the Presi-
dent revelled while feeling good. While he
Vas lazing Elysium was certainly more like
its name than when he was in a rushing
tnood: for just as he could infect us with
the dolce far nicnte snirit while he was re-
poseful, so did he instil into us some of his
superabundant energy. I have often won-
dered to whose immortal account the lan-
guage he used in doing it will be charged
The President loved roses and asphodels
and he made our home a paradise of them'
ft was in the up-keep of that garden he
fflade us work, and thereby kept our bodies
healthy while he lost his own soul.
* * * *
It was Sunday morning., and the only
Sound of toil in Elysium was the hum of
innumerable, bees among the roses. Under
the shade of tkd pepperwuod tree the Babe
played with thd toys we had bought her the
previous day ; and on the nearby lounge old
jLihys smoked and read, A few feet away
Little Joe, tiie Auvite, was busy preparing a
piece of oak for photographic purposes, hav-
ing lately taken to printing his master-
pieces on wood. The rest of the Honorable
Order of Bloques were scattered about the
lounges on Hie deep verandah.
the Tresideut appeared among
the roses, snipping olf superfluous buds and
blowing clouds of tobacco smoke over the
aphis-infested twigs, and in between cursing
the order for a lot of lazy loons. The roses,
he emphasised, were only half attended to.
As he listened to the President's ravings the
ghost of a smile fatted over the grizzled face
of the Welshman, and he winked solemnly
at the Hebrew.
Blowing a great cloud of smoke Rhys
beckoned the President to the seat, and when
the latter was seated the face of Rhys as-
sumed a dour, wowseristic expression.
The President looked at him with large, dog-
like eyes, and then as he refilled his pipe
he said softly :
“There’s something wrong in the State of
Denmark, eh? (Jut with it, old man!”
“You know, President, every one of us
in this place, excepting Cross, is a fugitive
from matrimony. And yet, what do we
find? Almost every night there are ladies
here, squatted round the verandahs, strum-
ming ac the piano, lifting their voices over
the whole neighborhood; and, hang me, if,
when 1 came home last night, 1 didn’t find
one of our young dogs on this very seat, with
a girl on his knee.”
“O Lord!” gasped the President, horror-
stricken. “They always play up like this
when I go working back.”
“Yes; but, listen, there’s worse still. When
Carrie was bringing in some, syphons one of
them met her in tin; hall and kissed her—■
kissed her right oil the mouth, too. Wliat
do you think of that now? Joe can bear
me out.”
The President wiped the moisture from
his big, shaggy head, and thought very hard.
“I’ll call a meeting of the Board!” he
said, with sudden decision. “I’ll call it
right now. I was going down to do some
work, but I must jump on this business
first. Come along.”
Three minutes later they were assembled
in the dining-room, the President in the
chair, Rhys with the Babe on his knee.
“Rhys, I don’t think Babe ought to hear
this. In after years she might recall some
word, some sentence; don’t you think !”
Rhys bowed acquiescence, and carrying the
infant to the door suggested she give Tow-
ser, the terrier, an early lunch. Then they,
heard the patter of little feet down the hall,
and the door dosed softly. As Rhys resumed
his seat the President rose.
“Bloques, 1 have called you together to
deal with a grave danger. I am sure we
have all been very lumpy here,” lie said,
shaking his great, shaggy head; “although
all of us, except bloque Cross, have tried
all other modes of life and found them in-
sufficient for happiness. We have drained
the wine of married life and found the dregs
bitter. This place is a refuge from the
tyranny of woman, either as a wife or hash-
tower queen. Let us keep it such.”
He was interrupted by applause; and
when the noise had subsided he resumed: —
“As you all know, I am a broad-minded
man ; but I am experienced enough to see
the danger coming. I can smell a rat a long
way off. To come to the point, there are
too many ladies calling here. Why they
come I’ll say nothing about. But lam sure
that no good will result from their visits.
If you ask ladies here, or encourage them to
come, and then, as I am credibly informed
some of you do, nurse them on the seats in
the garden, alone, they will begin to think
you are making love. The next thing, sure
as eggs, will be breaeh-of-promis.e actions,
and we’ll all be disgraced. Then again, one
of you, I don’t know which one and I don’t,
want to know, was guilty of the unpardon-
able sin of kissing our housekeeper, and right
on the mouth, too. Now I don’t want to
preach to you—but mind, it’s dangerous.
In an establishment like this it is essential
that we should avoid even the appearance
of evil. That’s all.”
Then he closed the meeting and hurried
off to the tram.
“I wonder if he had his tongue in his
cheek?” said Cross.
“Not on your life,” said Rhys. “He’s in
deadly earnest. And bv cst. David, he’s
right. He’s afraid of women because lie’s
got sense. There are some men in this
house who are not afraid of them because
they don’t know. But they will later on.”
Then Rhys went oack to the pepperwood
tree.
* * *
Usually the President stuck to one drink,
to wit, whisky. He got off the tram at King-
street and made for the club, feeling that
one would lioln to keep him up. On his way
he ran into Morvern. the leader writer, and
they entered the club together. There they
struck the rest of the bunch, with the re-
sult that the President was cajoled into
taking what he called “striped drinks,” and
mixed doses of plain whisky with gin slings
and mint juleps. The result was terrific.
He called for more drinks again and again,
and began to coruscate. Suddenly he real-
ised it was time to start back to Elysium
for lunch.
Rhys was at the head of the table carv-
ing, when little Joe. the Canaanite, looked
out through the window and suddenly ejacu-
lated that the President was “loaded.” The
startled bloques watched the President
climb over the gate, for the fun of it appar-
ently. and go out of his way to climb over
all the seats between the street and the
house. At the door he met Carrie, and seiz-
ing her by the waist and shoulder, waltzed
gravely down the hall into the dining-room,
where the Order regarded him with a dazed
and pained expression.
The President sat down and commenced
to coruscate some more, and poured oil on
the flame of his exuberance bv drinking
dinner ale. He ate his food quicklv. leaned
back and spread himself mightily, and be-
tween courses gave imitations of popular
entertainers he had heard and bosses he
had worked under. He wound up a brilliant
outbreak with a reproduction of the lecture
the Judge gave him when his wife secured
her divorce.

Just hereabouts Rhys rose and begged his
old friend not to corrupt the minds of their
younger members and weaker brothers by
discussing such questionable subjects as the
vagaries of hardened old divorce judges—
men who, though no doubt necessary, like
slaughtermen, should never be mentioned in
refined homes. In fact, he thought it always
better in a home such as theirs to avoid
the discussion of evil.
An hour later the President arrived at his
office and made the discovery that he had
come away without his key. He knocked in
the vain hope some other member of the
staff might be inside. But his knocking re-
verberated through an empty building.
What was to be done: To go back for the
key would occupy the best part of an hour
and would break up the afternoon ; besides
which the President felt that if he did not
get on to paper quickly some of the mag-
nificent ideas that were chasing one another
through his mind, they would assuredly
escape. So it was imperative that he get in
somehow. To burst the lock was impos-
sible. The ground floor windows were
covered with a huge steel shutter. He
glanced at the next floor. A ledge ran
along the line of the floor, and if he could
only reach it he could walk along and reach
one of the windows. And there was a
verandah to the next building. It was easy
if he could only get on to that verandah.
He turned round and found himself face
to face with what was (bviously a young
immigrant, one of those stalwart youths
London now sends out in such numbers.
He slipped half a crown into the youthful
hand.
“Give us a leg up that verandah post, I
want to get into that building!” he said,
with his most winning smile.
The young immigrant looked at him a
moment, and then glancing up and down
the street, which happened for the moment
to be quite empty, he gave the leg up. The
President scrambled on to the verandah
roof. Next moment he had stepped daintily
on to the ledge, opened the window and
passed through. He looked down to wave a
farewell and noticed that the immigrant
was standing on the kerb with his hands
behind his back; and his hands were opening
and shutting feverishly. It struck the Pre-
sident as a curious mannerism. Then, sud-
denly, the immigrant strolled off and a
policeman lurched past.
For two solid hours the President worked
like a Trojan. Then he lit his pipe and
took a small tot to keep him up. While he
enjoyed a few whiffs he packed some maga-
zines and a Science Quarterly belonging
to Rhys into a parcel to take home with him.
When he stepped out of the front door
with a parcel of magazines under his arm
the street was filled with the violet haze of a
Sydney evening, through which the lights
glowed goldenly. The tram-cars were bright
with the hats and frocks of women and
girls returning from Harbor excursions.
Couples were sauntering along arm in arm
loaded with ferns and bush-flowers; and
propped against the verandah-post he had
climbed some hours before was the immi-
grant.
“ ’Struth, that’s a mighty small swag,”
gasped the late helner as he hauled the
President into the more open roadw'ay clear
of the crowd. “Is it stones or splosh? Wot
a nell of a time you’ve bin —thort you’d
been pinched. Where we goin’?”
“What the devil are you talking about,
man?” said the amazed President. “You
didn’t think I was a burglar, did you?”
“Well, when a man arsks yer to put him
through th’ bloomin’ winder, and then you
stay all afternoon to watch for ther slops,
yer don’t s’pect ’im of bein’ a friend of ther
family!”
“But, you mad ass, I work there. I had
forgotten my key, and had to get in to do
some work.”
“What, they locks yer out, an’ you breaks
in to do work for ’em! Spare me dyes, what
sort of a country ’ave I come to! Oh, I sye,
dicken!”
“Dicken the devil,” said the, President
swinging himself on to a crawling tramcar,
and sitting down to find the immigrant
beside him.
“Here, what the devil do you want?”
The immigrant tapped the parcel of maga-
zines. “Yer can’t tickle me with that there
tale, mister!”
“Very well, take it —take it all, and much
good may it do you,” and he banged the
parcel on the stranger’s knees. He clutched
it, and then fell out of the tram and dis-
appeared in the crowd.
The President decided to say nothing to
the fellows at home, as it began to strike
him that the immigrant was quite justified
in making the mistake. It seemed to him
he had not only failed to avoid the appear-
ance of evil, but had embracod it with both
hands and feet. Least said the better.
* * * *
“By the way, President, what about my
Science Quarterly? Hid you bring it
home?” said Rhys suddenly as he rose from
the tea-table.
“Great Scott! Ido believe I left it in
the tram.” 1
“Oh, no matter; it’s got my flume and
address in, and it will go to' the lost pro-
perty office all right. That is unless someone
unusual picks it ur>.”
Carrie put her head inside the door and
informed Mr. Rhvs a gentleman wished to
speak to him, and privately.
Rhys left the room, and two minutes
later returned with the lost Quarterly in
his hand.
“I say, President, come here. What have
you been up to? Here’s a pretty kettle of
fish.”
The President rose. A feeling of impend-
ing disaster hit him in the middle of the
stomach. Outside, in the hall, he found an
inspector of, police, two detectives and the
immigrant.
“That’s him, gents!” said the immigrant,
excitedly. “That’s the very bloke!”
“This man says he saw you climb a vernn-
dah-nost, and walk along the ledge of the
first floor, ana break into an office through
a window. Being a stranger he can’t find
his way back to where the office is situated,
and he was too excited to notice the name
on the plate. He followed you into a tram-
car, and when you got out you left this
parcel. It contains magazines and my
Science Quarterly, and the last named
having my name in it, they thought it might
have been my office that was broken into.
But as I have no office quite im-
possible.”
The President glared helplessly at the
immigrant. If looks could have inflicted
sudden paralysis that immigrant had never
moved again. Here the whole story of his
escapade was out, and every bloke in Ely-
sium would be advising him to avoid, for
the future, the appearance of burglary.
As briefly as he could, the President ex-
plained what had happened, and how the
immigrant had helped him up the verandah-
post, and then demanded a share of the
swag; all of which that vociferous young
man luridly denied.
Next minute he found himself under arrest.
The President was requested to accompany
the party, and when his story was discovered
to be correct his accomplice was carefully
lumbered on a number of charges, and the
President allowed to return home, though
the police still seemed sceptical.
When the President arrived home he found
Rhys waiting up for him, and everybody
else out of sight. Over a whisky and soda,
Rhys explained that there had been a meet-
ing of the Board, and it had been decided
that no references were to be made to the
unfortunate occurrence.
The President filled his pockets with
glasses, and then with a bottle in one
hand and a syphon in the other he made a
pilgrimage round the rooms, and drank to
Oblivion.
C. A. JEFFRIES.
History.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I SAW a goddess high and splendid
Set on a throne in gleaming state;
Her face was curiously blended
Of light and darkness, love and hate:
For I beheld (it seemed to me)
The judgment-seat of History.
Within her eyes, in wondrous fashion,
While royally she sat on high,
Shone every sin and every passion,
And every truth and every lie:
And round her clustered row on row
Of men who came from earth below.
Ihere saw I Christ, with hands all bleeding,
And wearing yet His crown of shame,
And near Him, priests and bishops pleading
That she might know them in his name;
But well I knew that most of these
In vain would clamor at her knees.
Pell-mell towards her throne they scrambled,
A crew in motley robes and gowns,
And many a group of kinglets gambolled
Like puppets in their tinsel crowns ;
Mid such, at times, there grimly shone
A Cromwell or Napoleon.
Some scrabbled at her feet to hold them,
Some tried to seize her gleaming zone,
And some crawled sneakily to fold them
In the hung trappings of the throne.
A few walked forward, brave and wise,
To challenge her with quiet eyes.
And there were murderers and prelates,
And women with lascivious hair,
And meek-eyed saints and cursing zealots
All fighting for an inch to spare,
One little inch of standing-room
To save them from eternal doom.
For when she waved her hand toward them,
No matter how they clawed and clung
Not all their silly strength could guard them,
But off the dais they were flung ;
And then I saw not their distress,
For all the rest was nothingness.
Two men came hurrying together,
And twain were they whom well I knew,
Though on my life, I know not whether
Their names were ever heard by you ;
For though they make whole cities ring
This Fame is such a little thing.
“Great Goddess !” cried the first, and mellow
His voice rose through that conclave high,
“I am a most distinguished fellow
To be your comrade when I die:
I am Top Dog, the Nation’s Best,
Right Honorable, and the rest.
“For fifty years I schemed and plotted.
For fifty years I climbed and crawled,
Ten thousand speeches I have swotted,
> By twenty titles I am called ;
I’ve spun those tangled things named laws
Amid a party’s wild applause.
“I’ve been the best and loudest pleader
In many a mixed and wordy fight;
I’ve waked an Opposition Leader.
And been Prime Minister at night;
I’ve flummoxed the opposing camp
By changes in a postage-stamp.”
The goddess looked —her eyes were tender —
, And answered gently with a smile,
O Man of Men ! You rose to splendor,
Still must you wait a little while.
Sit by my footstool for a spell
Until your friend his tale shall tell.”
Half-truculently spoke the second :
“Maybe I’m even more a joke,
For I suppose I must be reckoned
As just an ordinary bloke :
I fought a fight with circumstance
And hit and kicked at every chance.
“I made men strike when they were trod on,
I made ’em vote when they were slack;
And when his Nibs here laid the rod on,
I showed ’em how to hit him back:
My mates were all that mates could be,
I stuck to them, and they to me.”
The goddess rose and said : “These matters
Quite long enough have I enjoyed:
The, funny little man who chatters
Must go his way towards the void;
He is the million-millionth freak
Of that same sort I’ve seen this week.
“Bloke, as for you”—her eyes grew merry—
“Your.sentence from the start was known •
Down on your hands and knees, and bury
Your shoulders underneath the throne - !
You’ve got to join the other chaps
Who help to keep Us from collapse.”
So, I beheld the Gabbler tumble
Into a darkness vast and black.
And heard the Bloke, with one dull grumble,
Under the chair-leg thrust his back.
The whole proud platform seemed to jump..
The Bloke? I only saw his rump.
N.S.W.
ADAM McCAY.
